acknowledgment will be made.

THE RED PAGE .
Behold! in those lines you have the lovely gliding which
only poetry in perfection and crystal waters knows.
Daley’s atmosphere is one of dream, his theme 'love.
Autumnal sentiment and gentle regret shade his work
with a beautiful sadness.
His work affects the mind like slanted sunshine after
rain, when all the roses weep. There is much of Swin-
burne’s intense colour in many of his verses, but little of
that poet’s tropic passion. Love with Daley is a delicate
sentiment, a something remote and pensive, a marble
statue touched and softened by the quiet glory
that reaches it through painted windows. There is
nothing coarse or fleshly or redly sensual in him. His
message is not to the animal, but to the soul; not to the
hot heart of the Present, choked with thick desire and
all in flame with passion, but to the slow heart of the
Past, where glimmers a faint love whitened over by
ashes. . .
My dream flies after him, and I
Am in another land ;
The sun sets in another sky,
And we sit hand in hand.
Grey eyes look into mine; such eyes
X think the angels’ are:
Soft a 3 the soft light in the skies
When shines the morning star;
And tremulous as mom, when thin
Gold lights begin to glow,
Revealing the bright soul within
As dawn the sun below.
He can be simple as milk, too, and sing heart-songs with
the best of them. “The Old Wife and the New” is a
tender ballad, sad with youth’s decay and love's decease,
and memories of surrendered power. . .
He sat beneath the curling vines
That round the gay verandah twined,
His forehead seamed with sorrow’s lines,
An old man with a weary mind.
His young wife, with a rosy face,
And brown arm 3, ambered by the sun,
Went flitting all about the place—
Master and mistress, both in one.
The old man bent his weary head ;
The sunlight on his grey hair shone •
His thoughts were with a woman dead
And buried, years and years agone.
The quiet loveliness of lines like these gravely insists on a
place in our memory. “The Old Wife and the New”
has some slight local colour :
Where once were gum-trees gaunt and stark
And blood-woods waved green-feathered foam.
But in other places Daley is not so happy in the use of it.
Although his sunset gallery i 3 purely Australian, and
perfect as may be, he is not successful when dealing with
the human side of our new life. His mind was cast in an
ancient mould, and the Now and Here are too fresh and
crude to inspire his best. The Romantic and the Uni-
versal were appointed as his domain, and he robs his gift
of voice when he descends to the Real and the Near.
Am I too venturesome in believing that the poet is a
present embodiment of all the experience that greeted
his forbears through all time ? Perhaps I am, but some-
times I fancy that Daley is a descendant of inland
dwellers who lived in a green calm, flowered about by
many a fairy tradition, breathing mild airs, and seldom
hearing the storm that shouted on a far' coast. This,
perhaps, is the reason why he is a lover of Nature in her
tranquil moods—river reaches, smooth as green glass—
mirrored trees, and seas enchanted by sunset. He lacks
the storm-note. To stricken seas and tortured forests he
is deaf ; and still he has an ear for the eerie and the
strange, the hiss of yeast on driven waves, and the sound
of voices wailing death. . .
Night winds soughing through grave-yard trees,
And wild winds ploughing the wild, salt seas—
These are my lovers, and only these. . .
In passing, it is a duty to remark that the omission from
At Dawn and Dusk of the poem to which these lines
belong is a deadly sin.
Daley’s songs must reside in our memory by the double
title of their sweet music and the pure insistence of their
imagery. As the child, of ancient Ireland he is heir to
her wine-dark legends. The fantastic beauty of twilight
times, when that country was a theatre wherein hero-
shapes moved as in a cloud, has descended through the
dark to him, and gilds his soul as a dusty sunbeam finds
a gem and is made manifest by its discovery. It does not
need deep analysis to arrive at a conclusion as to the
effect of At Dawn and Dusk upon Australian verse.
Daley has shown us how to sing in faultless tone, and
though our voices may crack in the attempt to follow
him, the literature of our country will be the richer
because of the effort. To oonclude, Daley is not a great
original poet; the day of the great Originals seems
done, and it is hardly likely that a school will be founded
upon him. He does not desire it. As a preacher of the
Beautiful he has brought loveliness into many a mind,
and his songs are so delicious that it seems deeply un-
familiar to find oneself writing of him in the present
tense. He is great enough to belong to the Past; and it
is surely high time that he quitted the shadow of the
sail, wherein he so long dwelt, to stand at last in the sun-
set glow.
* -
One does not understand the final expression
of opinion that Daley would be better out of the
way; or, rather, ono can only understand it by
light of that anecdote printed in a Melbourne
paper (denied). The Villains alleged that,
when poet Ogilvie left Australia, Quinn met
Daley at a prayer-meeting (denied) ; and
quoth Quinn (denied), “Well, Victor, there’s
only me and you for it now ” (denied).
’Tis a pity the anecdote is (denied), for
the idea that Australia has only a certain amount
of poetic credit for sharing, and that it is time
Daley stopped sharing, has many interesting
facets.
* * * * *
Daley, alas ! has been in very bad health lately:
he is getting on in years, and has tasted to the full
(with admixtures)
. . . the lot austere
That waits upon the man of letters here
and his friends are just now combining in an effort
to send him on a sea-voyage, and bring him back
with (not a sunset, but) a sunrise glow. There is
to be an entertainment, with musical and pictorial
trimmings; and many admirers of the poet have
come forward with financial help. Those who
care enough and can spare a little may, if they
choose, add to the fund at this office.
■
And, speak of the poet .. . ! As we are
writing, comes to hand a collection of “ Songs for
the Buccleuch Hunt ” made by Will Ogilvie—the
wanderer who, returned to his Scottish home,
continues to ride gray horses (with a pedigree) in
chase of fair girls (with a brogue). And foxes in-
cidentally. Ogilvie’s rhymes are really spirited :
in fact, they .challenge comparison with Whyte-
Melville, and fail only by a pulse-beat or sd. But
Ogilvie is a light-weight: Whyte-Melville surely
could not have ridden less than eleven stone. A
two-or-three for tasting—
And the gallop and the kill—
Oh ! it’s home t
Hacking home, where the mists are spread like foam,
When the world will soon be snoring, and the dew‘s a
silver flooring
For the dance of elf and gnome,
It’s a weird ride, hacking home !
But you ’re picking up the landmarks, and in half-an-hour
or less,
You are crunching over gravel where the fir-boughs curl
and comb,
And, for all so keen a rider, you are willing to confess
That you *re glad to be at home !
Landed home !
Safe at home, and mud-bespattered, splashed with foam 1
And the old horse slips on gravel and you lift him on the
snaffle,
And across the starry dome
Loom the gable roofs of home !
YOUNG DEMOCRACY
Hark! Young Democracy from sleep
Our careless sentries raps ;
A backwash from the Future’s deep
Our cliffs of Evil saps.
Unknown, these Titans of our Night
Their New Creation make :
Unseen, they toil and love and fight
That hocussed Man may wake.
Knights-errant of the human race,
The Quixotes of to-day,
For man as man they claim a place,
Prepare the tedious way.
They seek no dim-eyed mob’s applause,
Deem base the titled name,
And jilt, for glory of their Cause,
The tawdry nymphs of Fame.
No masks of ignorance or sin
Hide from them you or me:
We ’re Man, no colour shames our skin,
No race or cast have we.
The prognathous Neanderthal, •
To them, conceals the Bruce ;
They see Dan ffSsop in the Thrall |
From swagmen Christ deduce.
Tho’ butt for lecher’s ribaldry
And scarred by woman’s scorn,
In baby-burdened girl they see
God-motherhood, forlorn.
With them to racial siredom glides
The savage we’d deprave ;
That eunuch brilliant Narses hides,
A Spartacus, that slave.
They Ohristhood find in manger waif;
In horse-boys Shakespearhood :
And earthquake-Luthers nestling safe
In German miner’s brood.
The God that pulses everywhere
They know fill! Satan’s veins ;
No felon but they see Him there
Behind His mirror’s stains.
•Tis theirs Earth’s charnel rooms to clear.
And ruthless sweep away
The Lares and Penates dear
To man in his decay.
Their restless energy collects
Devices to disturb
The keeps whence feudal architects
Our free banditti curb.
Their unrelenting wars they wage,
These Furies of the Right,
Where myriad Falsehood’s legions rage,
Artilleried by Might;
Where Fashion’s stupid iron clamps
Young Innovation’s head;
And Law the robust Present cramps
In Past’s Procrustes-bed ;
Where Pride of learning, substance, blood.
Or prowess in the strife,
, Exacts from teeming lowlihood
The lion’s share of life ;
Where Gluttony would to the brutes
Degrade his loose-lipped gangs ;
Where Tyranny his venom shoots
From one or million fang :
Where Cruelty, in Wisdom’s mask,
Piths fame from agonies ;
Where blessed is racial Murder’s task
By priests of Gods of Peace.
In Punic or in Persian fray
With Love’s and Conscience’ foes,
Unadvertising Romans they,
And Spartans free from pose.
Abused as mad or traitors by
The harpies they’d eject;
Cold-shouldered by wan Apathy;
Ofsmotives mean suspect;
Out cast from social jollities ;
Denied life’s lilied grace ;
They mount their hidden Calvaries
To save the human race.
Some sweat within the factory.
Some languish in “ high ” place,—
Some serve the “ low,” and some the “ high,"
But most are in disgrace.
But whether in the mob or school.
In church or poverty,
They teach and live the Golden Rule
Of Young Democracy : —
“ To each whatc’er all can bestow —
That colour, caste's a lie —
That Man is God, however low —
Is man , however high. ”
v.
DANTON.
Roderic Quinn lauds Victor Daley’s verse—
The poets of the past live in the language of to-day.
It is their best habitation. So it follows that their songs
are sometimes sung twice over. There is nothing new
under the sun, and that is why the present-day singer
must content himself with saying old things newly.
However much he tries to be original, some hand from
©ut the past is always at his elbow, guiding his fingers as
they write. . Then he is charged with being reminiscent,
a pigmy striding in the footprints of a giant. All the
poet-gold was minted long ago, yet the poet-impulse to
pour golden coin upon the world survives, and the
only thing to do is to melt the money down, each in his
own crucible, and re-mint it. The Masters wrought
so well, however, that no matter how the imprint be
changed Georgius Rex will often show himself through
Victoria Regina. All this has very little to do with
Daley; he would scorn to borrow from any
man ; but here and there in At Dawn and Dtisk we
catch a glimpse of Rossetti. Sometimes, when I am
reading Daley, I remember Rossetti. In part, the
reminiscence exists through the kinship of pure beauty,
a gem recalling a gem—a star suggesting a star. For the
rest, he is like Rossetti because he is a true artist, con-
trolling his genius with the silken reins of Art, and never
permitting it to take head and get away with him. He
never soars out of sight or dives too deeply. The artist
governs the poet, and the poem must please them both,
else Daley himself will not be satisfied. Under this keen
censorship, every word is judiciously said, every image
happily set, and the whole meaning clear as in a mirror.
In At Dawn and Dusk all the figures are flooded with
tender light, melancholy perhaps, yet clear-cut as though
they were done in ivory. We find no Rembrandt faces
there, half shadow and half light, and this because Daley
is a poet of Statement rather than of Suggestion.
The faculty that enables him to control his inspiration
dowers him in another way. He does not soar too high,
but he soars always. Or to say it in different wise : his
path is that of sustained beauty winding always
among roses, and never deviating into a barren
waste of weeds. Perhaps his richest canvas is
the “ Two Sunsets,” and there he pursues the
painted tenor of .his way, evenly, but never monoton-
ously—gliding on from line to line and colour to colour
with delicious grace and invisible blending. . .
All things beneath the still sky seem
Bound by the spell of a sweet dream ;
In the dusk forest, dreamingly,
Droops slowly down each plumfed head;
The river flowing softly by
Dreams of the sea ; the quiet sea
Dreams of the unseen stars : and I
Am dreaming of the dreamless dead.
TO HORSE AND AWAY!
When sorrows come sobbing
To clutch at the breast,
When trouble comes robbing
The heart of its rest,
When cash-columns addle
The brain as they may :
Swing into the saddle,
To horse and away 1
To horse and away
To the heart of the fray !
Fling care to the Devil for one merry day 1
When tradesmen importurie,
When duns make advance,
When fickle Dame Fortune
Is frowning askance,
When Love’s looking lonely
And Life’s looking gray,
There’s one solace only—
To horse and away 1
To horse and away,
With spurs in the bay !
Ming Care to perdition for one merry day !
Behind each gay rider
Sits Care, we are told,
With dark hair to hide her
And clinging hands cold ;
If swift we be flying
Oare cannot but sway
And fall ! Leave her lying 1
To horse and away !
To horse and away !
Where she fell let her stay !
Hark for’ard ! And leave her—Oare dead for a day !
GET AWAY ON!
A stir at the end of the cover,
A hat in a hand for a sign,
And music to tell to the rover
Shore’s* beauties are out on his line 1
See ! from the corner a dog-fox has gone!
Get away on to him ! Get away on!
A rattle, a rush for your places,
A struggle to get to the gate !
Then show them the best of your paces
And toss for a panel with Fate !
Give him his head at the beat of your heart!
Half of the triumph is won at the start!
Oh ! hark to the rap and the rattle,
The thud of the rails going down !
And right in the front of the battle
A crash, and a riderless brown !
He will be cornered and mounted anon ;
Get away on to him ! Get away on 1
They ’re Streaming away in the stubble,
They ’re racing like birds on the grass,
The shirker’s already in trouble
And letting the better men pass !
Your heart is a-throb at the tug of the reins
And the music sets spinning the blood in your veins!
Since never a day is too long, man,
And never a gallop too fast, .
Let’s jump while we ’re still going strong, man,
And pace it as long as we last!
Soon from the field we ’ll be gathered and gone—
Get away on to him ! Get away on 1
HACKING HOME!
When they’ve broken up a good one after “ something
like a run,”
Or they've dropped him in the turnips, or run past him
in the fern ;
When a golden glow to westward gives a hint of setting
sun.
And it’s time for you to turn—
Then it’s home !
Hacking home, when the mists are on the loam !
When the night is gathering dreary, and the old horse,
game but weary,
Tosses back the flecks of foam,
It’s a long ride hacking home!
When the mud of mid-November gathers spotted on the
girth,
When the little roadway puddles lie a-gleaming to the
stars,
When the gusty winds are whirling all the dead leaves of
the earth
Round your chilly stirrup bars—
Oh ! it’s home !
Hacking home, when the mists are on the loam !
When the icy dews are falling, and the lonely night-birds
calling,
And the gliding owls a-roam,
It’s a long ride hacking home !
When you pass the dim farm-houses with their lights that
mark the miles,
When you pass the shepherd’s cottage with the candle on
the sill,
With your thoughts upon a hill-fox and his cunning Border
wiles,

RHEUMATISM

BRIGHT’S DISEASE
For the full understanding of the curative action
of Warner’s Safe Cure, it is necessary to bear in
mind the processes of nature by which human life
is maintained. Reduced to their essential prin-
ciples, these processes are simply the wasting and
renewal of the tissues of the body.
It is absolutely necessary to life that the body
should waste away, and that its substance should
be renewed by food, water, and fresh air. Night
and day this process of waste and replaceman goes
on unceasingly. Every time a man draws breath
his body gains in weight by the oxygen, etc., taken
from the air, and every time he breathes out it
loses weight by the carbonic acid and moisture
given forth.
Insensibly, too, the body is losing weight all the
time. You can prove this by placing your hand
on a cold piece of polished glass, when you will
see that moisture given forth by the skin has con-
densed. Were the body to be entirely covered
with a coat of waterproof varnish, death would
quickly result.
Apart from the skin and lungs, the organs em-
ployed in this process of waste are the kidneys and
liver, and the maintenance of those organs in
thorough working order is the grand secret of good
health .
The Kidneys. —The function which the kidneys
perform is to filter the blood and otherwise
separate from it the surplus water and the various
impurities which it contains. These impurities
are urea, uric acid, and mineral matter, which,
with water, constitute the urine.
When the kidneys have done their work, the
purified blood is delivered to the veins, and is
returned to the heart, having in its course
traversed the body and become laden with a fresh
supply of waste matter. The heart again delivers
the blood to the kidneys, which once more filter
it and extract the impurities. This process is
continuous.
The Liveh.— The part the liver plays in the
process of waste is chiefly the manufacturing from
the blood the liquid called “ Bile,” which is
delivered by the liver into the intestines, and acts
as a natural cathartic.
The liver and kidneys are very sensitive organs,
fiudden chills, fever, alcoholic intemperance, and
tropical heat are all liable to congest the liver and
reduce it to an abnormal condition. The kidneys
are exceedingly susceptible to inflammation. An
ordinary feverish cold, which closes the pores and
hinders the skin in performing its share in the
removal of the waste products of the body, throws
a burden of extra work upon the kidneys which
they may be unable to perform. Influenza, or
any kind of fever, taxes the kidneys unduly, and
may cause them to break down under the strain.
Any inflammation of the kidneys is very apt to
affect the liver, and vice versa.
Should the liver or kidneys, or both, become
unable to fulfil their purpose thoroughly, then the
blood becomes laden with urinary and biliary
poisons, which are carried to every part of the
body.
Sometimes these poisons become deposited in
the joints or tissues, and cause Rheumatism,
Lumbago, Sciatica, or Gout. In other cases the
blood, being contaminated, is in an unfit condition
to absorb nutriment, which causes Anaemia,
Neuralgia, digestive troubles, and Debility. In
other cases the supply of bile to the intestines
being inadequate, Constipation, with its attendant
evils result; in others, Biliousness. In others
again the impurity of the blood develops boils and
skin eruptions. Sometimes the uric acid is
deposited in the kidneys and produces Stone,
Gravel, and urinary and bladder troubles.
The reason, then, that Warner’s Safe Cure cures
so many apparently diverse disorders is that they
all proceed from one cause, namely—the inability
of the kidneys or liver to do their work properly,
and so rid the blood of these poisons in the
manner provided by nature.
Warner’s Safe Cure has complete mastery- over
functional and chronic liver and kidney derange-
ments. and by restoring those vital organs to
health and activity, does away with the cause of
these several complaints. When the kidneys and
liver are efficiently carrying out the wonderful
scheme of work allotted to them by nature, none
of the disorders here enumerated can exist.
A pamphlet containing fuller particulars
relating to Warner’s Safe Cure and the diseases it
cures will be sent post free to any address on
application to H. H. Warner and Co., Limited,
Australasian Branch, 528 to 532 Little Lonsdale
Street, Melbourne, Victoria.


An engineering correspondent to The
Bulletin :
“A novel valve-gear, invented by J. T. Marshall, of
Leeds, Eng., and which can be fitted at a small cost on
to any engine, is being adopted, after exhaustive experi-
ment, bv Great Northern Bailwav, Eng. Two ancient;
locomotives, built in ’B2 by Dubs, Glasgow, were used ini
trials. The boilers were very small, of less than 1000 ft..
heating surface, with 17|in. by 26in. cylinders and six;
driving-wheels, of sft. dia., total weight being 70 tons.
No. 1 engine was fitted with Marshall’s valve-gear, while’
No. 2 was unaltered. No. 1 hauled up an incline of 1 in,
7li a load of 307 tons, or over 50 per cent, more than,
similar engine (No. 2) with, old valve. With identical,
loads, coal consumption of No. 1 was 46 - 21 b. per mile
against No. 2’s 57'71b. With a 60 per cent, heavier load,
No. l’s consumption was 031bs. against No. 2’s 071bs. The
valve-gear keeps absolutely cool with a marked absence
of shock, and the blast is peculiarly long-drawn and soft,
and seems to have solved the spark-throwing problem so
disastrous to bush and crops in vicinity of railways. Old
engines of any sort fitted with this valve can be made to
do 60 per cent, more work at a much lower working-
pressure, and with less fuel. This invention should be a
great boon not only to railway's and. ocean steamers, but
also to mining.”

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Manuka.
My lady asked for a flower to wear, .
To dwell like a kiss in her dear brown hair;
So, questing wide for fragrance and hue,
I wandered my island-garden through,
And back to my sweetheart-love I went
With treasure of blossom-trove opulent.
I had climbed afar to those slopes forlorn
Where the glaciers grim and grey ax e born ;
And from its chalices green I chose
Our snow-white lily of the snows.
The Alps of their diadem I despoiled
To grace my dear with its grace unsoiled.
I had gathered for her the wealth untold
Of the spendthrift kowhai’s showering gold,
When the sombre bush in the wet Spring days
Is lit with its drooping boughs ablaze,
And it seems, by the gold on the hill-side strewn,
As if Autumn, enchanted, had come too soon.
I had brought the clematis, trailing far
Its constellation, star by star ;
As, with slender arms wound amorously,
It nestled high in a stalwart tree,
In its silver radiance shimmering
Like the crown on the brow of an ancient king !
The honeysuckle that over me flashed,
Red on the rewa, like blood-drops splashed,
The spear that the koromiko bore,
The shy black blossom the mapau wore,
The ribbon-wood with its foam of white —
These, and more, for my love’s delight.
I had broken for her a spire of bloom
The cabbage-tree’s feathery silver plume—
Whose giant bouquet drooped as if spent,
Sick with its burden of heavy scent,
While the tussock plain and the raupo-swamp
Stood panoplied in its splendid pomp.
And, then, to crown my love’s desire
I had gathered a flame of rata-fire,
Where the ancient trees, like monarchs. dread,
Draw over their shoulders their robes of red,
And, the bush with their glory smouldering—
They stand in their crimson, king by king !
But my lady pouting, dissatisfied,
My odorous opulence tossed aside,
With, “ This is too splendid, and this too rare,
For a modest Maoriland maid to wear ;
The only blossom that mine I would call
Is a blossom common and dear to all! ”
And she took from where it had lain unseen,
Smothered in color, a sprig of green,
Strewn thick with stars, till gem by gem,
It glowed like a delicate diadem,
Whose fragrance, pungent, sweet, intense,
Was myrrh and aloes and frankincense.
“ Stalwart and hardy the manuka stands,
Fine and sweet over all our lands,
And common and lowly it flourishes wide
And waits for my coming at each road-side ;
And the swift, clean perfume that it imparts
Is dear as the heather’s to exiled hearts !
“ In its sombre armies it crowds the hills
Till Summer upon it a splendor spills,
And each tiny quiet, green, leaf is lost
Beneath a ripple of silver frost,
And faintly white do the gullies show
With a dainty fall of its blossom snow ! ”
And my lady lifted the blossom-spray,
Sweet and white in its bride’s array,
And she breathed, “ It is neither rich nor grand,
But it is the blossom for Maoriland ;
The Manuka is my own land’s wear,”
And the Manuka gemmed her dear brown hair.
M.L.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
The Garden of My Heart.
Beyond the purple portals of the Western summer
sky—
Where sun-flushed cloudlets float in happy sleep,
Like showers of curling petals from a rose which,
fain to die,
Sheds the casket of her soul in perfumed heap—
Lies a garden of delight,
Where a sighing summer night
Sings a madrigal of longing in a low, delicious
croon,
Till a heart awakes and quickens in the lonely,
silent moon.
Beneath the lamp of Venus, where the love-light
hovers low,
Waits a throne of regal lilies for a queen,
While a surgeless river murmurs with a lang’rous
ebb and flow
As it wends its way, a thousand stars between.
And it plays upon my heart,
With its soft seductive art,
Like the cunning master-fingers of the tender
Lord of Love
On a harp of hazy harmonies, whose strings were
tuned by Jove.
Thro’ avenues of blossom, where the brooding
shades of night
Scarce seemed to breathe, and thrall’d the dewy
flowers,
Came footsteps softly stealing, while an iridescent
light
Lit the great magnolia leaves in all the bowers;
And the stately shimmering beds
Of the lilies bowed their heads:
Every tree bent down to kiss her as she passed
them on her way,
And the air strove to enfold her, and the breezes
whispered “ Stay ! ”
Thus in the spell-bound garden groves, a pulse
began to beat
That woke th’ enchanted poppies from their
dreams,
And thrilled the waiting lilies, as I led her to the
seat
That had blazed alight with flashing firefly
gleams:
Eor I wrought this fairy throne
Just for “ Her I love ” alone,
Where she reigns with sway despotic, but herself
may not depart
From her kingdom —for she’s captive in the
garden of my heart.
B.A.
ALAN.

SATURDAY. APRIL 12, 1902.
The Decay of Agriculture.
The year just closed showed a large falling-
off in the cultivated area of both New South
Wales and Victoria, and probably, when the
returns are all in, something of the same
kind will be found to have happened in some
other States also. This unlooked-for episode
has given the community something of a
shock. Agriculture is the “great natural
industry ” par excellence. The man on the
land is the country’s pride, its bone and
sinew, its principal defence, its Boer-
shooter, its main source of increase—for the
habit of being born has a tendency to die
out in cities —and the source of most of its
political flapdoodle. The invitation to go
on the land has been in the mouths of Aus-
tralian politicians ever since there were any.
The pride and boast of every Ministry has
been its fixed resolve to settle the people on
the land. The pastoral industry has crum-
bled miserably through drought, rabbits,
ticks, low prices, Argentine competition,
and other troubles. The gold industry is a
fading quantity over Eastern and Central
Australia. And if agriculture begins to fade
also the situation will be interesting, if not
disastrous.
• * * *
In Sydney and Melbourne alike, the Re-
spectable Press has arisen to explain the
situation. InN.S.W., it seems, O’Sullivan’s
public works minimum wage (paid out of
loan moneys) of ss. 6d. to 7s. a day accord-
ing to the brawn of the worker, is the
cause of the trouble. This may be the case
to some extent, but, even if it is, it doesn’t
make the position much better. Public
works, even if there were six O’Sullivans
to scatter jobs around instead of one, are a
precarious and shifting resource, and if the
persons who offer this explanation tried
heaving the mighty pickaxe in a stifling rail-
way cutting for from 335. to 425. a week
they would probably decide that going on
the land must be a fearsome undertaking if
such a wage for such work could tempt any
man off it again. Melbourne Age says, in a
general way, that the trouble arises in its
State through want of encouragement on the
part of the Ministry, theneglect and incapacity
of bell-.toppered persons in Govt, offices,
and the want of something vague and un-
specified in the head of the Minister for
Lands, or the Minister for Agriculture, or
some other Minister. This is a shadowy ex-
planation, and though the pitiable muddle
and backwardness of the "Vic. Lands Depart-
ment, which doesn’t know what land it has
to sell, and takes 18 months or five years to
get a simple application through, and the
painful dryness of an Irrigation Department
which never irrigates anything nowadays,
account for a great deal, they don’t explain
everything—not by a long way.
* * * *
Some, at least, of the causes are of a kind
that the Respectable Press can’t be expected
to mention. The Bulletin thinks it recog-
nises these eight :
(1) The export of a considerable section of the agricul-
tural population to South Africa to murder another agri-
cultural race which never did us any injury.
(2) The barrenness and drought-stricken character of
a great portion of the agricultural country, which makes
it necessary to plough up two acres in order to get as
much grain as the American gets off one, or four acres to
get as much grain as the Englishman gets off one.
(3) Want of irrigation to counteract this difficulty. (It
is difficult to borrow the money to irrigate with, and the
irrigation works would probably not pay interest on the
money if it was borrowed, and it is against all Aus-
tralian rule, precedent, and principle to do any public
work without borrowing the money.)
(4) High railway freights to the consuming centres.
Owing to the fact that the railways were built with loan
moneys, instead of out of the proceeds of land sales,
freights are twice as high as they would be if there were
no interest to pay. These high freights gnaw away an
unreasonable proportion of what little income the agri-
culturist makes out of his barren acres.
(5) The want of a large manufacturing population to
consume the agriculturist’s products. Already there is
very often a surplus for export to Western Europe—prac-
tically the only outlet. And the Australian farmer, who
has to plough and sow and reap about twice as much
ground as the American or Russian to get the same
quantity of wheat, and then to pay about twice as much
freight to get it to Europe, can’t make enough out of
growing grain for export to live as a while man should.
That is taking the farmer on an average. There are
isolated spots where things are otherwise.
(6) The unconscionable number of middlemen who bite
at the farmer’s scanty profits before his produce reaches
the market.
(7) The Cheap Loaf Superstition. It is understood
that boots, hats, clothes and other things should sell at a
price which will allow of the workman getting a decent
wage for a day’s work, but somehow this idea is never ex-
tended to agriculture.
(8) Last, and worst of all, the growing Landlord Class.
The squire is becoming acclimatised in Australia and
threatens to be the same curse here that he is in Eng-
land, Hungary, Portugal, and elsewhere. The land
syndicate and the private landlord, who don’t cultivate
their own acres but sublet them to rack-rented tenants,
are spread over a very small section of Australia counted
by mere acres, but they hold an enormous proportion of
the good part of Australia. And in many places where
agriculture, even for the sake of exporting the produce
to the other end of the world, would pay handsomely if
there were no exorbitant landlord to support, it does not
pay while that elevated loafer and frequent absentee is
living on the game. The Clarke curse and the Berry
curse and the A.A. co. curse and the V.D.L. co. curse and
the Peel River co. curse have all to be reckoned with.
* * . * *
The landlord trouble is not confined to
N.S.W. and Victoria, in South Australia,
a few years ago, it was announced that, out
of a little over 16,000,000 acres in private
hands, only 5,500,000 were occupied by the
owners, while 10,500,000 were occupied by
people who paid rent to the personal or joint-
stock squire. But in S.A., where the evil
reached its worst, it has decreased somewhat
by reason of the vigorous legislation of the
Kingston Government. In N.S.W. and
Victoria it seems to be increasing. In 12
coastal counties of N.S.W.—the richest part
of the State—nearly one-third of the land is
now held under private rental, and the area
has increased largely since ’95. This is the
district in which iand has been most closely
subdivided, but subdivision in itself is not
a cure for landlordism. The loafer who
sub-lets 500 or 1000 acres * is quite as
grasping a rack-renter as the loafer who
sub-lets 10,000 or 100,000 acres'. The policy
of breaking up the big estates is a good one,
but something more is required. The
whole landlord class is objectionable. In a
very large proportion of cases the landlord
is a non-producer, and the non-producer in
any shape is a burden on the community.
The agent who collects the rents and draws
up the leases, and the agents’ clerk, dun
and bum-bailiff are also non-producers
created by landlordism. The landlord,
especially the large specimen of the breed,
has an incurable tendency to become an
absentee, and when a man, or a group of
men, receiving, say, £30,000 a year in Aus-
tralian rents, settles permanently abroad, it
is exactly the same as if Australia borrowed
£1,000,000 at 3 per cent, and then dropped
the money overboard in the deepest part of
the Pacific. In each case there is £30,000
a year going out and nothing coming in to
balance the account.
■* * * *
The objects of democratic legislation
should be : (1) To break up large estates ;
(2) to extinguish the absentee landlord,
whether he be large or small (incidentally
the absentee money-lender and mortgagee
are equally in need of extinction) ; (3) to
extinguish the agricultural and pastoral
landlord, whether absentee or otherwise. In
other words, it should aim at a condition of
things under which the man who cultivates
the soil and the man who owns the soil are the
same man—except when the cultivator is a
tenant of the State. The N.S.W. law,
which taxes alike the absentee landlord who
spends his rents abroad and the local one
who spends his profits in the country, which
makes no distinction between the man with
1000 acres and the one with 100,000,
which treats the loafing, rack-renting squire
or syndicate as well as it does the person
who cultivates his own acres, is wholly
undemocratic. It seems rather to have
encouraged than retarded the formation of
big estates, judging by the following figures,
taken from Coghlan’s Statistical Regis-
ter, and dealing with the thickly-settled
coastal districts exclusively :
In Holdings of 10,001 Acres and Upwards.
Counties. 1896 1901
(acres). (acres).
Rous, Richmond, Clarence, Fitzroy,
Raleigh, Dudley and Macquarie.. 202,074 218,849
Gloucester, Durham and Northum-
berland 512,438 619,975
Cumberland, Camden, St. Vincent,
Dampier and Auckland .. .. 80,430 106,755
This takes in the whole coastal district—the
most thickly-peopled district and the one
where the tendency to subdivision is greatest.
And even in this favored and fertile area the
big estates are increasing. And, simultane-
ously, the tenant-farmer, as distinguished
from the cultivator who ploughs his own
holding, is increasing even more quickly.
• * • •
The Victorian land-tax, though it bears
more directly on large estates than does that
of N.S.W., also does nothing to discourage
the curse of the absentee and the syndicate,
and the lazy, rack-renting squirearchy. And
even the South Australian law—the one
democratic statute of the lot—though it
falls (somewhat heavily) on the big land-
grabber, and falls (not a quarter so heavily
as it should) on the absentee, makes no
special effort to discourage the parasite who
sits around and collects rents as distinguished
from the man who really works on the soil.
The Bulletin’s theory of what a land-tax
ought to be, or of something like what it
ought to be, is as follows :
(1) Every agricultural and pastoral estate to be free of
taxation up to a value of £6OO, provided the owner
resides on it and personally puts it to good use. All
value above £5OO to be taxed—say Jd. in the £ up to
£lOOO ; Id. in the £ on all values above £lOOO and up to
£l5OO ; ljd. in the £ on all values above £l5OO up to
£5OOO ; 2d. in the £ from £5OOO to £lO,OOO ; 2£d. in the £
from £lO,OOO to £25,000 ; 3d. in the £ from £25,000 to
£50,000; 4d. in the £ beyond that.
(2) Where the land is not occupied and put to good use
the £6OO exemption to be abolished.
(3) Where the land is owned by an absentee, defined as
a person who spends more than six months in any one
year out of the Commonwealth, the rate to be increased
one-half during the year or years of absenteeism. A
company or syndicate to be deemed an absentee if its
head office is outside the Commonwealth, or if more than
half its share or debenture capital is held by absentees.
(The work of defining whether a company is or is not an
absentee would supply some profitable employment for
the lawyers.) When a local co. is turned into an absentee
co. through the departure of certain stockholders, the
co. to have power to charge them with the extra tax to
which their default renders it liable.
(4) Last, but not least, a tax of 25 per cent, on all rents
derived from agricultural or pastoral property, for the
express discouragement of the landlord-class as dis-
tinguished from the class of resident owners and culti-
vators.
* * • »
There is no reasonable present hope that
any of the State Parliaments will take up
this policy, but there is a chance of the
Federal Legislature doing so when the
tariff and other things are out of the way,
and the bumptious Sydney Smith and his
fellow obstructionists have ceased from
troubling. There is a great work to be done
in the matter of getting rid of the absentee,
the land-syndicate and land company, the
rack-renter, the incipient squirearchy, and
the whole tribe of persons who own land in
the small fertile area of Australia, and put it
to no good use, and only serve to block
settlement, while the genuine farmer is
driven into the Central or Western districts to
degenerate into semi-barbarism while hope-
lessly arguing the point with Nature. There
is room in the comparatively fertile Eastern
Division of N.S.W. for all the agriculturists
of N.S.W.—for all of them twice over.
When that is filled up there will be a large
population from which to raise the money
for irrigating the dry and horrible interior,
and the work may then be really possible.
But the country can’t be effectively opened
up on the landlord-and-tenant system, and
the small landlord is only a little less
objectionable than the large one. The
European countries where agriculture really
makes people rich are in every case the
countries where small independent farmers
predominate, as in France, Holland and
Denmark. The countries where it decays
are the lands of squires and tenants, like
England and Scotland. The small indepen-
dent farmer takes a greater interest in his
holding and works it to better purpose than
the mere tenant ; he doesn’t become an
absentee cadging for a title in London and
draining Australia of money in rents ; and
when this country’s hour of trouble comes it
will find that it is the small independent
agriculturist (the one we have been burning
out in the Transvaal) who will be its main
defence. Five hundred sturdy farmers make
a formidable regiment when they are holding
a mountain pass and all they have, or hope
to have, in the world lies on the other
side. A white-handed aristocrat in London,
and 500 tenants who expect shortly to be
evicted, or to have their rents raised, and who
hear that an enemy is corning to burn the
landlord’s farm-houses and their own mort-
gaged crops, and to seize their own mort-
gaged cattle, are a very different kind of
defence. They are apt to drive off the mort-
gaged cattle and shift the movables, and let
the landlord’s house be burned. The old
saying applies here once more: “No man
ever shouldered a musket in defence of a
boarding-house,” and when Australia can’t
pay its defenders 4s. 6d. a day, but has to
call on them to fight gratis, it may realise
the fact.
The Tasmanian Mendicant at the Gate.
The election'of William Hartnoll to replace
F. W. Piesse, the lately-deceased Tasmanian
was partly a protest against the
abolition of Tattersall’s gamble, partly a com-
pliment to a generally-respected but hideously
Tory Tasmanian legislator, and partly an uprising
of the Party Which Doesn’t Want to Pay its
Way. In Tasmania just at present the Party
Which Doesn’t Want to Pay its Way is very
much in evidence. Its principal organ—which is
also the principal Freetrade paper of the island—
Launceston Examiner, openly demands that
Tasmania shall apply to the Federal Parliament
for loans to goon with, under clause 96 of the
Constitution, which provides that the Australian
Legislature may assist shoeless States that &re in
great want of a square meal. /The Examiner and
its party claim openly that Tasmania is such a
State. Of course, the Federal Parliament can’t
lend any of the money of Queensland or S.A.,
winch are also in great straits. Victoria and
N.S.W. are just pulling through with difficulty,
especially since the latest reductions in the tariff,
and can’t spare any of their surplus either. So the
probable result will be that Federal loan money
will be applied to the revenue purposes of any
impecunious State that wants aid. And the in-
terest on the loans will make the impecunious
State a little more impecunious.
* * * m
. Tasmania, in the old separate days, had a mas-
sive tariff. It was partly protective, but mostly it
was a very heavy revenue tariff of the George
Reid pattern. It wasn’t originally designed for
systematic Protection but simply for raising
money, though it protected a good deal by acci-
dent. In the last complete year of the old Tas.
tariff (1900) it yielded £464,425 —equal to a little
over 23 per cent, on all imports. Tasmania barely
paid its way with this tariff; that is to say, it had
a surplus of about £120,000, and it used £160,000
of loan money a,s revenue, so it was really £40,000
short. Tasmania, when it joined the Common-
wealth, was well aware that union meant the
abolition of duties on all its purchases from the
other States, and that involved the Toss of perhaps
£lOO,OOO a year of customs revenue. And it
sent an unexpectedly strong delegation of Free-
traders to the Federal Parliament to demand the
reckless cutting down of the rest of the revenue.
They were not so successful as they hoped to be —
still they managed to lose so much Tasmanian
revenue that, whereas the island’s customs and
excise together yielded over £489,000 in 1900,
they are now only yielding at the rate of
about £300,000 a year, or even less with
the recent further abolition of duties. Now
Tasmania returns William Hartnoll, who
demands a further reduction of duties from
the present rate, which is about 20 per cent, on all
goods imported from outside the Commonwealth,
to 15 per cent. Even supposing that, imports in-
creased 20 per cent, under a lighter tariff (which
is highly improbable, as the country hasn’t the
means to pay for them) this would mean a further
heavy loss of Tasmanian revenue. And Tasmania
declares itself a beggared and stranded community
already.
* * * *
By way of showing the advantages which wont
accrue if its policy is adopted, and the millennium
that won’t arrive, the great Freetrade organ of
Tasmania declares that, though the Federal
Government has materially reduced the customs,
duties on goods imported from outside the Com-
monwealth, still prices have not gone down, but,
have rather tended upwards. In fact, this is.
what it does say (Launceston Examiner, May
18): —-
There has been a notable remission of taxation throughi
the Customs. The consumer has not benefited by it to.
the extent he would have done had revenue duties only,
been imposed. Although he will not pay so much at the-
Customs House, he does not find the price of com-
modities decreased owing to the protective character of ;
the duties : in fact, in some cases prices are higher.
The Examiner finds, in short, that under a 20*
per cent, duty prices are as high as, or higher-
than, they were under a 23 per cent, duty ; but it.
consoles itself with the idea that a 20 per cent.,
duty which is meant to be Protective puts up.
prices, while a 23 per cent, duty which is levied!
on the same goods, with no definite intentions,.,
keeps prices down.
* * * *
According to Freetrade papers and politicians ,
all duties are paid by the consumer ; in fact it ;
is clainied that the consumer pays about twice-
as much as the duty amounts to. Under these-
circumstances it is difficult to see how the;
Freetrade party in Tasmania has anything to.
complain of. That island has now about £180,000)
a year less customs revenue than before. There-
fore, by all the canons of the Shoddy Party the.
people of Tasmania pay about £360,000 less than,
they did. Therefore it is only necessary for them!
to pay, say, £200,000 more in direct taxation, andi
the State Treasury will be richer than before, andl
the people will be £160,000 a year richer tham
before also, and Tasmania, instead of being at
mendicant howling dismally at the gate, will be ini
a glow of affluence. It is all so beautifully simple,
that it is impossible to see, on any Freetrade basis;
where the trouble comes in. Tasmania’s land-tax
is only £d. in the £ as compared with Id. in
N.S.W., and that alone furnishes an opportunity
for raising a good deal of money. It has no special
tax on large estates. It has no absentee-tax. If
it levied another £lBO,OOO a year of direct taxation,
and thereby put its finances on a reasonably sound
basis, its people would only be paying as much per
head, in State, Federal and local taxes, as the
English people do. At present they pay about 25
per cent. less. It is no part of the business of the
Federal Government to come to the assistance
of a State whose financial troubles arise solely
from the fact that it is resolved to pay about
£lBO,OOO a year less taxes under Federation than it
did when it was a separate State, merely, it would
seem, because there is now a Federal Parliament,
on which it hopes to shunt part of its responsi-
bilities. The election of William Hartnoll
ought to settle that matter definitely. Tasmania
having been relieved of a lot of taxation, accord-
ing to the theories of William Hartnoll, whom
Tasmania has just returned by a huge majority,
and having refused to put on new taxation to fill
the gap, is short of money. It elects, by a large
majority, a man who is pledged to barrack for a
further reduction of taxation, to make things
worse. It deserves to get that further reduction.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Latest African Tale.
The latest version of that gruesome story from
South Africa is, after all, only in keeping with
the accounts that many of the Australian and
Maoriland eohentingenters gave of themselves in
those letters which they sent home to their proud
relatives, and which the latter proudly handed
over for publication in admiring Jingo papers.
These contingenters described themselves as
thieves, looters, and brutish mutilators of the
dead, and it is now alleged that some Aus-
tralians, at all events, have lived up to the
character they gave themselves. The newest
English account of the proceedings which led
to the execution of one English and one
Australian officer, and the sending of two
more to penal servitude, drops all mention of the
alleged “Boer outrage” which was at. first set
up as provocation. (It is also alleged most flatly


by a returned cohentingenter that the “ Boer out-
rage ” yam was a lie, that Captain Hunt was shot
dead in fair fight, that his men promptly
bolted, and that when Hunt’s body was re-
covered there were no marks of ill-usage upon
—not one. The Boers hadn’t even knocked out
any of the teeth to keep as souvenirs, as some
horrible Australian contingenters boasted that
they did. And General Ketchener says there was
no evidence to support the alleged outrage on
Hunt. ) And in any case the ‘ ‘ outrage ” story tiad
this weak point at the outset : it set forth that
a recklessly brutal British officer ordered the
indiscriminate killing of all Boers, even if they
surrendered, and apparently whether they were
wounded or not, and that when the Boers took
him prisoner they killed him —according to a very
common prejudice against his butchery methods.
But even this story drops bodily out of the new
narrative, which seems to fly off at a tangent and
deal with a completely new set of charges. It
sets forth that certain officers in the British ser-
vice waylaid ten unarmed non-combatants who
were going in to surrender and swear allegiance,
and shot them after a mock trial in order
to get some £20,000 they had in their
possession. It tells of wholesale slaughter
of natives from sheer wantonness; of the
cruel murdering of Boer wounded and the
slaying of boys; of the cold - blooded mur-
der of a German missionary who was sus-
pected of knowing about the murder of the
10 Boers and of an intention to expose the crime ;
of the attempted murder of his servant ; of a
scheme to murder two non-commissioned officers
of their own corps who had refused to assist at the
“execution ” of the 10 Boers, and who were con-
sequently regarded as dangerous characters and
liable to give the show away ; and, finally, of
the secret murder of a trooper who warned
these two non-coms. in time to enable
them to bolt for their lives. This is
the cheerful tale told by a leading London paper,
and it is further added that justice came about
partly at least through the demands of the Ger-
man Consul-General for satisfaction on account of
his lost missionary. Ketchener’s report says
briefly that twelve murders were proved and
others suspected. These things majr be still
rather tangled and confused, but four points stand
out very conspicuously:
(1) The crimes, whatever the exact particulars of them
may have been, and by all accounts they were the
worst episodes in the story of the war, took place last
August, and the matter was kept dark even from the
London papers for over seven months. Therefore, the
art of suppressing news must have been brought to
considerable perfection in South Africa.
(2) When crimes of such magnitude can be kept dark
for seven months, it seems possible that a good many less
sensational outrages may be kept dark for much longer,
or for ever.
(3) The German authorities evidently knew of the
matter long before the British press did ; at all events, it
is said that their Consul’s demands for the replacement
of his lost missionary were responsible for setting
the tardy wheel of justice revolving. And their know-
ledge of it seems to have been previous to that his-
toric outburst of German indignation against the
manners of the British army in Africa. Of course, the
German press statements were dismissed off-hand as
“ pro-Boer calumnies ” ; still, in the face of this latest
version of the business, it would, look as if the German
press, at that date, knew rather more than the suppressed
British press, that its language wasn’t wholly unjusti-
fiable, and that it might be worth while to inquire into
the rest of its “ calumnies.” Apparently on some points
the German press wasn’t any more violent than might
have been expected.
(4) Finally, the meetings of Australian Mayors and
others to indignantly “refute ” all the charges of cruelty
brought against the British forces by the Continental
“ gutter press,” begin to look a trifle foolish. There ap-
pear to be more things in South Africa than even an Aus-
tralian Mayor can “ refute.”
Blood-drunkenness, or Slander—Which ?
Premier Seddon is reported by Sydney D.T. to
have raved last week at Papanui (M.L.):
He expressed disapproval of the action of the Imperial
Government in refusing to accept the offer of Maoris to
serve in South Africa. He promised to make representa-
tions to that effect when he reached London. His opinion
was that if 5000 Maoris under native leaders were given
their own way, and allowed to roam over the Transvaal
untrammelled by existing orders, which at present were
a drawback to the British forces, they would soon put
the Boers down. There was too much kid glove business
going on at the front, but the Maoris never allowed
enemies to hurt them again.
This comes immediately on top of the news about
the execution of English and Australian officers for
numerous cold-blooded murders of prisoners in
S’Africa. The savagery of the speech is so dis-
gusting that it is surely the invention of some
enemy of Mr. Seddon’s, satirising the wild
Jingoism of the erstwhile Democrat. But, if
Mr. Seddon did speak as he is reported to have
done, did advocate the letting loose of 5000
Maoris, “under their native leaders,” “un-
trammelled by existing orders,” men “who
never allowed enemies to hurt them again,”
men from a race who have recently practised
cannibalism and mutilation —on South Africa, then
it will leave an ineradicable stain on Maoriiand’s
history if the man is allowed to remain in political
life, or even in decent society, for another day.
There is no country in the world which should
welcome him as a citizen except Dahomey.
Abyssinia would be much too civilised. The
Bulletin hopes to hear within the next few days,
either that Mr. Seddon has absolutely repudiated
the savagery attributed to him, and brought his
libellers to justice, or that his Cabinet colleagues
have put him under surveillance for medical
examination.

The Point of View.
Some comfort begins to arrive by cablegram :
London, April 4.
The Times this morning says that naturally, but quite
wrongly, the Australians feel the slur which has been cast
on the good nafhe of their soldiers.
The Commercial Advertiser, of New York, in comment-
ing upon the execution of Lieutenant Morant and Lieu-
tenant Handcock, says that the impartial punishment of
colonials by Lord Kitchener ought to check the torrent
of abuse on the Continent against Great Britain.
As regards the second item, why should this event
“stop the torrent of abuse on the Continent?”
The Continental so-called “gutter press,” which
is mostly German in this case, will probably reply
somewhat after this fashion : “ Your men, by their
own showing, were murderers of prisoners on a
large scale, including, it would appear, wounded
prisoners. Even their thin story of provocation
falls through, for, by their own story, the order
to commit wholesale murder was issued before the
alleged slaying of Captain Hunt by the Boers (a
slaying now denied on oath by some of your own
men). Their principal defence was that this
butchers’ work was so common that it shouldn’t
be regarded as a crime—in fact, they pleaded
custom as their defence. And nothing was done
to bring these murderers to justice till the
interference of a German consul, demanding an
inquiry into the alleged murder of his missionary,
helped to drag the whole business into the day-
light, and it had to be inquired into. Even the
superior officer to whom the crimes were
reported hushed the matter up. In no case
where a German consul hasn’t been making a dis-
turbance has anything been done, and it seems
quite probable that nothing would have been, done
in this case either if he hadn’t clamored about his
dead preacher.” That is how the abusive conti-
nental press will look at the matter, and that is
how the British press would look at it in similar
circumstances. As regards the other cablegram,
it is a perfectly fair statement of the case. 1 he
crimes of a few Australian contingenters are not a
slur on the Australian people in general, nor are
the crimes of a few Englishmen a smudge on the
character of all Englishmen. The trouble is that
the same justice isn’t meted out to the other
fellow. The British or Australian crime is a soli-
tary outrage, which reflects only on the perpe-
trator ; the Boer crime, or alleged crime, is always
described as an evidence of the inherent brutality
of the whole Boer race. When Edgar, the Eng-
lishman, was killed in Johannesburg before the war
(even though he was a murderer who had brutally
slain another Englishman, and he was shot by a
semi-Welsh policeman named J ones while resisting
arrest) the incident was quoted in the papers for
months as a proof of all-round Boer brutality,and the
fact that a Transvaal Judge acquitted semi-Welsh
Eolicernan Jones, and told him he had only done
is duty, was held up to execration as an evidence
that there was no justice for a Britisher in Boer
territory. When three British subjects were hor-
ribly murdered in secret at Gatton (Q.) that was
a mere isolated episode, and the idea of holding it
up as a proof of the general brutality of the Aus-
tralian character wasn’t even dreamed of. And it
is so all down the line. When it was believed
that one or two Boers had killed in cold blood
Captain Hunt (the man who gave no quarter even
to the wounded) it was held very generally that
the event, to a great extent, justified the promis-
cuous shooting of Boers in retaliation, in addition
to the killing of the man supposed to be the actual
offender. If any one advanced the equally absurd
theory that the cruelties proved against sundry
British officers would justify the Boers in an
equally promiscuous slaying of British prisoners
the idea would be rightly scouted as that of a
barbarous lunatic. The logic never applies
both ways. The fact that the Boers have
captured and released, in almost every case
with kind treatment, some 20,000 British
soldiers, a clemency almost unknown in the
annals of war, has never been quoted by any
Jingo organ, on this side of the world anyhow, as
an evidence of the kindliness of the Boer race in
general. The very occasional Boer atrocity calls
for something approaching the extirpation of the
whole Afrikander nation as a brutish and mur-
derous community, but the innumerable acts of
humanity proved to stand to the credit, of the
Farmers’ Army are only mentioned as showing that
even among a cruel and degraded people the spirit
of chivalry isn’t quite dead. There have been
more brutal outrages committed in the push
suburb of Carlton (Melbourne) during the period
of the war than have been even charged against
the whole Boer forces. Yet no one has opined
that the Victorian in general is a brute who wants
wiping off the earth, and the person who thus
opined would be a fool of the worst description.
What is the cause of this? Simply the Point of
View and the utter non-existence, at this end of
the world anyhow, of the alleged virtue of
“ British fair-play.” The fact that it is equally
non-existent in other countries doesn’t make the
case any better.
“ Drumhead ” Court-Martials.
Among the many conflicting accounts as to the
wholesale murder of Boer prisoners in South
Africa, there bristles up often the mention of
“drumhead court-martials.” This seems to sug-
gest that the Australian officers executed were in
part, at least, victims of their ignorance of military
law and of the brutal homicidal carelessness—or
worse—of the Ketchener gang in appointing
blacksmiths, drovers and what-not, as responsible
military officers in disturbed districts. Now,
whatever his deficiencies otherwise, the regular
British officer knows something of military law,
and he knows that the “drumhead court-martial”
is a myth. But what was to be expected from
“ officers ” whose whole military training had con-
sisted of a year’s service in a contingent ?
The responsible S’African authorities seem to
have known that ignorance and unscrupulousness
were good qualifications for “ irregular ” officers,
and only when ignorance went the length of murder-
ing a German citizen was the curb put on. Hand-
cock, the “ veterinary lieutenant,” had been a per-
manent-way hand on N.S. W. Railways, and then a
blacksmith, and had gone to S’Africa as a shoe-
ing-smith. Morant was a horse-poet, and
Witton had been for nine months a Victorian
Permanent Artilleryman. Witton was the oldest
soldier of the lot. Morant and Handcock had
no more right to constitute themselves a court-
martial than they had to sit as judge and jury in
an Australian Supreme Court. The Army Act
provides for the holding, in cases of emergency, of
“ field-general courts-martial ” of not less than
three members, at least one of whom must not be
of lower rank than captain. There were only two
members of the “court-martial” which shot
some of the Boers, and neither was a captain.
The convening officer cannot sit on a court-
martial and therefore Morant was barred.
Also the proceedings of such a court are
to be sent “ with all convenient speed and with a
full report of the circumstances ” to the nearest
General. So far from doing that the Bushveldt
Buccaneers kept their little “trials” quiet, and
when a German missionary started for Pietersburg
to tell the British about them, Handcock
set out after him to stop him, and someone
cold-bloodedly murdered him as he was
reading in his tent at night. The story of the
series of deliberate murders carried on by these
irregulars is worse, because more circumstantial,
than any of the “ slanders ” that have appeared in
the French or German press. And, , we repeat,
the chief responsibility must rest with the
persons who were guilty of the criminal careless-
ness, or the sanguinary design, of letting these
irregulars loose on the Boers.

A State Tattersall.
The Tasmanian Government, it is printed (and also
denied, but then almost everythingisdenied moreor
less) threatens to institute State lotteries in place of
the suppressed Tattersall, and that is exactly what
it ought to do. The Federal Postal Department
can hardly stop the delivery of letters to the State
Treasurer of Tasmania—at least it seems improb-
able that it would do so. State lotteries, run on
exactly the old Tattersall lines, would dp no
more harm to Australia’s morals than Tatter-
sall did, and nobody except, the parsons and the
bookmakers ever discovered that he did any
harm. By establishing a State Tattersall,
Tasmania would not only recover its lost
postal revenue but it would also get the
10 per cent, commission (less expenses, of
course) which Tattersall used to get, and that
ought to amount to enough to make up for its lost
customs receipts and put its finances on an easy basis
again. If Tasmania establishes a State Tatter-
sall the staff of this paper will show its practical
sympathy to the extent of taking at least 100 tickets
on next Melbourne Cup, and it will alsodo anything
else in its power to help the proceedings along.
But if that Tattersall is established it must be
a cast-iron rule that all correspondence be
addressed to the State Treasurer or the State
Premier (both addresses should be allowed), and
then the Federal Postal Department can’t block
proceedings unless it stops all letters addressed to
these two potentates —which would mean shutting
up the Tas. Government altogether—or else takes
the almost equally large responsibility of opening
all their letters and inspecting the contents.

Pontius Philp and Neil Pilate.
In view of the specially harassed condition of
Tasmania, which reckons that it got its financial
knock-out with the abolition of the tea duty, a
time may be at hand when that indignant State
will leave the infant on the Federal doorstep. In
the old separate days Tasmania’s customs and
excise revenue (£489,000 or so) paid the interest on
its debt and left about £169,000 over for
other purposes. Tasmania’s Braddon and
other Freetraders, who valued their silly
fad more than their country, helped to
cut down the tariff with such enthusiasm
that in March Tasmania’s customs and excise
revenue was at the rate of about £264,000 a-year,
which isn’t sufficient to pay interest on the debt
by some £56,000. Under these circumstances, it
would be a large and picturesque thing if Premier
Neil Lewis called on Treasurer Turner —who is
a much safer man to call on than Kingston
and solemnly handed over the island’s public debt,
and the books and papers connected therewith, and
told him to keep enough of Tasmania’s customs and
excise revenue to pay the interest out of, and
if there wasn’t enough, to alter the tariff and make
it enough, and added that, if ever things changed
and there was a balance in Tasmania’s favor, he
would call for the balance. Queensland’s Premier,
Philp, who is almost in exactly the same situa-
tion, might do likewise. It would certainly hurry
up the question of taking over the State debts.
It would put the tariff question on a business-like
basis. It would make a logical end of the
anomalous position by which the Federal Legis-
lature gets the credit of removing taxes and the
States stand the loss and the Constitution
specially provides that the surplus payable
to any State may be applied to cover in-
terest on its debt. In any case it would
stir the stagnant surface of politics by a gentle
breeze of blasphemy and consternation. After
that episode neither Queensland nor Tasmania
could float any more loans, which would be a
blessed thing for both of them. And the Federal
Government would have to take up the burden,
or there would be such a shaking-up of Australia’s
credit that none of the other States would have
much luck with their future loans, either. Pontius
Pilate once got rid of a big responsibility by
washing his hands. Pontius Philp and Neil
Pilate might do a great deal worse than try the
same experiment.
Irrigation.
An irrigation conference has been gathered
together to consider the best means of watering
the Riverina district of N.S.W. and the mallee
country of Victoria and other dry spots, out of the
waters of the Murray. The Sydney Telegraph,
to which Victoria is as the Pope to Parson Dill-
Mackt, already sees in the proposition a Victorian
conspiracy to get the better of N.S.W., inasmuch
as it is proposed to water a part of N.S.W. which
trades to a great extent with Melbourne, but as
it is impossible to do anything without the puerile
persons who mismanage the Telegraph seeing a
dark Victorian conspiracy in it somewhere, that
matter may pass. The proposed irrigation
schemes, if they can be carried out, will turn a
large area of hideously dry wilderness into valu-
able country. Incidentally, also, they are calcu-
lated to knock out the Murray’s precarious useful-
ness as a navigable stream, and complaints to that
effect have already come to hand, but that aspect
of the business is worth no consideration at all.
The trade on the Murray is not nearly so exten-
sive as is commonly supposed, and even if it were
ten times the amount it is, Australia can’t afford
to keep rivers for navigation when their waters
are so much more urgently required for more im-
portant purposes. It is shameful waste, in this.,
dry land, for a single pint of fresh water to be
allowed to flow into the sea from, the Murray and
Darling system. The engineering problem which
must some day call for solutionis that of diverting
the Murray northward from’ about Morgan in
South Australia, so that instead of reaching the
sea it will peg-out in the great low-lying interior
desert, and the last remnant of its water be used
to moisten the arid inlands. Around the
Murray and Darling there is a vast area
of low country. From Bendigo, in Victoria,
and from the sea-coast in eastern S.A., right
north through N.S.W. to near Charleville in
Queensland, the land is nowhere more than 600
feet high, and most of it much lower than that.
This great plain also covers fully one-third of
S.A. The Murray and Darling are not sufficient,
in any ordinary season, to irrigate more than a
fraction of this plain, but, so far as their waters
will go, they should be used for that purpose, and
they should have no connection with the sea what-
ever. The navigation idea is mainly a specimen of
Australian humor. If the waters of the Murray and
Darling system are used for irrigation, the pro-
duce can be shifted by rail or bullock-dray—there
will possibly be enough produce then to make rail-
ways pay. If the same waters are reserved for
navigation there will be no produce most of the
time, and the chief traffic will consist in removing
deranged farmers to the lunatic asylum. As
regards N.S.W., there is possibly something
grotesque in the idea of going in for large
irrigation schemes in the West when so
much of the comparatively well-watered
coastal country is put to so little practical use,
but still the West must be rescued sooner or
later, so far as rescue is possible. But the work
must be a Federal one, so as to ensure a fair divi-
sion of the scarce and precious fluid among the
States concerned. Victoria supplies the bulk of
the waters of the Murray. N.S.W. contributes a
little of the Murray waters, and most of those of
the Darling, though Queensland also supplies a
portion of the Darling waters. Poor, dry S.A.
supplies practically nothing except an outlet, yet
it is much too late for N.S.W. and Victoria to
take up the attitude (which they incline to do) of
sole proprietors. If the combined Murray-Darling
is exclusively their river, they should long ago
have gathered it up, and shifted the intru-
sive object but of S.A. territory. The irri-
gation problem, on the immense scale pro-
Eosed, is a fascinating matter. There are
uge borrowing and spending possibilities about
it, also great openings for banquets, trips
of inspection, functions at which important per-
sons will turn the first sod and the billionth sod
and the last sod, and will turn on the water and
open things and shut things, and give vent to wild,
whirling eloquence generally. Somebody named
M‘GREaoR has already offered to carry out a great
scheme for somewhere over £5,000,000 and another
great scheme for £6,741,000. The Bulletin
doesn’t know M ‘Gregor, but if O’Sullivan
doesn’t get at his scheme and Dan O’Connor
doesn’t make speeches about it there may be
hopes.
From Brisbane Courier :
It is our pleasant duty (says Geraldton News) to
chronicle the fact that Mr. M. Dempsey has registered an
estate of some 33 acres under the provisions of the excise,
qualifying for the bonus of 4s. paid for cane harvested by
white labor. In connection with this, Mr. Mai.. Macpher-
SON, of Stockton, is prepared to form a gang of white
cane cutters, and has made an offer to Mr. Dempsey to
load and cut his cane at 3s. 3d. per ton, on condition that
Mr. Dempsey lays the rails. We also understand that
Innisfail has registered. This is a step forward, and Mr.
Macpherson will have no difficulty in procuring a good
gang of men.
They are not long in giving practical proof that
the “small holdings” can be run with. white
labor, and it is the small holdings that will save
Queensland. The man who can load sugar in the
stifling holds of the small steam lighters up the
Johnston will willingly cut sugar in the cane-
brakes at a white wage.
+.
The value of General Hutton as military com-
mandant is now fully understood in Australia, if
it never was before. It required a good, loud
militant howler to throw suitable language at
Australia’s Second Very Drunk Federal Contin-
gent of Farm-Burners, and Hutton filled the bill.
The Second Very Drunk Contingent was a failure
all along. It was largely composed of demoralised
returned soldiers, who had lost their _ taste for
work after a long course of burning private pro-
perty, smashing goods and chattels, and dragging
women off to the cohencentration camps. Appar-
ently, many of them had also lost their taste for
fighting, and desertions were constant. So were
other things. The ranks were always being filled
up, and yet the Second Very Drunk Contingent
was always falling short of its proper strength.
When the time came for taking the oath it
fell quite a lot short. There was a depart-
ing of the Farm-Burners to the . four winds
with this country’s lawful victuals inside them,
and, in some cases, this country’s lawful property
under one arm. The Second Very Drunk Con-
tingent was got on board in a frightful state, drew
its month’s advance of pay, and then, in many
cases, sneaked over the side into a waterman’s
boat and was no more seen. When the actual
time of departure came the cohentingent (the
N.S.W. instalment of it, that is) was partly miss-
ing and partly tight, and winking confidentially
at the infuriated Hutton. It departed covered
with objuration, with its original commander sus-
pended for incapacity in allowing it to behave as
it did, and the police are now in pursuit of the
lost portion of it. Either the officers had terribly
bad luck in the selection of their armed drunks, or
else the supply of sober men who are willing to
burn Boer women out of their homes at 4s. 6d. a
day is running a trifle short.
Quaint yell from Launceston Examiner (Tas-
mania) :
It was high time some steps were taken by the British
nation to protest against this wave of lies and misrepre-
sentation. Since the counter-demonstrations have taken
place there has been a partial cessation of ill-feeling, and
its virulence has in many quarters raised up defenders
who previously were disposed to favor the Boer cause.
Evidence, even from German sources, is accumulating to
the effect that there is not the slightest truth in the
assertions made. German residents, and those of other
European nationalities in South Africa, are protesting
against the vilification of the British soldiers, and the
fact that ex-Presidents Kruger and Steyn and General
De Wet entrusted their wives to the keeping, of the con-
querors is the strongest proof possible of their humanity.
There is a postscript to be added to that last
clause. The retreating Boers, in many cases, left
their women behind them, as the French mostly
did when they retreated in 1870, and the Aus-
trians when they were driven through Bohemia in
1866, and the Danes when they were pursued out
of their southern provinces in ’64. In fact, as a,
retreating army can’t well carry a swarm of
women and children with it on long, forced, night-
and-day marches, they are always left behind,
unless the pursuing force consists of Turks,
Afghans, or other such genial characters. But
when the Boers left their women-folk behind
they mistook Ketchener for the ordinary
kind of soldier. They didn’t know that he
would make war on women, burn the,ir homes,
and pen them up with their children in
concentration camps, where the little ones would
perish at the rate of 2000 a month. No Boer
general has been heard of as voluntarily leaving
his wife behind since Ketchner broke loose.
Ip the story of the S’ African atrocities told by an
Australian Bushveldt Buccaneer, now in Mel-
bourne, be true, those two officers deserved —not
shooting, but hanging. Melb. Herald, which
printed it, seemed to have been impressed with its
genuineness. This man said that Hunt was shot
in fair fight; he saw and spoke to the buccaneer
who prepared the body for burial, and there was
no mutilation whatever. The narrator gave a
detailed account, too, of the shooting, in, cold
blood, without the semblance of a court-martial,
of eight unarmed Boer prisoners near Pietersburg
before Hunt’s death. Later on, six other defence-
less prisoners were murdered; and still later a
third lot of three —one being an old man and
another a boy of 16. Also one of the Car-
bineers—Vanburen—was made away with, and
the next victim was a German missionary.
Also, when Hunt was killed at the assault on
Viljoen’s house, he (Hunt) was aided by 200
armed Kaffirs, who were to be permitted to loot
the place afterwards. This is probably the in-
formation which General Hutton hints he holds
back, and, take it all round, it is just what might
be expected of some of the Jingo-press-inflamed
cohentingenters this country exported in its later
drafts. A significant point is that, though
there must be many ex-soldiers in Australia who
knew all about the crimes, until the cables told the
tale not one of them considered the matter suffi-
ciently important to mention. Can anyone sup-
Eose the actions of the Bushveldt Buccaneers to
e isolated eases, or that these officers hadn’t
good precedent for their conduct ? The true story
of the later stages of this “humane” war are
gradually coming to light.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
