»
Something of the great silence that has
lately pervaded Sydney show business is to be
lifted on 19th April. Things have been very deso-
late in recent times around the.city with the large
damp harbor. The Criterion closed through
plague, and, though the episode is ancient history
now, it remains shut. The Royal banged its front
door long, long ago when Musgrove’s Co. gave up
the struggle ; and Her Majesty’s took fire and is
now mostly ash. This left Sydney with the dry
husks of a melodrama and two variety shows—good
enough things in their way but not calculated to
provide soul-food for a great mind. Under these
circumstances, it is pleasing to hear that J. C.
Williamson opens the Royal on 19th April, which
is the Saturday of next week. The co. will
include Miss Janet Waldorf, from the United
States, of whom most people have heard ; and
Mr. Norval Macgregor, of whom everybody has
heard, seeing that the father of one half of him
fed his flock in a famous poem on the Gram-
pians, and the other half of him charged in a des-
Srate and irresponsible kilt at Culloden. Also,
. H. Vincent returns to the Australian stage
for this occasion only after an absence of 12 years.
The season is to be partly Shakespeare and partly
otherwise, and “Ben Hur ”is off for good. That
great Hebrew drama required as much machinery
to run it as would suffice for an ocean-liner, and
it compressed so much bad luck into its short
season that no one feels inclined to build the
gigantic structure up again. The chances are
that the wheel of the Jew’s chariot has run over
the Roman’s ear for the last time in Australia.
*# * *
Salerno is still the great draw at Sydney
Palace, and he carriesout his remarkable juggling
feats now with more certainty than on the opening
night. Crowded houses have been the rule during
the past week, and the season promises to be a
most successful one.
** * *
•‘The Alhambra Girl,” a comedy of the
mother-in-law order, was revived by the Cosgrove
Co. at Sydney Empire on Saturday night. The
prompter deserved a call for the conspicuous
part ne played in the drama. The Cosgrove Co.
has its good' points, but on first nights it is apt
to leave the impression that some of the co. only
received their parts that same morning. Miss
Lilian Raymond, who plays the lightsome Al-
hambra Girl, gets the married hero into those
weini, unreal troubles that the mother-in-lawed
comic man always experiences on the stage, but is
much too large and solid for her part. Her best
asset is the cynical laugh with which she
sees the troubles and misery that perpetually be-
fall the stout and crushed hero. Miss Rose
Phillips, the Sapho of the other day, makes a
nice, good wife to the worried hero, and a small
girl, lost in a voluminous white gown, gets him
into further unhappiness as his niece. John Cos-
grove takes the part of the hero, and every time a
sin he hasn’t done is found out he gives a subdued
howl and says the avalanche is upon him. Other-
wise, the unhappiness of married life sits ill upon
his round and cheerful countenance, though his
gibbering when the avalanche does descend is
effective. Mr. J. P. West has not lost his
cunning in old-man parts, and the rest of the com-
pany is much as usual, which remark is meant to
be taken severely.
, '.. & * * *
Bland Holt’s “Span of Life ” at Sydney
Lyceum progresses promiscuously through Africa
and other places to packed houses and an extra
appreciative gallery. The wild black sea and the
gaily lighted vessel moving away from the excited
fog-bell on the lighthouse keep to their parts as
well as usual, but some day Mr. Bland Holt
should think of letting fly a seagull from the
wings, or hire a dolphin or two to pop up
occasionally above a stage wave. A good
many horses appear in this drama and
circus round above the heads of the orchestra, and
the Harley Bros, form the living bridge as before.
For originality of idea the play is not up to the
previously staged “ Hearts are Trumps,” but it is
more pleasing to the average public, which likes
its Baker as a stalwart hero, who seizes hostile
foreign Powers by the heels and knocks their
heads against a tree, rather than as a moralising
Australian millionaire. They have simple tastes,
these melodrama goers, and their ideal hero is
built after the manner of Samson, the simple-
minded, lone-hand Jew, who never seems to have
had a really bright idea in his life, but tore up
lions occasionally.
Fitzgerald’s circus still rides, jumps, and
jokes in its old canvas home to crowded audiences.
Miss Rhodesia, the Moultons, the dogs, and the
other performers keep up to their old standard,
though nothing new has occurred to the pro-
gramme since the last time these persons attracted
this paper’s eye. Twenty new acts, however, are
promised for this Saturday, when a changed bill
wul be presented, and the lion is studying a
bright, new part wherein lie will catch flies in an
entirely novel fashion. The lion is said to be a
patient and conscientious study, and sits up late
at night with a wet towel round his head learning
his dialogue.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli added the Craggs to its pro-
gramme last Saturday, and those agile acrobats
toppled about in their customary daring fashion.
Just as good a turn in the same style is that of
the Lenton trio, who have now improved a good
deal on their business.
** * *
“Sweet and Twenty,” the little three-act
comedy which Mr. Brough introduced to Mel-
bourne on Saturday last, belongs to the sweet and
sickly order of stage stories. In the beginning it
promises to be humorous so far as a quiet vicarage
environment will permit. A handy man-servant
(Robt. Brough) with a light-hearted genius for
dropping crockery and falling through glass
houses and cucumber-frames contributes a series
of smashes to the first act. This casual destroyer
is popular as long as he continues to wreck the
vicar s property, but in the second act he breaks
nothing to speak of, and subsequently he is seen
no more. Miss Susie Vaughan is a similar dis-
appointment. At the outset she appears as the
garrulous mother of Miss Sweet and Twenty, and
her tongue works on a swivel for five minutes
whilst her relatives and friends suffer in silence.
Then she passes away like a whirlwind, never to
return. Also a bright young she-boy assists
the first act. with a few remarks suitable to his
time of life, but leaves the rest of the
comedy to its sentimental fate. “Sweet and
Twenty” gets to t be very soft and unconvining
when the trouble begins. The vicar’s two sons
love the one girl, and she prefers the younger
brother because he wears a naval uniform and
doesn’t look so despondent as the brother who is
studying for the Church. Moreover, the damsel
persists in her rather absurd affection for the same
brother after he has been ejected from the Navy
for being drunk and disorderly. Probably the
other brother spoils his chance by persisting in his
original policy of gloom, for when Cecil Ward
runs to gloom he can be as trying as the toothache.
Cecil intercepts letters and otherwise strives to
prevent .his trustful relative capturing the girl,
put it is always obvious that Love will ter-
riumph in the end. The heroine has set her heart
on marrying the comparatively cheerful boy, and
the heart of a modern British sugar-candy comedy
heroine is not a thing to be bruised. She achieves
her unpromising object in life. The prodigal
returns home for the second time, laden with
virtuous resolves and temperance principles, and
parrots-—the results of a nine months’ sojourn in
Australia, of all places ! Then the girl compels
him to cease pretending that he doesn’t want to
marry her. As for the gloomy plotter who is
qualifying for the Church—well, he doesn’t shoot
himself. The curtain descends upon the sad fact
that he has decided to live on. The pistol goes
off, but the man remains.
• * * *
Miss Brenda Gibson plays the heroine
quite charmingly, and with all the necessary
power where force is wanted. The emotions of
the brethren, one of whom —Ernest Vere —has
only one note, in his voice, excite no sympathy
from the audience, whereas Miss Gibson gives
warmth and interest to her side of the story.
Miss Emma Temple is not at all old and earnest
m an elderly character. On the other hand,
Charles Garry is the most natural of vicars, though
the dramatist makes him a nonentity in the play.
The vicar seems to be of.no consequence compared
with his wife, an invisible lady who rules the
household from her bedroom upstairs.
* * * .
The revival of “In Honor Bound ” as a
curtain-raiser to “Sweet and Twenty,” reminds
one that Sydney Grundy couldn’t be guilty of so
stagey a playlet nowadays. Brilliant acting can
blind one to the falsity of a play which is at
least smartly dialogued. The performances of Mr
and Mrs. Brough, Orlando Daly and Miss Chat-
teris were not brilliant enough for that. Besides,
Miss Chatteris displayed a pair of red hands
against a white satin gown, and no artificial situa-
tion will support a red-handed lady in full evening
", ,°be ” is billed for next Saturday
(12th) and Monday. After which, two nights
each of “ The Second in Command ” and “ The
Tyranny of Tears.”
** * *
“The Wrong Mr, Wright” has come to
stay many weeks at Melbourne Bijou. Seeing
that the third eccentric comedy by Broadhurst ie a
rather more violent success than the previous two
—“Smith” and “Jones”—the talent of the
author probably amounts to genius. Broadhurst’s
stones are naturally farcical, or farcically natural,
it doesn t matter which. His characters have a
more human interest for the world in general than
the world is aware of whilst they are rushing
through their work. “ The Wrong Mr. Wright,”
in particular, appeals to the feelings of the
accountants and clerks’ association, and all other
inky toilers, for when the full-blooded, close-
fisted millionaire in the uncharitable frock-coat
has been converted to tweed knickers and a
flaming red vest by the artful touch of
woman s hand and the sound of a voice
that is music, he raises the salaries of his
clerical staff 50 per cent, by telegram, and dates
the rise back six months. Singleton Sites, from
Chicago, is about the strongest farcical character
an actor Can ever hope to get, and George Wil-
loughby makes a lot of it, although he continues
to discount the effect of his otherwise vigorous
style by toddling to and from the filter. The lady-
detective is a positively picturesque character,
notwithstanding that she has to bump a 13-
stoiie capitalist off the sofa. And to describe
Miss Roxy Barton as a Dream is to understate the
case. She’s the sweetest peach in the whole
Garden of Eden—the brightest daisy in the dell—
the prize-pebble on the beach—
Just once in every twenty years, or so,
The bardlet’s weary fancy turns to love ;
Then some well-appointed vision here below
Takes the semblance of an angel from above.
Now, despite her hats and frocks, he
Can persuade himself that Roxy
Is a plumed and haloed angel from above.
She sits upon a blotting pad, ’tis true,
According to the notions of the crowd,
Whilst her figure, in the poet’s higher view
Is enchantingly reclining on a cloud.
Though she squatted on a box, he
Would declare that dainty Roxy
Was an angel smiling sweetly on a cloud.
Just once in every twenty years, or more,
’Tis good to get away from sordid truth ;
As he gazes at the girl he can adore,
Sure, the baldest-headed party is a youth.
And there’s simple orthodoxy
In this hymn of praise to Roxy,
I or Love’s the true religion of our youth.

At the Royal a change of melodrama is to
happen on Saturday. “The Night Birds of
London will make way for “The Greed of Gold,”
otherwise “ The Story of a Dumb Man.”
** * *
“ A Runaway Girl ” has arrived at the
last weeks” period, and probably she will drop
out of the bill, exhausted, before the end of the
month. Meanwhile “The Mikado” is getting
ready for production, although the Gilbertian
picture of Joe Chamberlain’s new ally begins to
look very like a, foul libel on the Hemp'ire. This
py way of starting a newspaper discussion in the
interests of the management. Some more new
features were added to the present show on Satur-
day. said features being mostly of a ballet nature.
A Runaway Girl ” should command much pat-
ronage for the ballet of her declining days. It is
a warm, festive dance, and- the general effect of
legs, arms, and light trimmings will be admired
by all who believe in the Habeas Corpus Act and
the liberty of the subject.
*# m *
Just as this goes to press an almighty
crowd is listening to the opening notes of Amy
Castles’ Concert in Melbourne; but as to the
quality of those soaring notes this paper can say
nothing till next week. The little girl from
Bendigo, Paris and other plages where they
grow and make singers, is to warble four times in
Sydney Town Hall. She appears on the evenings
of April 17th, 19th, and 21st. All who are unable
to get admission on these dates wilt have a final
opportunity at a matinee on April 22nd,
#* * *
Williamson, “after due consideration,”
fixed his prices for the Castles concerts at 65., 45.,
and 25., but the speculators quickly saw to it that
Melbourne wasn’t going to enjoy its songbird at
those rates. Day after the plan opened front seats
were snapped at from 10s. 6d. to £l, while hardly
the humblest worshipper far away back in the
South Gallery got there for less than 4s. The
Mayoral reception was a spasm of public adula-
tion. The Town Hall was unapproachable for the
crowds waiting to get but a glimpse of the Castles,
and when she was driven off an hysterical throng
of youths and women crushed almost under the
horses’s feet, and raced after the vehicle up the
street, cheering and shouting in a spasm of intense
emotion. Amy Castles has already got Melbourne
by the wool.
** * *
This week’s entertainment at Rickards’
Opera House is the same as last week, plus Miss
Ray Jones, a little lady who sang prettily in
comic opera of yore, and is singing even more
prettily at present. The club jugglers will leave
the Opera House on Friday, but the name of the
other person or persons required to fill their vacant
place nas not been stated at time of writing.

“F.C.” describes a new Melbourne show :
Somebody lately thrust on me a copy of Elijah Dowie’s
paper, Leaves op Healing, and, as I suffer from a chronic
thirst and a squint, I decided to try a course of diluted
Dowie per medium of his local overseer, Wilbur Glenn
Voliva, who has shifted from the little Prahran tabernacle
to Queen’s Hall, Bourke-st. 1 hadn’t been there since
the days when, as the Victoria Hall, Frank Clark exploited
his Silk Stockings company, and my emotions on enter-
ing were considerable. Wilbur, etc., was assisted by
a small dark man with a large head, and a tall fair man
with a small ditto. There were present 30 men, 50 women,
1 Salvationist, 12youngsters,and afox-terrier pup—chough
the last was afterwards ejected. At the singing Voliva
deftly waved the audience to their feet but himself sat—
being a deputy-prophet doubtless carries certain privi-
leges. During the prayers there was considerable groan-
ing and many “ Amens,” chiefly by elderly gentlemen
with a fringe of white hair round a bald top-piece ; these
were evidently epicures in emotions, and took their re-
ligion as soulfully as they would the ballet on week
nights. Voliva’s education as a lecturer has been sadly
neglected, interjections during the alleged sermon find-
ing him utterly lost for a retort. He would consider for
some seconds, then come down on one persistent inter-
rupter with great tonnage ; but meanwhile the objecting
one had thought up a fresh observation, and W.G..V. was
paralysed for a further space. Voliva said the Faith-
healing meetings were held on Wednesdays, and though
I reckoned he was coming the double on me, I risked a
second attendance. This time he was assisted by a
couple of phonographs, or gramophones as he called
them. These related, among other items, Mrs. Dowie’s
address to the women of Zion, and from the method of
its delivery I opine Mrs. D. to be a short, fat woman who
labors heavily in her speech. Also, I fancy she ran up
several hills just before speaking into the’machine. I
reckoned the healing business was about due, but either
the apostle hadn’t enough faith, or the collection wasn’t
up to the mark, or something ; anyhow, there was no
healing.

The Adelaide Royal re-opened Easter
Saturday with a strong No. 2 co. in “San Toy.”
Brownlow had a tremendous reception. Fred
Graham, has apparently modelled himself closely
on Lauri, and Chas. Trood has borrowed Howard
Vernon’s “ Mikado ” voice for the Emperor.
Rose Musgrove is almost pathetic in the name
Sart, while Lilian Digges has most of the fun.
he Tivoli ladely treated itself to a three-days’
recess, this being the first since the house opened
in June, 1900. It is now going strong with
Winton (and .M’Ginty), the Lundgreeus, and
Callahan and Mack.

The Wild Cat Column.
H. : (1) It is simply that out of every Is.
Eaid by the insured, and of every Is. of interest produced
y the accumulated funds, the Industrial Department
spends 6Jd. in expenses, and 5Jd. is left to pay for actual
assurance. (2) Try the 3d. a week table ; allow for. the
fact that years must pass before the bank would allow
any interestand, in the period you mention, the Savings
Bank would give over £4O against the co.’s £3O. And if
the payments are dropped before maturity , as they are in
the majority of cases, the bank gives back the money paid
with interest ; the industrial assurance concern doesn’t,
which more than sets off the death-risk. (3) You
assume that a proprietary office is safer than a
mutual, one if all the big policy-holders die by
epidemic, because the shareholders must stand the loss,
but it all turns on whether the shareholders’ capital is
big enough to. stand it. The Citizens’, for example, has
£8,376,000 of insurance in force, and £20,000 paid-up
capital, also £lBO,OOO of callable capital, though no co.
expects to collect all its callable capital at a crisis ;
the A.M.P. has nearly six times the business, but its
reserve-fund (£245,000) is 12 times the other co.’s paid-up
capital. And the reserve-fund doesn't take £2O per cent,
dividend out ofthe profits. (4) The Ordinary Branch of the
Citizens’ was run last year at a cost, including dividend,
of about £l5 15s. 6d. per cent, of total income, which was
very cheap. The A.M.P. was run for about £9 2s. per
cent., which was a lot cheaper, and the Maoriland Govern-
ment Department for £l4 9s. lid. As regards your state-
ment that proprietary management must be cheaper than
the mutual system, the five most cheaply-managed British
offices are mutual societies, and the 23 most costly are
proprietary. This is dealing with British offices only ;
you can find the figures in Whitaker’s Almanac for 1902.
The expense-rate of the most cheaply-managed British
proprietary office is more than double that of the most
cheaply-managed British mutuai society. The expense-
rate of the most expensively-managed British proprietary
office is more than 4J times that of the most expensively-
managed British mutual office.

That misleading and gasbaggy politician, Theo-
dore Fink, M.L.A., has been lecturing in Melbourne about
the need for retrenchment to save the State from
insolvency and in the course of the proceedings (Age
report) he explained that “ Victoria was the heaviest-
taxed land in the world—he meant of civilised countries.”
This dull lie, which the Tory person tells in almost every
country, was originally invented by that Tory person to
avoid paying a.reasonable amount of taxation, but it is so
pitiably silly in Victoria’s case that it seems strange
that Fink should lend himself to it. Victoria is less
heavily taxed (local and national taxes included) than
Great Britain, France, Holland, Queensland, Westralia,
Tasmania or Maoriland. Taking national taxation in the
Australasian States alone this is how the results compared
during last financial year (figures from Coghlan’s
“ Statistics of the Six States of Australia and Maori-
land,” page 24):
Total Taxation.
Northern Territory .. .. £6 10 2 pel head
Westralia 6 6 2 „
Maoriland .. „ .. 319 8 „
Queensland .. .. „ 313 8 „
Tasmania .. „ 312 6
Victoria 214 11 „
South Australia .. .. 212 8 „
N.S. Wales 2 4 7 „
This includes direct and indirect taxation. In direct
taxation (principally land-tax, income-tax, probate and
stamp duties—the kind of taxation the Tory most objects
to) the results come out thus :
Direct Taxation.
£ s. d. £ g. d.
Maoriland, 1 0 4 p. head N.S.W., 0 15 8 p. head
Tasmania, 017 3 „ Queensland, 013 3 „
Westralia, 016 0 „ Victoria, 012 1 „
S. Aus., 015 9 ~ N. Territory, 0 8 4 ~
The Tory person who originally invented the yarn about
Victoria’s heavy sufferings from quite unusual taxation was
just a plain liar; the Tory person who repeats them is either
a silly ignoramus or something else. The curse of Australia is
that poor, whining, unmanly, un-British coward, the Tory,
who, in lightly-taxed States like Victoria, S.A. and N.S.W.,
is always weeping about the excessive burden of taxation,
till he persuades the people that it is really impossible for
Australia to pay enough taxation to cover its outgoings,
and, under that impression, it goes on borrowing and
getting deeper and deeper in the mire. Tory politicians
who talk this tearful slush about the inability of Australia
to stand any more taxation, even for the urgent purpose
of reducing the great loan curse, are a disgrace to the
manhood of the country. Victoria spends much money
on salaries and foolishness which should go to pay for
railways and other public works, but it doesn’t raise too
much money—in fact, it doesn’t raise enough.
* • • *
Some interesting points:
(1) In New South Wales, and also in some other States,
the Parliamentary authority to raise a loan never lapses.
Therefore See and O’Sullivan, if they feel so inclined, can,
while Parliament is not sitting and without saying any-
thing to Parliament if it was sitting, borrow over
£25,000,000 (twenty-five millions) in London or New York
or wherever the money is obtainable. At various times
during the past 50 years loans to that amount have been
authorised by Parliament but not raised, and the power
may be used at any time.
(2) Money borrowed by N.S.W. for one purpose may be
used for any other puipose. A good many years ago an
Act was passed which gave this remarkable power.
Besides, loans raised nowadays are always for certain
specified purposes “ and other public works.” The N.S.W.
Government raised £3,644,918 for the Sydney land
resumptions and works connected therewith “ and other
public works.” Only about £700,000 has gone in paying
for resumptions.
(3) There is no law stating what kind of works may be
paid out of loans and what kind should be charged to
revenue. Everything is charged to loans, therefore, that
an unprincipled Ministry feels inclined to charge.
South Australia’s Premier Jenkins, while re-
porting a probable deficit of £120,000, spoke comfortingly
about the Crow-eater State’s public debt, which, he said,
was mostly represented by public works that yielded 31
per cent. It is a pity that someone didn’t rise ana
scoff at Jenkins Over £6,000,000 of S.A.’s public
debt (about a quarter of the whole amount) is repre-
sented by no interest-producing assets at all. It
was spent on roads, bridges, fortifications, schools,
Government offices, “improvements to Crown lands,”
dead and gone village settlements, “ geological examina-
tion of the province ” (think of geologically examining
the province out of loans), and over £900,000 was used to
make good revenue deficits. S.A. has to raise about
£300,000 a year now by taxation to pay interest with, that
being the amount that is not provided by the receipts
from its public works, which is a heavier interest burden
on each inhabitant than that of any European country
save France. False prophets and false Jenkinses may

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Iron founder,
&c.,of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either.patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, Burning, patent Nejyambo earth-scoops or tank,
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work.*

Vanning Tables.—The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known.ast.be Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites,.carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done bn “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. . The
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-,
street, Melbourne, and 40 rlunter-street, Sydney, are the

Will some member rise in each of the State
Parliaments and put these two questions in a firm voice :
(1) When a railway loan of (say) £4,000,000 falls due,
and is renewed at (say) 92, making an addition of £32Q,0.00
to the public debt, is that £320,000 added to the amount
of debt incurred for railway purposes, and for which the
railways have to provide the interest ?
(2) And if it isn’t, why isn’t it, and what is the £320,000
debited to, and what becomes of the alleged management
of the railways on commercial principles?

Prospecting for gold in Western Victoria
(Ararat district) is not all glitter. This from one at it:
“ I would have written before, only I hadn’t any ink (and
no lead-pencil), and this is a brute of a place to get any-
thing . . . in short, it’s a detestable hole. . . . I have to
walk about four miles to and from work, and when I get
home at night I generally have to try and- catch a rabbit
for next day’s tucker. I got one to-night, so. am right for
a feed to-morrow. I have not found enough gold to pay
for salt to put on the rabbit j-eb.”

After much amending, the Federal Public Ser-
vice Bill enacts that Federal public servants must insure
their lives with any rotten (or otherwise) life assurance
office Carrying on business in Australia, and if they tan a
with a very had specimen and lose their money, it is
their own look-out. That is to say, a policy for (say)
£5OOO in the Maoriland Government Insurance Depart-
ment (the safest office in Australasia), or in the ocottisn
Widows’ Fund (assets sixteen millions, and probably tne
safest office in the United Kingdom) won t do, because
they have no local offices, while one in the lamenta le
1.01 F., or the People’s Prudential, or the Australian
Metropolitan, because they have local offices, will be quite
sufficient! ! ! If the Federal Government lacks the cour-
age to establish a Life Assurance Department of its own
it should at least nominate the seven or eight local insti-
tutions which are absolutely above suspicion, and strictly
confine the business to them. The Government can t
honorably compel its servants to spend their money on
life assurance without taking some responsibility in tne
way of pointing out where they can assure safely.
* * * *
The chief reason why Sydney’s water supply is
in a precarious state may be found in the Water and
Sewerage Board’s finances. It should have been laid down
by the Government as that Board’s duty to raise enough
money to pay working expenses, maintenance, intereston
loans, and to gradually pay off the debt, so that when it
became necessary to spend another million or two or three
on an extension of the system it wouldn’t be a case of
heaping one debt on top of another. Instead this is how
things have gone :
Sydney Water and Sewerage Department.
Revenue. Expenditure. Deficit. Surplus.
Dec., 1894 £254,301 £249,943 £1358
June, 1895 128,476 138,458 £9,982
„ 1896 259,843 280,824 20,981
„ 1897 263,637 290,216 26,679
„ 1898 268,570 302,647 34,077
„ 1899 298,288 306,697 8,409
„ 1900 312,433 330,959 18 526
„ 1901 328,639 353,474 24,835
The last year when the Board paid its way was 1894.
There has been no serious increase in management ex-
penses despite the growth of the watered and sewered
area—in fact, the management expenses absorbed a very
much smaller percentage of the revenue in 1901 than in
1896. But as the system grew older and more in need
of repair tlie cost of maintenance increased heavily, and
the interest bill also increased, and these two items
swallowed up the new earnings wholesale after this
fashion
Maintenance. Interest on Loans.
1894 .. £43,411 .. £180,302
1901 .. 67,002 .. 254,030
Any sensibly-managed department would have raised
enough revenue to write off a large sum every year for
depreciation. The money thus raised would have been
applied in reduction of its debt ; so that when the sys-
tem began to grow old and cracked the saving in interest
would have more than balanced the increased cost of
maintenance. The board was partly responsible for the
fact that this wasn't done —only partly. The occasional
surpluses of the early days were not nearly large enough
to provide for depreciation, but, such as they were, they
seem to have been grabbed by the Government and
spent as ordinary revenue, instead of being applied
in reduction of the water and ' sewerage debt.
Thus the board’s debt didn’t diminish ; in fact,
as many things as possible besides extensions
appear to have been charged to it, and both it and the
interest bill constantly increased. The later borrowings
were for the purpose of carrying the Board’s works into
thinly-peopled suburbs, where the returns on the money
spent were less than on the city works, and interest began
to grow out of all proportion to revenue. As the works
grew larger and older maintenance also became a heavy
item. The Treasurer had to supply the Board with
money instead of blewing its profits, because no one had
courage to raise the rates. Despite the increase in the
cost of maintenance, the allowance for repairs seems
to have been kept dangerously low, presumably
out of regard for the anguished Treasurer, and
the works appear to have gone somewhat to seed.
The extension of Sydney on to the high surrounding
country made a water system which depended on
pumping almost obsolete. So now the Jake Garrard
department has all its debt intact, an interest bill that
increases every year, a water system that isn’t worth the
money owing upon it, an unreliable water supply, and a
big annual deficit—all through the early policy of making
things as easy as possible for the moment, and letting
the future slide.
Just by way of showing that there are worse
things in the Water and Sewerage way than even the
Sydney Jake Garrard department, here is the fearsome
record of Melbourne’s Water and Sewerage system, bossed
by hard Fitzgibbon :
Melbourne Water and Sewerage Department.
Revenue. Expenditure. Deficit.
1895 .. £166,522 .. £195,785 .. £29,263
1896 .. 167,344 .. 227,871 .. 60,527
1897 .. 163,735 .. 258,216 .. 94,481
1898 .. 168,553 .. 281,421 .. 112,868
1899 .. 236,100 .. 306,002 - ... 69,902
1900 .. 269,213 .. 337,079 .. 67,866
1901 .. 279,308 .. 362,516 .. 83,208
By comparison with the awful Fitzgibbon thing in Mel-
bourne (Heaven only knows where It is going to !) Jake
Garrard’s department is a model of solvency. The
Melbourne institution has gone in for wild and fearsome
sewage-farm speculations and other extraneous matters
till its insolvency (unless the Government holds it up)
seems dead certain.

As the House of Representatives keeps knocking
one item after another off the tariff the revenue keeps
diminishing. Customs and excise yielded £773,952 in
January, £756,429 in February, £721,406 in March, and
the biggest loss (calculated at £600,000 a year) through
the knocking off of the tea, rice and some other duties,
had hardly had time to make itself felt in March.: A very-
little more re-committing of the tariff to please the selfish
and politically mad-drunk M'Miilan and Conroy and
Pumpkin Smith push, and only two States will have a
pretence of solvency left.

There were some large Trustee Companies (large, as far
as their reserves are concerned) in Australia before the
’93 smash, but their reserves were mostly invested in big
buildings with mortgages on them—and there are no big
companies now. Still they are all earning dividends
now,-which was far from being the case a few years ago,
and hi eight cases; the dividend has increased materially
since 1898(the Queensland Trustees has recklessly doubled
its dividend since then). The Perpetual Trustees, Execu-
tors, and Agency of Dunedin (Maoriland), and the National
(Melbourne) seem to make no serious effort at building up
their reserves, and most of their money goes in big divi-
dends, Two strikingly progressive institutions are the
Perpetual (Sydney) and the Ballarat Trustees (Ballarat,
Vic ). The former promises, in another year, to leave
the National behind very badly, and to become the lead-
ing trustee co. of Australia. The Ballarat concern applied
most of its past reserves to writing-off a goodwill item
the price it paid for a business acquired some years ago,
but since it got rid of this incubus its progress has been
very rapid. The Union Trustees would have shown an
addition of £l3B to reserves but for sums written off de-
preciation fund. Taking all things into account, how-
ever, the trustee-business shows up better in these bad
times than most varieties of joint-stock enterprise.

The Equitable Building Society (Launceston,
Tasmania), now in its 32nd year, publishes a solid and
promising balance-sheet for 1901. The year’s profits were
£3Bll. A dividend of 7 per cent, to shareholders absorbed
£3282, and the balance raises the reserves to £13,068. In
comparison with the capital of about £51,000 the reserves
are very substantial indeed.
• • • •
Kempthorne Prosser and Co.’s New Zealand
Drug Co. (Dunedin, M.L.) publishes a cheerless balance-
sheet for the year ended 31st January, 1902. It sets forth
that during the year the net profits of the business were
£2747. Against this a 7 per cent, dividend is paid,
absorbing £13,549. A sum of £2554 is written off to cover
over-valuation of stock, and this cheerful process of pay-
ing £16,103 when the profits are only £2747 produces
lamentable results. Certain premiums on new shares
(£1527), a matter which, in every well-conducted com-
pany, should be carried to reserves, have been treated as
ordinary earnings; but even with this, the total reserves
dropped from £30,593, at January, 1901, to £18,(64
—a fall of £11,829. For a co. of such dimensions (the
paid-up capital is £194,102) these reserves don’t amount
to much.

* * * •
Editor Bulletin. —Be par (“Wild Cat,” B.
15/3 ’O2 on the coupon business at Broken Hill. The
’umble individual referred to made such an enormous
profit that a company (not co-operative, but in reality a
syndicate of 25) was formed ostensibly to “ out” the u.i.
They found it such a good thing that the majority of
shopkeepers here resolved to start the Mutual Concern in
opposition to this syndicate. The Mutual Co. is formed
entirely of the local traders, who, seeing that the public
will have coupons, have made their share-list large
enough to admit every shopkeeper as a holder. In
redeeming, they deduct simply the office and working ex-
penses, and then receive back the surplus remaining,
which they themselves have made. It will be readily
seen that they are thus only paying one man a weekly
salary to regulate a cash-discount system amongst them,
and considering the large number of shareholders (over
109) the expense is a mere nothing to them individually.
The shareholders of the Mutual are the most anxious
people to have the coupon-system abolished by legislation,
but, till that comes about, they are simply a self-protective
association against the coupon-fiend, as represented by
syndicates and monopolies.—The “Mutual” Secretary.

* * * •
Another of the English co.’s that were going to
make pots out of Broken Hill zinc-lead sulphides has gone
under—the Smelting Corporation, which owned the
Burnham process, and spent over £130,000 at Ellesmere.
Things didn’t turn out right; especially when the mining
co’s found it paid best to concentrate the ores, and to cut
the Smelting Corporation out, and it is now seeking more
capital to turn the works into an electrolytic plant for
treating copper.
“ Mudgee ” : A good mine, but you can expect
nothing from it under present management. We know
many people who have “ been there.”
* * » *
The Carmichael-Bradford desulphurising pro-
cess is very soon to be pushed. The past six months have
been spent by the co. which now owns it in getting over
the mechanical difficulties which the discoverers
(chemists) hadn’t solved. F. H. Snow, who engineered
the Adelaide tram-concession, takes the new scheme with
him to England—what for the writer doesn’t know, and
the people interested don’t seem to know either. The
experiments with silver-lead sulphides having been satis-
factorily finished (though costs are higher than originally
estimated) the process is now being tried on gold pyrites.

Wrightville (Cobar) branch of the A.M.A., re
par (8., 8/3/’O2), states that the Miners’ Relief Fund has
not affected it in the least. The sec. writes : “We had to
rely upon the doctor’s certificate before the Miners’ Relief
Fund came into existence, and still rely upon it. If the
sick miner now loafs upon the funds, on account of re-
ceiving a few more shillings per week, the doctors are to
blame for allowing him to do so.”

The gold yield in Westralia continues to in-
crease, but the profits of gold-mining are growing much
less as the mines go deeper and working expenses
become heavier. Things have gone after this fashion
since 1896 :
v , . Proportion of gold
„ Dividends. won paid in
gold won. dividends.
1897 ..£2,564,977 £507,732 £l9 15 11 percent.
1898 .. 3,990,699 606,124 15 3 9 „
1899 .. 6,246,733 2,057,421 32 18 9 „
1900.. 6,007,610 1,394,705 23 4 . 3 „
1901 .. 7,235,652 1,091,154 15 1 7 „
The moral is that lOoz. of gold on the surface, where the
prospector can rush up excitedly and hang his hat on the
projecting knobs of pure metal, are worth more for
dividend purposes than 20oz. even a short distance below
the surface. The Westralian production has increased,
and none of its mines are at any great depth yet—com-
pared to the 3000 ft. sinkings at Bendigo, they are mere
surface scratchings—yet where £77 out of every £lOO
won went in expenses in 1899, £B5 went in 1901. This is
where the joyousness of the shareholder’s prospect comes
in. Unless something is done to reduce the tremendous
waste of money in the London offices, it looks as if there
would soon be nothing at all left for him. The fact that
an increase of nearly £1,000,000 in the gold yield since
’99 has been accompanied by a decrease of nearly
£1,000,000 in dividends accounts for a lot of market
happenings. , * «

The people who hold the supposed oil-bearing
blocks near the Warren River, W.A., are moving round
as though they meant business. Already bore plant,
capable of drilling to 3300 ft., is on the ground ; and a
Melb. co. is sending another. Also, “ Mr. Rockefeller’s
agent” is said to be on the track. W.A. Govt, geologist
has reported unfavorably : still a bore will provide more
conclusive evidence.
* * * *
From Westralia : Bewick Moreing’s opinion of
Lake View Consols is sobering. It knocks flat the
common notion that lode means ore : “ Average width of
main lode, 30ft. Within it are bodies of high-grade ore ;
but outside these bodies the value is too low ever to be
profitable.” Gf two main bodies the north runs down to
300 ft., the south to 600 ft. ; “ it is claimed that the reserve
blocked out in the bodies is 47,930 tons,” and there may
he a moderate increase of this. In the lower levels there
are “isolated ore bodies”; and vigorous-development
here is recommended, at a cost of £4OOO monthly. A
manager’s estimate of ore available in Westralia Mt. Mor-
gans has-been made. -Between 150 ft. and 400 ft., it is claimed
enough stone can be taken out, without further develop-
ment, to run 60 bead for two years. The lode, from 15ft.
to 30ft. wide, is chiefly sulphide, workable by battery and
cyanide. Averages at different levels run from 17dwt.
to 33dwt., although, as the report on the Lake View
shows, averages of lodes don’t count for much. Mines
Minister Gregory is being viciously pecked at for publish-
ing the Government Geologist’s adverse report on the
Warren oil shows. But the Minister’s position is all
right; if in the interests of promoters, favorable reports are
published (and there has never been any row about that),
in the interests of the public adverse reports should go
in. In the course of this row some nasty things have been
said, among them that the American oil people have
£200,000 to spend in flattening any local oil venture.——
Great Fingall threatens a 12,0000 z. monthly output by
May, though it will hardly do it. Bewick Moreing’s
are bustling at far-out Lawlers. A steam tram-line, seven
miles long, is being laid between the East Murchison
United ana Wahroongah South, mainly to haul the latter’s
stone to the United mill. -In the sausage-shaped bodies
in the Kalgoorlie deep levels diamond-drill prospecting is
worth little. YV here the drill entered Tetley’s lode at the
600 in the Perseverance it showed about 7ft. of 18dwt.
stuff. A few feet south the body was 3ft. and very poor ;
a few feet north there is 10ft., worth 30dwt.
• * • •
Hannan’s Star (W.A.) has leased its mill to
Hannan’s Oroya for six months, and is developing mine
on earnings of mill. Mine has already been horribly mis-
managed, but is in better hands now, and may pay its
way in future. As a div.-earning proposition it is un-
likely to shine, but will add to output of gold, and prove
an asylum of refuge for miners for long to come.
• • * *
Great Boulder South, another mine in same
category, is a Gardner-Lane feline. Was floated sixyears
ago and never justified its existence. At the annual
meeting in December, 1899, shareholders were told that
the Gardner vein, at 368 ft., was 18in. and worth 3oz. ;
still, at a recently-held meeting, the fiction of the
mine being payable was again foisted on share-
holders by the mesmeric Gardner. If six years
unsuccessful mining can’t convince scrippers of the
poor nature of their venture, they must be dense.
At meeting, the astute Frank flourished scrip for 97,000
shares before the audience to prove that he had not
deserted a sinking ship, and said they cost him over
£200,000. As Zeb. Lane and Gardner, vendors of mine,
pocketed 150,000 shares on the deal, readers can draw
their own conclusions as to the cost of Sitting Bull s hold-
ing. ......
Gardner’s adjoining show on south, Boulder
Bonanza, has just got six months’exemption, on plea of
exhaustion of funds and unpayableness of mine. All
Gardner-Lane gold - flotations, including most recent
Anchor Consols and Windsor Consols, but barring Great
Boulder Perseverance, proved absolute failures, and, had
the pair not had luck to secure Perseverance, their re-
putations and their pockets must have suffered severely.
As things are, their one gold-mine has proved their
salvation—in pocket, at least.
* • • *
Once-famous Hannan’s Reward (W.A.) was
recently amalgamated with adjoining Mt. Charlotte,
capital'now being £150,000, in £1 shares. Main North
and South lode on property valueless, but large number
of east and west veins carrying immense bodies of low-
grade ore exist on property, and could supply large
tonnages of, say, Bto lOdwt. dirt. Unless worked on a
very big scale, nothing under 10,000 tons a month, and
with thoroughly modern automatic equipment, instead
of with the antiquated hand-fed 30-head battery now in
use, mine will never pay. New working capital provided is
utterly inadequate to fulfil its mission, and mere chucking
of good money after bad. Would need £lOO,OOO at least
to develop and equip mine on Transvaal scale, so co. will
merely drag along.
• • * •
Boulder Main Reef (W.A.) still disappointing at
depth; nothing of permanent value disclosed, so far, below
600 ft. Boulder gold-chutes apparently peter out, and
become barren or patchy, at or near the Gt. Boulder
boundary, as no mine, either north or south, has come
upon really continuous runs of pay-ore, but merely patches
and dabs calculated to string people on to ruin.
* * * *
Capt. Parkes of Oroya is confident that rich Iron
Duke chute will be located shortly in North drive of 656 ft.
level. Many people on the spot are sceptical of the
chute continuing so far, but, as it has already been
proved to travel without break for above 240 ft. to Oroya
boundary, there seems no reason why it should not
continue further. No break or change of country inter-
venes to disturb the lode. Probably ere this sees print,
something definite will be known. If the thing proves
good a scramble for shares is inevitable as the gold should
provide all working capital necessary.
• * * •
Brown Hill Central (W.A.). an outsider on the
far eastern fringe of the Golden Mile, is working in a half-
hearted way with the proverbial two men and a boy on
more or less broken-up and patchy low-grade lode. Like
all poor mines in the district the B.H.C. makes up in water
what it lacks in gold. Strange freak of nature that, were
it not for wild cats supplying water, all Kalgoorlie gold
producers would be in Queer-st. Most gold producers pay
3s. to ss. per ton treated for water alone. South Kalgurli
even buys water for drilling, as there isn’t a drop in her,
and Ivanhoe buys 40,000 gals. daily from Kalgoorlie
Oonsols at 4s. per 1000 gal., and condenses half that
quantity at heavy further cost. Yet some people
argue that the Coolgardie water scheme is wholly
unnecessary. Even if cost is 10s. instead of, as estimated,
3s. 6d. per 1000 gals., it would be all mopped up with
avidity. Under existing conditions price ranges from 80s.
to 120 s. per 1000 gal. to householders according to season
of year.
J • • * *
Bonnie Charlie, Kanowna (W.A.), again under
exemption. The venture enjoys a very bad reputation,
and has been sacrificed to the greed of .an Eastern
crowd. False reports have been circulated without stint,
and false returns, like that mythical 5700 z. from 57 tons,
have gone round the world’s press without official contra-
diction. Duffer and needless appliances have been
foisted on this co., and recently a mile of second-hand
pipes were laid to a water-shaft providing 2000 gal. daily,
whereas 1500 gal. is needed to treat one ton unless the
water is returnable, for which no provision ; hence failure
is assured. London mine management is rotten enough,
but holy Adelaide can give the Big Smoke points in
helpless imbecility and working rigs.
• • * *
Apropos several things: Hoisting Lake Views
from £5 to £29 by faking the returns from 8 'oooz. to
30,0000 z. per month, and then keeping the returns and
the scrip bubbling up and down is a splendid game for
the man pulling the strings, but very bad for the mine,
and poor sport for shareholders. During the last 38
months the Lake Views have produced an average of well
over 12,0000 z. a month. Suppose this average had been
maintained throughout instead of so flagrantly see-
sawing, how much better would it have been for W.A.
and for the shareholders 1 But then the boodlers would
not have enjoyed the gamble.
* • * *
Report by engineer John Muir on the trans-
continental railway from Adelaide to Perth, Bi john
Forrest’s other gigantic wild cat, has been laid on the
table of the Senate. The great difficulty in runnin, this
railway, Muir points out, is that of water-supply. The
railway would traverse about 475 miles of country in
W.A. alone which is, to all intents and purposes, water-
less. Of this length, for the first 100 miles water could
be conserved in tanks, using the granite and iron-
stone ridges in the localities as a means of catchment,
but for the remaining 375 miles, in the limestone
country, the porous nature of the soil precluded any
hope of being able to conserve surface supplies. Two
suggestions are made for solving the difficulty—a pipe
line connecting with the Goldfields water-supply (truly a
broken reed to rely on since there is the gravest doubt as
"to whether the water will ever reach Kalgoorlie) ; or
artesian wells en route. Mr. Muir regards the prospects
of wells as very good. If this source of water supply is
available, the cost of the railway from Kalgoorlie to the
S.A. border is set down at £1,732,185. If water can’t
be got from wells the cost would be £2,435,010. The
country passed through Is suitable in some parts for pas-
toral settlement if the water difficulty can be solved. To
the sum as estimated by Mr. Muir has t<s be added the
cost of the South Australian line from the: border to the
existing railway system, making the- total at least
£3,500,000. It doesn’t look as if the .Commonwealth
could afford a luxury of that description—especially in
View of recent tariff alterations. Fofrest is the only
friend that the project is likely to have in the Cabinet. -
And Ke wants to go to London 1

H. C. Callahan, ex-manager of the Lake View,
now of Paris, set out lately In a London financial paper to
relieve Westtalian managers of the blame of mismanage*
ment, and to “charge the average board of mining
directors in London with either selfishly or ignorantly
misleading the English public by giving promising and,,
evasive statements, and trying to protect, their owii
hides.” But instead of discussing managements he dives ;
in at once to declare that “ Kalgoorlie mines have bot-
tomed ” —•' an established fact for the past 18 months,” he-
says, “ except as to the Boulder, Horseshoe, and Ivanhoe. u
And he explains: “ Except the three named, the mines
are on the eastern belt, where the gold occurs in deposits
or lenticular belts, in altered (schistose) diorite. At a
depth varying from 100 to 500 ft. the schistose diorite
changes to a hard, blocky diorite, with now and then a
few bunches of schist and calcite seams carrying a few
dwt. of gold.” Cruelly, though, the paper footnotes this
with extracts from a circular, in which the Lake View
directors gave, or purported to give, Callahan’s
expression of “ absolute conviction ” of “ the
permanence of the vein formation," and belief in
highly satisfactory divs. for a long term of years. While
it is probably true that it is harder for ore chutes to live :
in the lower levels, and while they may therefore be more t
irregular (which is a first-class reason for keeping develop- ;
ment works unusually well ahead), it is nevertheless a
fact that the Perseverance has ore at 900 ft., the Kalgurli
at 920 ft., and the L.V. Consols at 1000 ft.—quantities, of
course, not yet defined, though the Kalgurli body runs
to 30ft. wide. But, anyway, this isn’t experience peculiar
to the Kalgoorlie. eastern belt; it is common Australian
experience.

Anything may happen in clays when a row takes ;
place at the annual meeting of the freezingly-respectable
Wallaroo and Moonta Copper Co. Three years ago, :
as the end of the Moonta mine seemed near,
the directors decided to keep away the wolf by
having a “cut” for the Broken Hill lead-smelting
business. They, therefore, spent a lot of money (it isn’t
certain how much) in getting a plant, and then they
spent a lot more money in proving that there were too
many hands milking that cow already (how much they
spent that way is another of the heap of things which
this co. doesn’t tell). Now, when the works are shut up
for good, the freezingly-respectable Chaffey Baker and
other directors are charged with having recklessly
plunged into the business on no better advice than their
own copper-smelting expert could give them. The count,
of heads was against the directors ; but that was soon
reversed on an appeal to the proxies in the directors’ ;!
pockets—the other people, apparently, hadn’t thought of
coming loaded. . i f

The reported discovery of large deposits of
phosphates of excellent quality at Port Lincoln (S.A.)
will, if confirmed, be of considerable advantage to the
wheat State in providing a cheap fertiliser for its ex-
hausted lands. These phosphates are the remains of
myriad animalcule.

A syndicate is being formed in Melbourne to
“ exploit the mineral fields of the Northern Territory.”
The locale of exploitation will be over 2000 miles nearer
the South Pole, on fields where the principal metal will be
brass. ' ' !

reporting on the Tasmanian shale prospects, mainly
Adelaide-held at present. Shareholders are circularised
that the report is thoroughly satisfactory, but, for an
unspecified reason, it isn’t made public. Probably the
suppression is connected with some underwriting negoti-
ations in Melbourne.

It was at the end of October, 1899, that
Australia first went to needless war with a white
people. Within three months Providence ex-
pressed its opinion on the matter by sending the
plague here for the first time, and the plague and
the war still run side by side. The most disas.

n any years broke out in four States immediately
after the national sin was perpetrated. The
drought, which was bad before, assumed its most
aggravated form during the war period, and all
the prayers of the faithful are of no avail to lift
it. If no parson can see the obvious connection of
these episodes, then the dullness of the clerical
mind is surely incurable.
The stay-at-home militaire, who desires to
have all the social and sartorial advantages of a
commission without any of its dangers, is having a
bad time at the hands of Federal-Commandant
Hutton. More than two, who had no other
thought than to swell it at garden parties, have
been almost kicked by him to S’Africa in quest of
the glory they would have left toothers. It has
been “ Curly’s ’’ pleasant custom on interrogating
any officer to ask “ Whom did you go to the war
with ? ” In those cases, where the answer was “ I
haven’t been, sir,” the General would look
seriously at the featherbed swaddy for a few
seconds and then remark blandly: “There will
be another Commonwealth contingent forming
shortly, so you will be able to volunteer.” This
little plan might work harshly in the case of
officers with conscientious objections to farm-
burning. But, alas, though you may find such in
England, even up to the rank of Field-Marshal,
they don’t seem to grow but herb.
*. » » *
There have been some narrow escapes
from revolution in Auckland owing to all the
cohentingents being mobilised and dispatched
from other M.L. cities. Premier Seddon began
to see a shadowy writing on the wall, and averted
the impending doom by letting Auckland have the
North Island battalion of the Ninth Cohentingent
all to itself. The camp was pitched somewhere
out Onehunga way, and passengers on that line
had a rough time. Young fellows, when taking
their best girls out up the line, would suddenly
find the carriage invaded by a uniformed, swag-
gering crowd of blaspheming Boer stickers, who
hugged the girls with a joyful impartiality and
dealt out stoush to any civilian who remonstrated.
Complaints to the police merely resulted in the
civilians being arrested and brought up before the
courts for insulting the King’s uniform, and, even
if they escaped fine, they had to pay the costs of
their defence, after having had their best girls
hugged promiscuously without the slightest satis-
faction. Altogether, Auckland became very
sorry it got any cohentingent at all. The only
bright spot in the spectacle was the common sense
of the police, who, realising that to tackle the
ruffianly soldiers meant black eyes, torn clothes
and damaged ears, promptly jugged the inoffen-
sive civilians. Yet, when these gentry get to
S’Africa the Boer women and girls will, of course,
be as safe in their tender hands as though they
dwelt in castles, with mailed guards sleeping on
their door-mats; and Auckland will be as ready
as any other place to repudiate any German
aspersion to the effect that their behaviour towards
women isn’t that of perfect gentlemen.
An officer of the 3rd battalion of the
Commonwealth contingent describing a pet
private:
He’s not a bad sort. If there’s anything I take a fancy
to he steals it for me.

“Stamper”: Heard a returned swaddy
one night relating his S’African experiences.
“Did yer ever rook a stiff, Joey ? ” asked one of
the listeners. “ Wot! go through the dead ’uns?
Rather? But we was fair beat at first —could
iiever find any think on ’em, till we noticed that
their pants above the knee and coat-sleeve above
the elbow uster be cut open, and blow me if it
wasn’t the Tommies put us up to it after all. So,
after that, we always rushed them, and I can tell
yer there uster be a scramble at times over likely-
looking ones—to see who could cut the pants and
coat quickest. An’, by ghost! they nearly all had
some stuff stowed away in the belts fixed round
their knebs or arms.”
• * » *
The almost forgotten bushranging industry
has made a fresh start in Queensland. The
persons who are the cause of the present
trouble, are described as a family with an old-
established cow-stealing connection in the Mara-
noa district. The men who annex the land that
is underneath the cow always look with reproba-
tion on those who annex the cow that is on top of
the land, and adjacent capitalists were not
friendly with this family. A squatter named
Dalke and a policeman rode a long distance
recently to speak to some of them on the usual
subject of cows, and both have disappeared. It is
known that there was some shooting, and a pool
of blood was found, also two pairs of spurs. That
is all so far, but it is assumed that the cow-stealers
have taken to the bush in the good old style, and
the police are raising great clouds of dust all over
the country in pursuit. It is a miserable region
to go bushranging in—little to eat and not much
to drink, and hardly anything to steal; and it
would pay almost as well to build a bark ketch
and become a pirate on the dry bed of the Paroo
River.
* * * *
Overcrowding on Victorian railways lead-
ing, to and from suburban resorts on holidays
staggers the capacity of even the sardine simile.
One train corning from Brighton on Easter Mon-
day was so tightly packed that in two carriages the
people could not leave when the doors were
opened at Elinders-street, and had to be dug out,
like figs out of a box, by strenuous porters. On
St. Kilda line a boy was hurt through being
“ crushed ” off his feet and having his head forced
into the lamp aperture.
Frederick Keleher, an unsophisticated
bushman, visited Sydney, and ere going to bed at
night blew out the. gas instead of turning it off.
He was found moribund and soon expired. The
poor fellow’s fate recalls a story suggesting the
advantages of electricity in such cases, “I
couldn’t blow out the light, ” explained thebushman
to his mate—“they’d put it in a blanky bottle.”

A dreamy and somewhat weak-sighted St.
Kilda (Vic.) man, who once narrowly escaped a
big difficulty through the usual sweet gazelle, and
is now extremely cautious, got into an empty
carriage at Flinders-st, the other day. Just as
the train was starting, a smart young female
entered and greeted him affectionately. The
veteran promptly skedaddled, although the train
was just moving, and scrambled into the next
compartment. His inward congratulations on a
“ narrow escape ” were somewhat damped by the
subsequent discovery that the lady was his own
grand-daughter. It is she who tells the story.
* * * *
Which looks like a good idea
“ J.” : Up Robertson (N.S.W.) way recently was much
struck with the splendid crop of fruit on the blackberry
vines, which cover tens of acres in that district. Heard
that a local storekeeper had had to refuse an order from a
Sydney jam-firm for 25 tons, as pickers could not be got.
As the work is light, and at the price offered for picking
(3s. per kerosene-tin full), the minimum daily 7s. can
easily be earned, why not have an annual summer out-
ing for the aged and infirm unemployed ?
* * • *
Something that reminds one of a few bad
old countries is taking place at Broken Hill. A
man who owned, or was reputed to own, many
workmen’s cottages ordered a bailiff’s sale of a
tenant’s goods ; and, whether he knew it or not,
the tenant happened to have been sick —through
lead-poisoning. An angry little crovyd gathered
at the sale ; and within a month three of the cot-
tages were mysteriously burned at different times,
and equally mysterious warnings reached the
people living in the others to shift if they didn’t
want to be roasted. A woman has now been
arrested and charged with arson.
♦*m - m
Judge Rogers, at Quarter Sessions, com-
plained that Hyde Park, Sydney, was given over
chiefly to people who “ lolled on the grass”—he
could not understand why the reserve should not
be given up for public purposes— i.e., for the erec-
tion of a noisy, smoky railway-station. Judge
Rogers lives at Burwood, in his own park, so to
say. Let his Honor live in Kent or Sussex street,
and he will not advocate the abolition of Hyde
Park. The Judge’s complaint should have been
directed to the night-hawks who prowl the park
for. lack of a strong park police patrol and 'electric
lighting.
• • • *
A highly-placed Sydney medico makes
the entirely brilliant suggestion that, as an
improvement to; Sydney’s water-supply, the
old Botany swamps should be utilised as
an emergency reservoir. These swamps are
on _ the borders of a thickly-populated
district, which contains most of the tan-
neries, fellmongeries, bone-mills, wool-scours
and so on of Sydney. Also, the district has a
flourishing sewage-farm, and the swamps are
generally used for watering cattle and swimming
dogs.

Rumors of a case of leprosy existing in a
fashionable Sydney suburb persist in spreading.
It is believed the Health Department accepts the
restraint of mere Seclusion, where the house is
large, but the conditions are too vague to be
wholly satisfactory. Besides, domestics have to
be considered. ( : .

The eternal baby-that-isn’t-wanted made
a new appearance in a Ballarat court t’other day.
The mother had left her husband through his
cruelty, it was said, and had just reached the
Benevolent Asylum in time to escape being
confined in the street. The superintendent
wanted the infant—then but two days old—made
a ward of State; the bench declined and told him
to find the father and make him pay for its keep.
Thereupon the superintendent calmly deposited
the small derelict on the floor of the court and
went about his business, and a flabbergasted bench
was left with an unclaimed infant on its hands.
« • * •
At Coronation time the cost of living in
London will be more than doubled. The terms
at such first-class hotels as the Cecil will be, at the
very lowest, £2 per diem ; and these exaggerated
prices will probably be maintained for about a
fortnight. It will be only silkworms that can
crawl there.
• • * *
The death of Andrew Clarke gives Premier
Peacock the excuse he badly wanted for sending
Works Minister M‘Culloch on a Coronation jaunt.
,M‘Culloch has been going for many reasons, all
advanced feelingly within the last three months,
but the reasons provoked such public snorts that
they were always indignantly repudiated next
morning. Now M'Culloch goes to “reorganise”
the Agent-General’s office—and have a good time
at the people’s expense. M‘Culloch will be the
third “thorough business man” sent ’Ome to
reorganise.the A.-G. office. Sinclair was the last.
M 'Culloch is going to reorganise him. In a month
or two the Govt, will probably be sending along
another expensive business person to reprganise
M'Culloch.

“Grease the fab hog!” Healthy-looking
bonuses have been given to those Victorian public
servants who were engaged in painting Melbourne
red for the little Duke, including one of £250 to
Jenkins of the Beard, but nary a coin was
given to those “ outside ” assistants of the said
Jenkins—four clerks and a lady typist—who were
kept going until 11 p.m. every night of their
engagement, without any overtime allowance.
• • * *
Certain yellow heathen of Coburg (Vic.)
have temporarily reduced the law to a state of
chaos. They had been toiling on Sunday (growing
vegetables, not putting up arches for a Duke), and
as Charles ll.—of sainted memory—disapproved
of that sort of thing, no less than 25 of them were
summoned. The Chows unanimously declined to
take any interest in the matter, and went to
market as usual. They were fined ss. apiece, and
haven’t paid. The alternative provided in default
of payment is two hours in the stocks, and even
the antiquated penal system of Victoria doesn’t
possess those articles.
• • * *
People who profess to have reliable in-
formation about the state of the rolling-stock
on the Vic. railways declare that serious break-
downs are likely to happen shortly as the
result of the tinkering policy adopted of late.
“ Enough for the day is the deficit thereof ” has
undoubtedly been a guiding principle in Vic.
railway management for the past 10 years.

* * * *
The Dowie cult, in Melbourne, is advanc
ins bv leaps and bounds. Dowie’s emissaries
keep on at the Victoria Hall, Bourke-street,
where they pound away three times every Sunday,
and they have started healing as well as souling.
Prodigious has been the hallelujahismg over the
secession of the Booth-Clibboros from Boothism to
Dowieism. The Dowie gang is also doing well at
Adelaide, where “Zion” has now 200 adherents.
Services are held on Sundays, a Yankee agent of
the man with the Purple Wings preaching. There
is talk soon of a newspaper to help Dowieism in
Australia; so far most recruits have come from the
Salvation Army, the Spiritualists, and others
whose religious views had before been somewhat
of the nature of a malady.
* * * •
At Albert Park, Melb., the other day, a
squad of infantry-men on parade came into collision
with a battery of field artillery in the course of
manoeuvres, and a squabble ensued, resulting in
an unfortunate horse being jabbed in the stomach
with a bayonet. The jealousy which always exists
between two sets of soldiers competing for the
admiration of the same supply of nursery-maids
seems to have been at the bottom of the encoun-
ter. If you give a man a weapon to stick into
things, he will eventually find someone to stick it
into, for some reason or none. In this case, it
was only a horse, but it might as easily have been
an ass.
A Victorian station is manned by a rather
mixed staff, amongst the number being one Jew,
two Germans, one Chow (cook), one Jap, and last
but not least, a full-blooded Boer. The latter, it
is said, is the best man on the station, and when
he hinted to the manager some
time ago about going home to
give his countrymen a
hand, his wages were
raised.
Melbourne Age, which so severely bully-
ragged Trenwith for his use of the luxurious car
which Yio. railway department built to accommo-
date the small York, might inquire why Com-
missioner Fitzpatrick does not put that beauti-
fully-upholstered concern into common everyday
use, as promised when it was being fitted up.
The car is at present reserved chiefly for the
visiting politicians, and is sent up regularly every
Tuesday to Albury to bring down N.S.W. and
Queensland M.’sP., returning the following day
for stragglers, and frequently going back to Mel-
bourne empty.
» * * •
Mysterious are the ways of Sydney’s law-
givers. While every place of amusement in the
city is compelled to close its doors on Good Friday
(unless the “sanctity” of the day is dodged by
giving what is humorously termed a “sacred and
classical concert”) the “Royal Agricultural Society
of N.S.W.’s ” Grand Commonwealth Exhibition —•
less magniloquently known as the “ Agricultural
Show ” —is in full swing. High-falutin talk about
giving an impetus to pastoral and agricultural pur-
suits notwithstanding, this affair is simply a big
“show,” devoted to the vulgar gathering of
shekels, as witness the evening “ musical enter-
tainments.” Further, although under divers pains
and penalties, no one may partake of liquor within
a N.S.W. public-house on Good Friday, every
facility is afforded for getting “drunk on the pre-
mises” at the R. A.S.N.S.W.G.C.E. The way-
back farmers who come to Sydney and call at
the Agricultural Show for enlightenment are
sadly disappointed. It is a tradition that there
are some pumpkins and potatoes shown some-
where, but the show is mostly drapers’shops, wine-
booths, beer-bars, spieling side-shows, horse-
racing, tea exhibitions, and machinery. It is a
humorous kind of affair to get a State subsidy for
its services to the farming and pastoral industry.
* * * *
The police are losing bits of the N.S.W.
Liquor Act every day now. An inspector who
prosecuted for a “ sing-song ” in a Newtown pub.
has been ignominiously routed, the magistrate,
Mr. Isaacs, stating that he himself lately sang a
song in a Sydney hotel, and was encored by the
Minister for Justice. Barrister Garland, defend-
ing the publican, said he attended a Bar dinner
in a hotel a few weeks ago, when Judges and bar-
risters sang without any interference. The Liquor
Act will soon be too holey for use.

Re mixed bathing at Little Coogee (Syd-
ney) which somewhat shocked the virtuous
O’Sullivan and to a much greater extent the
sensitive and shrinking Paddy Crick —mr the
cities of Continental Europe, of whose im-
morality we hear so much from the pietists who
strive to make a horror of the Sydney Sunday, no
man would be permitted to enter the water in any
public swimming-bath without bathing-drawers.
There is apparently no such rule here. At Coogee
there are basins in hollows in the rocks which, on
any warm afternoon, are crowded with men and

Enoggera reservoir, the. chief water-
supplier of Brisbane, is in a lovely condition.
Alleged that it hasn’t been properly cleaned-out
for 25 years. The statement that a dead Chiiia-
man was found floating in the dam, however, is 3

tery, and the doctors and undertakers there are
doing all right.
Now that Sydney’s “ City Fathers ” have
compelled householders to furnish themselves
with covered garbage-boxes—which should have
been done years ago —they may possibly find time
to attend to their own uncleanliness and procure
covered garbage-carts. At present, they seem to
consider a patch of foul, ragged sacking all that s
wanted.

One Sydney eating-house, with many
waitresses, carries on an additional business to that
of catering, and is a most dangerous den. It is in
the very heart of the city and well known to the
police, but is allowed to continue its existence^— a
sorry trap for many a country girl or visiting
bushman.
• »
Sydney D.T. t’other day printed, with
illustration, a device for electrocuting the bubonio
rat. All firms using electricity could, it said,
have a wire connected with a trap, so arranged
that a rat seizing the bait would get a shock
sufficient to kill. A Sydney electrician once fixed
up a similar trap, but with a much stronger
voltage than would be available in an ordinary
lighting service, Soon a rat came along, and,
after due caution, went for the bait. There was a
shrill squeak, the rat jumped feet into the air, and
then —scurried for his hole. The beggar must
have told all the others, for although many were
seen running round the trap not one would 1 touch
itafterwards. The voltage necessary to kill a human
being, even with special appliances for the proper
transmission of the current, is enormous; . how
much stronger would it need to be in the case of a
rat covered with fur and dirt, both excellent non-
conductors ?

Dr. Harston, who has had much plague-
experience in Hongkong, and who is now in
Sydney, strongly emphasises the importance, as a
precaution, of swathing the legs “ putty ’’-fashion,
especially in the case of persons _ engaged in
cleansing operations. He says that in most cases
of inoculation by flea the spot selected is between
ankle and knee, and cites the significant fact,
that amongst the soldiers who cleaned up the
city of Hongkong many cases of plague occurred

case was recorded after they had been made to wear

Harston also suggests, should be treated with a
strong antiseptic ; but the chances are that, just
because these precautions are so simple and easily
carried out, they will continue to be neglected.
People don’t appreciate the facile.
