A WOMAN’S LETTER.
M,Dm. 4 »- Sydney, April 8, 1902.
Rain-cloaks, umbrellas, underskirts, open-work
stockings and the most expensive and unservice-
No, it’s not an order-list; it’s a
Band wick memory of the last day’s race-meeting’.
Rain fell in torrents. As one of the jockeys
observed: “It was terrible sloppy overhead ! ”
Rut the meeting was a crowded one. All the
well-known women were present, and if they got
no chance of showing their gowns the afternoon
was one long opportunity to display their shoes
and underskirts. Not, one bad-weather foot-
sheath trod the sodden lawn or waded through
the bridal-veil form ition of water that poured
down the asphalte paths. They were all of the
pump kind, or the fancy shoe in divers fancies.
Some women have twenty pairs of shoes, yet not
one in which they could walk half-a-mile with
ease. As for the artistically-ventilated hose, like
the shoes, prettiness, not utility, is desired. You
can pay as high as £25 per pair for your stockings
now, if you like ;or have it paid for you—if you
like. A Sydney shop has a sample pair at 18
guineas, but no one seems desirous of footing the
bill or them. They are hand-painted with
violets, pink roses and lilies-of-t-he-valley, and, of
course you can’t wash them, and the frescoing
might not stand spraying with formalin—so it is
better not to buy them till the plague is off. The
shopman who showed me the precious pair told
me that I could sew on the jewels for myself.”
? U B rom the depth to which it
had fallen and echoed “ jewels ! ” and he exhibited
tmy paste diamonds which were to be sewn on to
thepetals of the violets scattered over the instep.
Violets gemmed with dew.”
But about those underskirts. They were m all
the primary, secondary, and tertiary colors, but
only in a few artistic instances did they match the
wearer s dress. Mr ;. Alison’s was of brown silk
—her frock was brown. Mrs. A. G. Macarthur’s
brown velvet gown displayed a scarlet underdress.
Brown gowns were plentiful, brown birds on hats,
brown furs. Mayoress Hughes (of Sydnev) wore
her reception dress of the day previous and brown
sables. Mrs. Pfaipps-Hornby, the American wife
or the captain of the Pylades, and reputed pos-
sessor of barrels of money, was sensibly attired in
a very coarse navy-blue serge, the kind her
nusband s crew might wear on its day off, with a
storm collar and a magenta hat with a brown
outcrop.
girl with hair to match wore a cape of
Canadia i fox, which, you know, is of a sandy-red
color, like an Irish setter’s coat. At a distance
it looked remarkable, as if her own hair was
draped in a mat around her. Several hats with
sea-gulls upon them were abroad ; the milliners
had faxed the birds with an effort to make them
loox as if taey were enjoying life on the ocean
wave instead of being dead on a hat.
Mayoress Mrs. Tom Hughes held her first
official reception in the Town Hall last week. The
lady can iay the honor to her soul, and keep it
there till some succeeding Mayoress wrests it
from her, that she had the biggest day of its kind
! m record. Three thousand callers shook her
hand, nibbled and sipped at her refreshments, and
overflowed the nail and into the balconies and
listened to the band. It was a well-dressed
assemblage, too, and its collective complexion
looked so good that one sought the cause—and
found it m the light, which filtered through pink
shades. An innovation—these shades—for which
a> were truly thankful. Also, it didn’t look at
all the assemblage that carries off the caterer’s
spoons and glasses—just as mementoes of a happy
dar, you know. Such sentimentalism has been
common at previous first-day Mayoral receptions,
but on this occasion the caterer is not lamenting.
The Mayor, in his robes of scarlet and ermine,
and lown-Clerk Nesbitt, in a barrister’s wig and
gown, and the Mayor’s big brother with his aged
hair and radiant young face, stood around alert to
take visitors off the hostess’s hands, if they showed
symptoms of settling down for a prolonged chat
Mr. Layton announced 8000, and no one com-
plained of the way her name was pronounced or
shortened. Women who had a double-barrelled
name heard both barrels discharged at the hostess
and shiny-faced youths of 19 (there were ever so
many 01 these) were presented as if they were over
30 and had big banking balances. The Hughes
clan is a pretty strong one, and it filled the
Mayoral enclosure, which also seated the
Mayoress’s venerable mother and kept out her
grandsons, one of whom irreverently wore the
Mayor’s cocked hat. Mrs. Hughes’ gown was
mousseline de soie, applique in a lace design over
white silk, a toque of blue and white panne with
The crush was too dense to see callers’
gowns, but the hats viewed from the balcony
made a fine study of broken color. There was
one girl there with one curl falling from behind
one ear, a fashion of the present Queen’s early
pnncesshood, with a revival of which English
papers have threatened us. It was a nice curl, but
you could see by the weareFs agonised expression
of face that it weighed like Ossa on her conscious
soul.
At a local private dance of last week Mrs.
Wills-Allen and Miss Irene Marks wore the
Alexandra curl. Worst of it is that you won’t
get the credit of owning the switch save by right
of purchase.
That idea of the syndicate-hostess, started some
three years ago, is good and economical. A com-
bine of 38 hostess-power entertained 400 guests at
a dance at Paddington Town Hall last week ;
and, although the supper and decorations were
good, the party cannot have lessened the assets of
the givers to any remarkable degree. The 38, who
each invited a certain number of guests, and wore
badges of pink and green, were mostly in the pure
merino style, with a sprinkling of the wives of
fashionable physicians and K.C’s. Twelve debu-
tantes were at this dance added to our over-girled
society set—mostly pretty girls ; but not one of
them came up to the Scotch steamshipman’s
daughter who was present.
The widow’s might. Report tells of the engage-
ment of Mrs. Willie Mitchell to a Captain Fowl;
(awful name !), an English man o’ war, who is
said to be “a bit elderly.” Time was when Mrs.
Mitchell, her gowns, her hats, her style and con-
tempt of convention, were the chief topics of Syd-
ney small talk. Well, a woman with her figure
and drooping eyelids need not be a relict a week
longer than she wishes, and the gallant Fowle will
no doubt prove a kind bird to the smart widow’s
growing and lively boys.
Dr. Clubbe and his white, gold and pink
Mittagong bride were quietly married last Satur-
day at St. Peter’s in Melbourne. Next medical
wedding will be Dr. Tidswell’s on the 17th.
Amongst the passengers outward bound by the
Sonorpa last week was Miss Millie Coffee, a
daughter of the local speculative American,
Frank Coffee of Chatswood. Miss Coffee is a
violiniste of promise, and, after visiting American
kin, goes to some foreign centre where they
worship the Stradivarius, to be trained in the cult
and fulfil her promise. Another outgoer was Dr.
Power of College-street, Sydney, whose wife, a
daughter of the late Mr. W. W. Wardell, designer
of St. Mary’s Cathedral, died awhile ago.
Mrs. C. Martell and her pupils’ elocutionary
recital brought a big audience of fashionable folic
to the Centenary Hall last week. The elocutionist,
who looked very picturesque and girlish, with a
pile of flaxen hair and a pink frock, has some
clever pupils amongst the number who recited
and acted on Thursday night. The programme
was unhackneyed, and Mrs. Martell’s own con-
tributions to it, especially the epilogue to “As
You Like It ” were flatteringly received.”
Sydney women are just now the despair of
their manicurists. The craze for disinfection in
consequence of the plague outbreak leads to the
use of carbolic soaps, to washing witli lysol and
formalin solutions, and so on —all deadly to a soft
skin. After a little formalin, carbolic, or lysol
treatment, the blandest of paws become harsh and
hard. Shaking hands with a dainty hostess now
is like patting a shark on the fin.
Persons who possess specimens of the new
coinage had better not spend them on molasses,
candles and coals, as the issue is likely to be
stopped and they will become curiosities. Ed-
wardus instead, of Eduardus: that W in the
Nmg’s name grits the teeth of cultured England,
and Someone Big has been dismissed over the
coster Latin.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
Amy Castles went away a bashful, brown-
faced, bushy-haired chit, wearing the shortish
skirts and flat hat favored by Bendigo girls till
they attain ‘Sweet and Twenty.’ She has come
back a nice little woman, pleasing to look at,
thanks to glowing cheeks, honest brown eyes,
wavy dark hair, parted demurely in the middle,
and good-humored smile, revealing splendid
teeth. Pity she’s, such a dot of a thing in height,
but sweeping train, steep heels, and piled hair
will add to her apparent height when the time
comes to discard the aspect of little Miss Bendigo,
Home-sweet-homing,’ heart and soul, for that of
a prentice prima donna, in training for the Melba
stakes. As matters stand, the Castles’ figure,
brief though it is, has a finished, pocket-woman-of-
the-world air, thanks to the long, smooth waist of
its owner’s Parisian frock.
“The lesson conveyed by Miss Castles’ tight
frocks should be laid to heart by all short women.
Bocal dressmakers are too fond of decoying
pocicet-charmers into Russian blouses, etc. ‘some-
thing fussy to make more of you, Miss.’ The
little woman, bless her heart ! should aim at
resemolmg a dainty, duodecimo edition, not a
dwarfed, dropsical folio.

“Amy Castles was present on Easter Satur-
day night, at ‘A Runaway Girl.’ Between the
acts a frenzied admirer in the gallery called
aloud for ‘ three cheers for Amy Castles,’ winch
everybody arose and gave, Amy herself rising in
the manager’s box, in a pretty, spangly, white
satin frock, mostly hidden in an ugly, useful-
looking grey cloak, her hair arranged in Madonna
bands, much after the style of Fedora Moore’s
inky chevelure on the stage. And the band
played ‘No place like home!’ Judging by the
way it is insisted on as a great virtue in Amy that
she returned home so soon (though, by the way,
Australians are flocking away from their home
just now by shiploads), people seem to be regarding
that popular sentiment, ‘There’s no place like
home,’ as the Red King in ‘ Wonderland ’ did
ham sandwiches. ‘ Nothing like a ham sandwich
when you’re faint! ’ ‘Wouldn’t water be better ?’
asked Alice. ‘I didn’t say there was nothing
better. I said there was nothing like it ! ’
“ Bookfellows, artists and others put in a brisk,
brushed-up appearance at the literary, musical
and circumlocutory shivoo at the Gallery, in
Swanston-street, last Wednesday evening. The
Commonwealth Exes were billed for appearance,
but his Lordship stayed away and the taciturn
Countess, with A.D. C. Duff in tow, moved slowly,
like a big threatening iceberg, around the assem-
blage. President Langton and brother-librarians,
chilled at the very proximity of the Polar attrac-
tion, marshalled the Excellency in with awkward
gait and disconcerted amenities. The slim Earl
would have eased matters wonderfully by his
presence, but, as he was missing, that stiffened
little band of martyrs steered for the platform
and docked the Countess on a State chair in
solitary dignity. They perched themselves around
like so many insulators about a telegraph-post.
The bare back of Mercury in sculptured dignity
rose before the eyes of the Excellency and the
respectable clan of learned brothers. The assem-
bled quests faced Mercury’s front and the dais.
This was a source of comfort to the inartistic, who
see sin in plaster and marble nudities.
“ The Countess was a figure of splendor at this
library junket. She wore black, a severe regal
•black, with a hard handsome bodice border of
coruscating silver applique, and an admission of
coquetry (such is the power of Eve) in a single
blush-pink rose in the left flank of the corsage.
There were many diamonds in the neat veiled-in
black coiffure, and about the unbending neck. A
novelty appeared in a neck band of ‘ Hopetoun ’
Pink ribbon passing through small square hurdles
of diamonds. The ceremony of opening the Loan
Collection of musty and blue-mouldy curios fell to
the Countess, and she did it by rising from her
chair, moving her lips and sitting down again. Per-
haps the Mercury who stood in his proximate cold
altitude heard the words—none in the gathering
did.
“ All the dressing at the conversazione ran to
black and pink, A la Countess, among the dames,
and white and red among those of tenderer years.
There was a wealth and warmth of blonde 'tones
and healthfullness about the second star of the
social realm that night—Lady Hutton. She
moved about with Miss Duff, viewing canvasses
and etchings of the old gallery collection like a
stately ship—probably, too, sighing for the touch
of the Commandant’s hand, and the sound of the
Commandant’s voice—just then being put to some
j n Sydney. A weighty and well-built gown
of black tucked glac& silk was rich enough to pro-
duce a thick layer of cream on the bodice top. It
was a lace and chiffon fold. Diamonds acted as
electric lights on a dark locality. They blazed on
the black, the cream, the neck, the hair. Lady
Hutton is pleasant; her smile comes and goes not
by instigation, but as if evolved from a state of
natural contentment. Miss Duff repeated the
popular tones, black and pink.
“Everyone carried away from the Gallery en-
tertainment copies of prints produced by a creak-
ing rickety old press, treasured by the trustees as
the first ever used in Victoria. They also bore

curios, and a plenteous supper-table. The Library
trustees did things up to the exalted Exes, stan-
dard. Women folk are always glad of the Hope-
touns’ presence; it ensures such vastly superior
refreshments. Life, from our point of view, after

“Gov. Clarke, with supercilious chin in the air,
and hands clasped behind, delivered a dissertation
on horticulture in general, and the smiling cot-
tage-gardens of Victoria in particular, on Thursday
last, when he was fairly wound up, for the opening
of the Flower and Vegetable Show at the Town
Hall. Flower faddists smiled happily as the
grey Governor (grey in years, dress, and turn of
mind) discoursed on the autumn-tinted subject.
His words come easily, but they are very simple
words. He does not search for an equivalent when
about to repeat himself, nor does he take any of
those picturesque, metaphorical bends that give
so much grace to the Waxellency’s river of speech,
but he gets to the end with easy side-stroke—-
neither breathless nor inflated. Our Gov.
suffers from over. self-consciousness. He sits
down with painstaking primness, then
finds his knees too close together, and
separates them ; then finds his coat-tails in
need of adjustment; then his hands have to be
arranged differently, and so on. Small matters
iike these are but points denoting the mental
Gov. Clarke’s dame is also a grey personage—
very much harmonising with her lord. She never
appears in anything but a grey blend, sometimes
grey and black, again grey and white, but always
grey. At this flower show she sat smiling (in a
grey outfit, a black chiffon and tulle ruff, a steel-
grey toque, and a grey halo of hair) all through
the opening harangue. Her smiling pleasantness
is perhaps a little too prim, too circum-
scribed One would like the wide smile
TO told up again sometimes, or unfold into
a oroad laugh. But she is no end of a help to her
h© sits down, after speechifying she
bends forward with a private word and snnle— a
rescuing hand it is when the proud little man’s
seJt-consciousness is heavy upon him.

last sprint of ‘The Amazons’ at the Princess’s.
(April 4). The George perhaps enjoyed the play
m his dreams—for tie slept whenever anything

bright as a button ’ When intervals arrived, and

across G;eorgie-Porgie spending his holidays with

“A difficulty has arisen in connection with the
reception to a certain far-famed star by her old
class-mates. It appears that the said star was a
very boisterous, devil-may-care young person, and
one wild and desperate deed (not parting her hair
properly, or something of that sort) led to her
leaving college. Now, as it is against the rules of
the select seminary to honor a recalcitrant pupil
in any way after she has been at extreme logger-
heads with the authorities, how is it possible, ask
a few sticklers for conservative principles, to
ignore, even after this long period, the school
rules and offer incense and laurel leaves to the
offender ? So the matter hangs while those in the
highways and bye-ways are sniggering softly at
the dilemma and wondering by what Pecksniffian
loophole the organisers can escape the infringe-
ment of their own inviolate rule, and swing the
censer with the rest of the world.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Cecil Rhodes’ last words have be n grossly
misrepresented. What he really did say was:
“So many done—so many more to do.”
Died, lately, at Adelaide, aged 71, “ Yallam
Jaeky,” the last of the Penola tribe of blacks. It
was Jacky yho first saw the Tantanoola tiger.
The bunch of men known as the framers
of the Victorian Constitution Act is now extinct,
Agent-General Andrew Clarke being the last of
them. .
That was really modest of Cecil Rhodes to
provide that the place of his own interment should
be the nucleus of a cemetery for the great men of
S’Africa.
Dan O’Connor on church bazaars :
Bazaars are good institutions. Not only is there non-
felonious robbery under most lovely arms, but marriages
usually follow.
Railway-Commissioner Gray (Q.) retires
on a six months’ leave with full pay and an after-
pension of £666—which is the number of the
Beast of Revelation.
The death of Agent - General Andrew
Carke makes Charles Gavan Duffy doyen of Vic.
iiensioners. Clarke went up for £BOO in 1857 ;
Fifty, for £lOOO in 1863.
One harmless-looking Sydney financier
taps the banks for the monthly salaries of no less
than seven M’s.L.A. as soon as the instalments
are entered to their credit.
Is it quite the thing for Major Randall
Carey to act as President of Sydney Daily Tele-
graph, which adversely criticises the conduct of
hissuperior officer, Federal Commandant Hutton ?
Governor Havelock is expected shortly to
amend his proclamation formula to read : “ Gover-
nor and Commander-in-Chief in and over the State
of Tattersall and his dependents.”
The imprisoned editor of Gulgong Adver-
tiser states that of the six occupants of the
debtors’ prison at Darlinghurst, no less than three
are J.’sP. The editor is one of the three.

logical conclu-

that the office

A death at the house of Mr. Sweetapple, a
robbery from the house of Mr. Redapple, and a
birth at the house of Mr. Crabtree—all being
residents of Carlton (Melb.); and advts. relative
thereto appeared in file daily paper on the same

It is not generally known that Inspector-
General Fosberv, of N.S.W. police, graduated in
the 'Victorian force, which he joined in ’53 as a
mounted cadet. He was afterwards chief clerk in
the Vic. department.
Sydney’s Mayor Hughes deserves credit
for being so far (The Bulletin grows cautious)
about the most energetic and progressive civic
official the city has yet had ; he almost makes one
think that there is hope for an alderman.
William Ohandos Wall, formerly member
for Rylstone, N.S.W., has invented a clothes-
washing machine which he exhibited at the Syd-
ney Easter Show. Mr. Wall is probably the first
politician to wash dirty linen by machinery.
Australian Jingo press disclaims Handcock,
executed in S’Africa, as an Australian. Well, he
was born at Winburndale, near Peel, Bathurst
(N.S. Wales), the ground landlords being the ex-
tremely Jingo Suttor family. Mr. Handcock had
his training for court-martial work as a black-
smith.
The lately-deceased Andrew Clarke, Vic.
Agent-General, was the only son of an Australian
Governor who rose to Ministerial rank in Aus-
tralian politics. His father was Westralian
Governor in 1846. The late Agent-General was
member of the Haines Administration in 1856,
and almost became Premier of Victoria a little
later. ’
The Duke of Leinster narrowly
being fined £lOO in Adelaide last week. He
brought a case of grapes, p icked in vine-leaves,
by the express from Victoria. The Customs
officers confiscated and burnt the fruit, but let the
Duke off the penalty on account of his ignorance
of the law. 8. A. has no phylloxera and does not
want to get any.
Acting-Justice Pring, who sentenced
Lukins to death for the Dulwich Hill (Sydney)
tragedy, may be a practising barrister to-morrow,
a District Court Acting-Judge the day after, and
the day after again an Acting-Supreme Court
Judge. Years ago this rotatory arrangement was
reprobated in N.S.W. Parliament as a vicious
playing with life and death.
Arthur Edwards, organiser of M.L.
Liberal and Labor Federation, just dead, was an
Oxford man, and held a position as a schoolmaters
for 16 years. He was a born organiser, and acted
in that capacity for the Primrose League ere
leaving England. The huge federation, which is
Seddon’s great stand-by, was almost entirely
built up through Edwards’ zeal.
It has long been related of a much-loved,
foreign-born member of an Australian legislature
that he has been seen wearing a tall hat and a
long black coat and trudging behind a plough
drawn by his wife and a cow. The yarn has
become classic, but is probably untrue and
founded on the fact -that the wives of German
farmers often do much field-work.
As to Admiral Moraut’s cabled disavowal
of Lieut. Morant’s paternity, The Bulletin has
received several letters attempting to refute the
Admiral’s statement. But after all what does it
matter? What advantage to anybody if the body
of the son is hung around the neck of one
particular old man ? Morant has been executed.
Is it necessary to also execute his family ?
The farewelling Bishop Goe has given
Melbourne some 15 years of his life. His imme-
diate predecessor, Dr. Moorhouse, gave only ten ;
but Perry, the first Bishop, gave 29. Australia
has had several C E. bishops with long ruling
records. Broughton, the first, reigned for 17
years ; Barker, 28 ; Turner (Grafton), 24 ; Thomas
(Goulburn), 29 ; Tyrell, 32; and Short, 34.
Millionaire Squatter “ Sam ” McCaugliey’s
pet hobby is engineering—sheep-breeding is only
his business. On all his stations there are evidences
of his fad, in well-constructed dams and canals.
He has also invented several useful pastoral
appliances. At Coonong, the big squatter has a
splendidly fitted workshop where he spends his
spare time blacksmithing and carpentering.
N.S.W. Justice M. H. Stephen flatly says
he intends to resign on pension, having now
served his full 15 years. Mr. Stephen, as a pen-
sioner, will doubtless live practically for ever, as
did his father, Sir Alfred, who did not pass out
until long after 90. Justice M- H. Stephen is a
frisky young gentlemen of about 73, who lately
married a charming lady 50 years his junior.
Chief Justice Way is just the same age as
the State in which he resides : on Friday (April
11) he was 66. Gavan Duffy, the knighted “ rebel
to the backbone and spinal marrow ” who draws a
pension of £lOOO a year from the Victorian
Sassenach, will be 86 to day (Saturday). Dr.
Quick, Federal legislator, will be 50 on Monday.
General Booth was 73 years old on Thursday,
April 10.
Lieut. Witton, the man sent to gaol for
life for aiding in the murder of Boer prisoners,
had better stop there, if the evidence of his own
letters can be taken as indicating his character.
Writing to his brother whilst awaiting trial
It is hard luck for any man who offers his services and
his life to assist to end this war to be tried for his life
when he wipes out a few of these Dutch gentlemen. No
more British Empire building on these terms for me.
Printed that Samuel Griffith is “ a deter-
mined candidate ” for the Federal Chief Justice-
ship. The “mentioned” candidates number
about a dozen—Griffith, Barton, Wise, Way,
O’Connor, &c., &c., &c. Of the lot, if considera-
tions of personal fitness and Federal gratitude
have their proper weight, R. E. O’Connor should
be chosen. He has the necessary ability, is a
comparatively young man, is a fine lawyer, and of
admirably judicial temperament, and he has been
of most distinguished service to the Federation.
From London Globe : •
An early friend of Charles Dickens has passed away in
the person of Mr. Charles Kent, who was a constant con-
tributor to Household Words and All the Year Round
when Dickens was editor. Mr. Kent was for many years
editor of the old evening newspaper, the Sun. He was
an authority on the life and works of the first Lord
Lytton, and he also edited editions of Lamb and Thomas
Moore. Born in Parramatta in 1823, he was always said
to be the first British subject born in the colony of
N.S.W. His father was a naval officer, and his grand-
father was Captain Kent, R.N., discoverer of the Kent
Islands.
Miss Flora Shaw, the London Times repre-
sentative who visited Australia and championed
the Queensland Kanaka cause, and who seems to
have been thoroughly “in the know” over the
Jameson raid, is going to marry Colonel Lugard,
Governor of Northern Nigeria. For long past
Miss Shaw has occupied the position of colonial
editor of The Times. She is a woman so bright
and charming that one could almost forgive in her
the practical advocacy of slavery and the promo-
tion of war.

R.C. Archbishop O’Reilly (Adelaide) tries
to promote brotherly feeling among his flock by
wearing both a yellow and a green ribbon in his
button-hole on St. Patrick’s day. On the other
hand, a local shepherd, who is a strong Orangeman,
managed to induce a creeper adorning the front of
his town house to blaze out into one great expanse
of yellow on last March 17. The Irish who passed
it tried hard to believe that it was a mere
horticultural accident.
Federal Treasurer Turner’s fall in Collins-
street some time ago has permanently injured
his right hand. In stepping from roadway to
footwalk he missed his footing and fell on his
right arm, tearing the flesh, and twisting the
sinews. For weeks he carried his arm in a sling,
and, during the tariff debates, suffered terrible
agony. The other day when the bandages were
taken off it was found that the little finger and
the middle finger of his right hand were stiff. He
has great difficulty in using a pen, and is now
trying to write with his left hand.
Mr. Hussey Malone Chomley, the retiring
chief of Vic. police, is the last survivor in the
force of the officers’ mess at Eureka Stockade. He
joined as a cadet in ’52 ; others who went in about
the same time were C. 11. Nicholson, the former
Melb. P.M., and Elliott Clark, a happy-go-lucky
young sprig who afterwards adorned the British
baronetcy. The retiring Commissioner climbed
into that position while two of his seniors were
wrangling over its possession. J. B. Patterson
was faced with the alternative of ridding himself
of one or other or fixing up an impossible joiut-
commissionership. He got rid of both.
F. E. Winchcombe, who sits for Ashfield
suburb in N.S.W. State Assembly, now knows
the sort of Parliamentary lackey some consti-
tuents like. Medical advice caused him to shift
his family for 12 months from his constituency.
So he advertised and circularised that during that
time he would be in his Sydney office every day,
Saturdays excepted, and that constituents could
interview him there in business hours and write to
him at Parliament House at all hours, when
“ prompt attention would be accorded.” Yet a
demand has been made on him to retire “in favor
of some gentleman who can reside in our midst ” !
M.L. Native Minister Carroll has done
much hard work lately inducing natives in the
different districts to fall into line with Govt, legis-
lation, and appoint councils to manage their affairs
in lieu of the Maoriland Parliament. At Waioma-
tatini (Poverty Bay) “Jimmy,” in his shirt-
sleeves, haggled with a meeting of 1500 all night
and well on into next morning, and at intervals
for three days afterwards, until he bent the dusky
multitude to his will. Seddon was sent for when
the battle was at its height, and he travelled day
and night to lend moral support to his henchman.
The civilised Carroll acted the thorough Maori
right through, conformed to all requirements of
Maori etiquette, joined in'the tangi of respect to
Ropata’s memory, saluted the chiefs in time-
honored fashion, and took his place amongst them
lying on the floor.

Concerning a lately-deceased mining-
man : ■ ; f :.
James Dick, the Glasgow millionaire, whose demise
was lately cabled, was well-known throughout Aus-
tralia as a successful mining speculator. He did riot
profess to know anything about mining, hut as the result
of good luck he succeeded in gathering piles of “gear”
from that source. He first visited Australia in the early,
days Of Broken Hill and acquired a big holding in the
Proprietary mine, in which, for some time, he was the
largest individual shareholder. He also made successful
deals in Mt. Morgan and Mt. Lyell shares, and was chief
owner of the Scottish Gympie mines. For years he was
interested in Mitchell’s Creek gold mine, Wellington
(N.S.W.) and lately became sole owner. He at once
ordered the dismantling of the old plant—a curious col-
lection of oddities, a general rattletrap and ramshackle
makeshift. To-day the mine has the best equipment of
any gold mine in the State. It is a coincidence that the
day on which the new battery was given its trial run was
that on which Mr. Dick died. A Scotch paper describes
him as “ one of the plainest and pleasantest men that you
could meet on a long summer day.” This is a true bill. A
less ostentatious or better-natured man never breathed. A
stranger meeting him casually would hardly have credited
him with being possessed of a “jingle on a tombstone.”
Writer will never forget his first meeting with Dick at the
Mitchell’s Creek mine. Was told that Dick was in. the
vanner-house. Found a group of men at the end of a
vanner, only one of whom seemed to be doing any work.
This was Dick himself. He was coatless and collarless,
and perspiration was pouring out of him. He was wield-
ing a strongly-heated smoothing-iron and was enveloped
in most evil-smelling fumes arising from a cauldron of
molten matter. He explained that he was giving .Jus.
men a lesson in welding balata belting. The pupils had
the best of that particular job. He was a remarkable
man for a Scotchman in that he had a supreme contempt
for the “ mickle that makes the muckle.” He was keen
when tens of thousands of pounds were involved, bub the
paltry £5 details worried him to irascibility. This is said
to be the reason why the Mitchell’s Creek partners were

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the ease of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“ S’Africa ” : Re proposed Australian ac-
climatisation of the eland. Like most of the
antelope tribe, the eland can get along on about
two drinks a week, but anyone who imagines it
would live in a country too poor for cattle, is mis-
taken, for, in its native regions, it has good
(though sometimes dry) grass all the year round.
If our squatters want to experiment with a new
animal, I would suggest that they should try the
ostrich, or, failing that, wait until a new beast
occurs that will fatten on desert dust, and quench
its thirst in the imaginary limpid waters of the
mirage.

* * * *
For the benefit of his pray-for-rain people,
Rev. Baden-Powell, M.A., F.R.S., &c., wrote in
1859 :
The very essence of the whole argument is the invari-
able preservation of the principle of order : not neces-
sarily such as we can directly recognise, but the universal
conviction of the unfailing subordination of everything to
some grand principles of law, however imperfectly appre-
hended in our partial conceptions, and the successive
subordination of such laws to others of still higher
generality, to an extent transcending our conceptions,
and constituting the true chain of universal causation
which culminates in the sublime conception of the
Cosmos. It is in immediate connection with this en-
larged view of universal immutable natural order that I
have regarded the narrow notions of those who obscure
the sublime prospect by imagining so unworthy an idea
as that of occasional interruptions in the physical economy
of the world.
* • * *
“J.C.R.”: There is nothing whatever
theoretic in the proposition of seasons alternating
from north to south successively. The torrid con-
ditions just experienced in Australia followed
upon intense summer heat in the Northern
Hemisphere. And most certainly will the ensuing
winter prove analogous to that at present preva-
lent in Europe—abnormally cold and blusterous.
The 19 years’ cycle theory is but a theory ; and a
long-winded one at that. But that seasons alter-
nate from north to south is a proposition ascer-
tainable by prompt observation. The droughts of
India and Australia have connection, the alterna-
tion being governed by analogous climatic condi-
tions and other physical modifying influences.
Drought in India would probably be followed by
droug it in N. Australia.
“ Long-un ” : S.A. Govt, is at present
very hot on Closer Settlement. It recently bought
53,000 acres (Yongala estate) at 465. an acre,
mostly arable and a fair amount rough pastoral
country. Lessee has to pay 4 per cent, on the
purchase money, and can pay for improvements in
21 annual instalments or in cash. The one-horse
township of Yongala went wild with excitement
during the fortnight occupied in allotting the
lands. The publicans erected huge booths, and
the place swarmed with squatters, thriving far-
mers, poor cookies, and starving, long-bearded
toilers from outside the line of rainfall. What
tales these last had to tell of seven years’ drought
and an average of half-bushel crops, many years’
arrears of rent, subsistence on bread and dripping,
and children walking seven and eight miles to and
from school ! Some of the applicant.:, broke down
completely in describing their woes. A few of
these starvelings were allotted lands at rentals of
£3O to £lOO yearly, and the Almighty only knows
where the money is to come from. Most of these
misguided people are under the impression that
the rents will all be reduced by-and-bye. If so, it
will be at the expense of the taxpayer, and Closer
Settlement will speedily cut its own throat.
* * * *
The cussedness of Fate ! A grazier in
Merool district (N.S.W.) who had managed to
keep his sheep alive through the drought by cart-
ing water for miles, lost most of them from the
cold caused by the recent rain. Another farmer,
near Temora (N.S.W.), had been in sore straits
for want of water, and just after the rain came his
two-year-old boy fell into a puddle and was
drowned.
* • * *
“Malle”: “A. 1.8.” (B. 24/8/’01) does
not multiply his bee-nests enough. On the Upper
Murray flats, near Khancobar, I have counted 11
nests within 100yds., on both sides of a straight
three-miles track. Men' were sent out with a
dray , from Towwong station every autumn, and
could always collect enough honey in a couple of
days to last the station-hands for the rest of the
year. From 2cwt. to 3cwt. of strained honey is a
common yield from a nest, and is occasionally
doubled. I believe it would pay to go up with a
turn-out; and,
after procuring
a load, send it
to Sydney or
Melbourne.
Distance from
railway and dif-
ficulty of coun-
try are the only
drawbacks.
“Bob R.” : Immediately after a fall of
rain out-back you will see the sheep standing with
one ear down in an attitude of expectation. Fact
is, they’re listening for the earth to crack, prior
to a blade of grass shooting up ; and, the moment
this appears, they’re on to it. In that God-for-
saken country the poor jumbucks can’t afford to
take any chances.
* * • *
What the aborigines will do for opium.
A nigger, in Springsure (C.Q.) district, walked
from a station 12 miles to that townlet and back,
in six hours, for a smoke.
* * * * «
“ Roddam ” : He “ Indra’s ” question
about the identity of bell-bird with leather-head.
The latter has a bald poll, a cry like “ roc-e-doc,”
ad infinitum, and is mostly found in big timber—
ironbark for choice. The bell-bird is feathered
on the top, is greenish rather than grey, and has
only two notes —“ ting-tong, ” like a small bell—
always goes in mobs, frequents low scrub, mostly
at heads of gullies, and keeps to the same ground
for years. I know one patch in Upper Burra-
gorang where they have remained for the last
seven years. Have never known them to 'tackle
fruit, but have shot leather-heads in the act.

From an Adelaide paper :
The South Australian troops went into camp at Black
Forest on Thursday night. The programme of manoeuvres
was abandoned, owing to hundreds of cattle being in the
country where the troops were to be exercised.
Boers, perhaps; but bullocks—no fear !

“ Traveller ” : Re recent pars about bush
persons who have never seen the sea or a railway.
I know a man in a N.S.W. town—distant only 40
miles from the sea—who is over 30 years of age,
yet has never exerted himself sufficiently to dis-
cover the ocean. An intelligent and well-informed
man, too. Talk about phlegm !

unearthed, measuring sft. sin. long by fin.
diameter at thicker end and gin. at the smaller.

“Trotter”: Some of Queensland’s rail-
ways would make a cat laugh. Emerald-Springsure
is a white elephant. Bowen-Wangaratta runs to
a gum-tree. Cooktown-Laura runs through 67
miles of desert. The last has a train twice a
week, and rarely has a passenger bar a nigger or
lubra.
“J.W.C.”: Re color-variety in rabbits
(“Wallah,” B. 24/8/’01). In all country newly
invaded by the pest the only colors met are grey,
and an odd sandy, but where the rabbits are very
numerous white, black-and-white, blue, brindie,
etc., are to be seen. My opinion is that these
variations are due to breeding by immature does,
resulting in deteriorated progeny.
» * T *
Those Victorian farmers who have been
losing their stock wholesale lately—apparently
through poisoned rabbits or noxious weeds or
something uncertain—have had some plain truth
just handed to them by vet.-surgeon Kendall and
Chief Stock Inspector Weir. After dealing with
the effects of the “disease,” their report gives the
cause :
Soil exhaustion—the first indication of which is the
bone-chewing habit in cattle and wool-eating in sheep.
The next stage is “cripples,” and finally the present
paralytic form. For upwards of 50 years the land, never
very rich, has been robbed of its annual crop of beef,
mutton, dairy produce, or cereals. If the English or
Danish pastures were left without fertilisers for ten years
they would no longer carry stock. The. fact is, stock-
owners have far more land than they can manage, and in
many cases it would be wise to sell half, or more, and
expend all the money in fertilisers and forage crops.
* * * *
“ Wallaroo ” : The average cockie is either
criminally improvident or beastly mean. Every
year he cultivates just another acre of his already
over-stocked grazing-land, sells every bag of grain
as soon as it is harvested, and never thinks of
growing anything to help his stock through the
winter. Sometimes he even completes the ruin
by “running a few sheep.” Result: a distracted
farmer, who sold at (say) Is. 3d. per bushel,
eager to re-buy at double price, or hiring a
station paddock, about mid-winter, for his ema-
ciated horses to die in. And Brown wonders
why his half-dozen starved cows yield less milk
and butter than Smith’s one well-fed cow. The
cockie never profits by experience.

The Maoriland chestnut about the Dunedin
Chinaman who, in deference to Scottish local
preponderance, took the surname of Macgregor,
is easily beaten by a true story from Oamaru. A
grocery firm, trading as Dooiey and MacTavish,
went bung, and during bankruptcy proceedings
anxious enquiries were made for the mysterious
MacTavish. At length, yielding to entreaties,
Dooley said —“Oi’ll bring him along.” And,
presently, with an introductory “Mr. Dugald
MacTavish, gentlemen!” he led up the donkey
which, hitched to liis senior partner’s vehicle,
performed the daily rounds for orders.
Kava-drinking in Fij is becoming
prosaic. The custom was for natives
to sit round in a circle chewing kava
root. Each individual would then
get up in turn, approach the large
kava bowl in the centre, and
solemnly spit into it. When all
had done this, water was added,
fibre being used as a strainer,
which was manipulated to the
tune of some native lament ; then
the national beverage . was handed
round in a cocoanut bowl, and each
wished each other good luck and long
life in about 2000 words. The usual
custom now is to use a bread-grater to
grind the kava, a tin colander to strain it,
and a pannikin to toss the stuff down. All
the old-time ceremony is dispensed with. Kava
has a peculiar effect. It does not “go to the
head,” but to the legs, so that a man can be
“rollingdrunk ” below, yet quite sober aloft. All
alcoholic drink is forbidden by ordinance to
natives or half-castes.

“Cecil P.” : Bora Creek, N.S.W , gets
its name from the presence of a Bora—or place
where young men were “ made.” The little glade,
ringed with timber, where the mystical ceremony
took place, was pointed out to me by a local
prospector.

Dear Bulletin.— Re the casualness of the
Australian native. A young girl, aged about 19,
came into my shop here (Broken Hill) for a dona-
tion to help defray her expenses to South Aus-
tralia. She stated that the young man she was
“going with” had got her into trouble. Never
in any other country have I been asked to sub-
scribe to a complete stranger’s immoral elope-
ment.—Brock.
« * • *
“Itan ” : It is very rare to meet an abori-
ginal in the Hunter (N.S.W.) valley who knows a
word of the native lingo. Many of them, ill fact,
speak better English than the whites. This pro-
bably because there are many settlers in isolated
parts who, week after week, never see a strange
face, whereas the blacks are more inclined to
settle near towns.
* * * ♦
Cooktown (N. Queensland) Independent
asks anxiously “Whit shall we do with our
girls?” There are very few girls in Cooktown,
but Chinese and colored miscellanies do the
cooking and washing, and there is little or nothing
left for even the scarce white girl to do. She can
hardly even “fall”—Japanese ladies do most of
the falling up that way.
* • * •
“Bob R.” : Encouraging “the man on
the land.” A N.S. Wales C.P.-inspector lately
officially visited a small holding and found the
selector absent. Inspector proceeded on his round,
and on his return journey called at the same selec-
tion, stayed the night (unofficially) and was enter-
tained by the same selector with the best he had.
Nevertheless, at the court of inquiry, the inspector
swore that he had officially visited this selection
and found nobody there, nor any signs of habita-.
tion, though he admitted that lie had seen the
selector there later on, The holding was accord-
ingly recommended for forfeiture.

“ Elrey ” to “ D.E.R.” (B. 31/8/’01) : I
have seen a ring, as described, made by the W.Q.
aborigines. It was about 50 yards in diameter,
and the ground was slightly raised and quite firm.
I believe the blacks use it secretly for the man-
hood-initiation ordeal. If the youths pass this
they are allowed to come to camp again and take
a wife ; if they do not pass they are put to death.
So at least I was told by an old gin, who further
said that any woman prying into the matter would
be also slain.

THE END.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sun that scorches the treeless plain,
Moon that mirrors its heat again ;
Driving sand where the track should run ;
The body . . . Pain,
And the water . . . done.
Walking—crawling—a parching air;
A reeling brain and a dull despair ;
A vision of wells and water deep ;
A curse ... a prayer . . .
And . . . the Final Sleep.
MERIDEN.
OUR ANCIENT RUIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The new-chum leaned against the bar
And tapped his boot and tipped his beer,
Then, looking round, remarked : “ Er —ar —
One thing you chaps don’t have out here !
“ You’ve churches, chapels, pubs, and halls—
Er—er —and such and so-and-so ;
But you’ve no ivied abbey-walls—
No ancient ruins, don-cher-know ! ”
Then Jack the Shearer left the bar
(It might have been to dodge his shout !)
They.filled ’em up with Dick’s three-star,
And scarcely noticed him go out.
He came back, lugging by the hair
Old Ned the Cook, all rags and beer—
“ Er—er ! ” the joker said. “ Er—air—
“ We’ve got one ancient ruin here ! ”

“ Picola ” : Statist Coghlan lately pub-
lished some startling figures on the birth-rate of
N.S. W. Where would the figures be without the
prolific dairy-farmer ? The ruling percentage is
only kept at its present mediocre standard by this
vigorous class of the peeple. They can’t afford to
have small families; they want plenty of hands
to work, and they set out to produce them as fast
as Nature will allow. Show me a successful
dairy-farmer, and I will show you the father of a
numerous family ! Within a two-mile radius, of
where I write there are 20 farms, each supporting
a family. Amongst 12 of these families there are
123 children, giving an average of 10. .Maximum
is 15; minimum, 7. The remaining eight farms
are run by young couples, who, however, show
strong indications of at least rivalling the splendid
record of their elder neighbors. What fine,
strapping lads, and strong, hardy, broad-hipped
women they are, too ! There’s nothing anaemic
about your dairy-bred youngster. Moreover, they
are of the sort that stick to the soil as pertinaci-
ously as the soil sticks to some of them.

* * * *
Biking along a bush road
t’other day I met a wooden-faced youth driving a
pathetically-featured horse incidentally fastened
with fencing-wire, etc., to a disreputable old
shandrydan of a waggonette. The vehicle con-
tained a weird, howling figure, tied flat on its
back with wool-ropes. Asked the driver what on
earth the noisy substance behind him was. He
reined up and calmly replied: “Oh, that’s my
father ! ’E’s bin on a spree at Blanktown for a
week, an’ I’m takin’ ’im ’ome ! Git up, Kate ! ”
Mounted my spinning-Janet and went my way,
praising the Lord that I had no sons to tear me
thus away from my beer.
* * * *
Tahune Linah tells The Bulletin about a
human ostrich :
Recently saw a “ Dutchman ” sailor bite sections out of
stout tumblers, crunch the glass, and swallow it with
apparent ease. Also saw him light a cigar, and, when
it was fully drawing, inhale the smoke, and then bolt
what remained of burning portion—ashes and all. Am
informed that his favorite delicacy is old clay-pipes,
which he devours with relish. Last Boxing Day he killed
a snake at a Huon port, skinned it, and ate it raw.
When remonstrated with he said, “ Dat is nodings !”
and promptly pouched the skin ! Pact. Says he has
been 12 years at this sort of thing. I cannot understand
how a man can eat glass without injury to his vitals. Saw
this phenomenon myself in a bar do the glass-eating
“ turn ” to convince a bushie who declared that the thing
was impossible. Bushie's comment was —“ You’ll be a
stiff-’un in a month ! I can believe natural things—but
you’re super—blanky—natural 1 ”

“Cecil P.” : How many bushmen know
how to get the sun on a cloudy day? Get into
the open, hold a knife-blade perpendicularly,
touching the thumb-nail, and turn self slowly
round till the blade oasts shadow on (lie nad.
With any idea of time, sufficient gracp of com-
pass-points may be thus obtained to prevent one
from being too badly slewed.

“Cric” : How’s this for luck? A good
few years ago, Frank Allan, “ the bowler of a cen-
tury,” was shooting near Warrnambool. Jumping
a fence in pursuit of a bird, he came down with
his boot on the end of a snake, the other end of
which immediately tvvisted round his ankle.
Fearing to look down, he kept on grinding away
with his heel. After a time he mustered up
courage to investigate, and found that it was the
reptile’s head he had pulverised. No more shoot-
ing that day. Made tracks for home and went to
bed.
* * » *
On either side of the Calliope River, near
Gladstone (Q.), are fixed-up notifications that the
crossing is dangerous if there is more than Sin.
of water on the causeway. Many a poor wretch
has met his doom through neglect of this notice.
The danger is due to an exceptionally strong cur-
rent with a falling tide, and a growth on the
stones that makes them slippery as glass.
Blank, the celebrated Aus-
tralian actor-author-manager,
taking his world-famous original
drama, “ Ashes to Ashes, or the
Bushranger’s Bi’ide,” through
the provinces lately, struck a bad
streak in a Yic. Western dis-
trict township. The second act
of “The Bushranger’sß.” oper.s
in the heart of the Australian
bush as represented by a paint-
ing of a poplar-tree, a number of
boughs, and an invisible laugh-
ing-jackass. The kookaburra
lends local color by laughing
hilariously in the wings imme-
diately the curtain goes up. The
Western district audience had
not been well pleased by Act 1.,
but it cottoned to the kookaburra
right away, and gave his laugh
a tumultuous encore. The bird
smiled loudly again in response
to the pressing public demand,
and the audience was so enrap-
tured by the marvellous fidelity
of the imitation that it continued
encoring for many minutes, ab-
solutely refusing to hear a word
of Blank’s world-famous drama
while the bird-mimic had a grin
left in him. At length in despair
the manager trotted his come-
dian down to the lights, and
introduced him as the clever
impersonator of Australia’s na-
tional bird ; but, unfortunately,
at that moment the real songster
behind pealed forth again, and
Blank was left stammering and
blushing before an uproarious
audience. This was too much
for the celebrated Australian
actor-author-manager, and stand-
ing 0, and shaking his fist con-
vulsively R.U.E., yelled: “Who
the devil’s ticklin’ that blanky
burrd ? ” There was no more of
“The Bushranger’s Bride” that night.

“S.S.” re the employers’ wail as lo the
pernicious effects of Peacock’s Pactories Act on
Victoria’s native industries, and the allegation
that it has drawn all the laborers from the well-
paid, salubrious fields of rural employment to
munificently-rewarded, but unhealthy, factory-
work in the cities :
The employers of the Goulburn Valley have been
loudest in their wail. Some months ago a relative of
mine obtained a promise of a job at a vineyard in that
district. He followed that promise up pertinaciously.
At length he was packed off, at his own expense, not
having dared to ask what the rate of pay would be for
fear of being considered an ingrate and a pro-Boer. He
now finds that the assiduously-pursued billet is worth
10s. a week and plain tucker, and the work is dabbling
painfully and dangerously in caustic soda, cleaning vats ;
but there is a splendid promise of a rise of half-a-crown
when grape-picking begins.

“C.D.P.” : Cow died. I blamed Dailing
pea in imported chaff. On opening carcase found
whole skin of paunch peeled off and hanging in
shreds. Symptoms like arsenic-poisoning, but
test proved otherwise. Also gave my pad one
feed. Result, he became so drowsy that I had to
kick him along the road. Won a two-mile trot
with him same afternoon simply because nothing
would make him “ break.” Stake and bets more
than paid for cow, so can’t grumble. Would like
to hear from some Bulletineers on the subject.

“ Yarrum ” : Was in a shop t’other day
when a decrepit Chinaman came in for tobacco.
It took him minutes to find his solitary sixpence,
which was carefully wrapped in rag after rag, each
dirtier than the next, the shirt from which the
whole was taken being a miracle of filthiness.
Yet some people put money in their mouths !

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.

disappeared.— Yours truly,
“ JOHN B. MONCKTON.”

The Greatest Remedy in the World!
Kutnow’s Powder
Endorsed, used, and prescribed by the most distinguished
Physicians of Great Britain and the Colonies —Sir Andrew
Clark, M.D., Sir Spencer Wells, M.D., Sir Morell Mackenzie,
M.D., Professors Lawson Tait, M.D., B. L. Wyman, M.D.,
I. N. Love, M.D., W. R. Jones, M.D., F. H. Griffin, M.D.,
Julian Altliaus, M.D., George Harley, M.L>., Stanley Rendel,
M.D., and hundreds of others too numerous to mention.
Constantly Used by the Royal Family.
KUTNOW’S POWDER is doubly efficient in Rheumatism and Gout. It dissolves
uric acid and phosphatic sediments as well as other products difficult of elimination, while at the
same time it exerts a moderately stimulant effect upon the renal cells, and thereby facilitates the
swift removal of insoluble materials from the body. Without such actions insoluble sub-
stances will precipitate in the kidneys and bladder. The intense suffering produced by stone, together with
consecutive pyelitis and cystitis, are avoided by prompt elimination. Unquestionably, although the speedy
removal of uric acid and other products of faulty tissue change is of conspicuous benefit, yet to pre-
vent their formation is a service still more important. This service is performed
by KUTNOW’S POWDER when it corrects those digestive failures which are re-
sponsible for the production of deleterious materials. —Vide Medical Record, Sept., 1901 .
Beware of Fraudulent Imitations.

cine Vendors. See that the Registered Trade Mark, “ Hirschensprung ” (or Deer Leap), and

POLITICAL
POINTS
Rhodes did paint some of Africa red :
British red, Boer red, Australian red, Tasmanian
red, Maoriland red, Canadian red.
At a recent Childers (Q.) fire, several
Kanakas helped shopkeepers to remove goods
from the burning buildings, but, when the flames
reached the store of the local Labor member,
Tommy Tanna refused to try and save any of his
goods.
One rabid rag threatened Barton with an
adverse R.C. vote at next election for refusing
to treat St. Patrick properly in postal affairs ;
now another rabid rag threatens him with an
adverse Protestant vote for his speech on St.
Patrick’s Day.
Tasmania is rapidly being goaded into
declaring war on the Commonwealth —refused a
paid portfolio in the Ministry; deprived of her
beloved Tattersall; and now her soldier hero
abruptly sacked from the command of the 3rd
battalion Commonwealth contingent!
Emissaries from Sydney and other daily
papers hung about the lobbies of the Federal Par-
liament for weeks “ interviewing ” members about
postage on newspapers, and yet no one had the
courage to hand these sturdy beggars over to the
police for soliciting alms.
The curious news is published that a
“ first batch ” of Australian settlers is leaving for
the New Hebrides to settle on those lands taken
over by the Federal Government from Burns,
Philp, and Co. This seems good enough for
Burns, Philp, and Co., who will carry these people
across, and whose commercial business will be
improved by increased settlement, but where does
Australia come in ? It can’t afford to export able-
bodied taxpayers.
Prime Minister Barton does not get all the
credit he deserves for his fat and fervid loyalty ;
apparently, he protests too much. There are
papers like the Westminster Gazette “jollying”
him mercilessly because, in his assumed ardor and
frantic fictitious Imperialism, he cried out about
Australia not wanting to hear “idle talk of a
general amnesty or a lavish re-stocking of Boer
farms at the expense of loyal citizens.” Really,
this is too bad, considering the difficulty Toby had
in working himself up to some semblance of
patriotic fury.
Altogether six members of the old South
Australian Parliament, which ceases to exist at
the end of March, will be absent from the
election fight in May, but as six members
have been cut off the Council and twelve off
the Assembly, there is still material for
many catastrophes when polling-day comes.
The half-dozen not standing again
are :—Council—Brewer
Smith, who goes to
seethe Coronation,
and auctioneer
Sand ford, wh o
suffers from
“parson’s
throat,’’and has
to nurse his
voiee. Assem-
bly—Hourigan,
who is dead;
Hague, who in-
tends in future
to “ mind his own
business”; and
Jamieson, who has
been balloted-out by
the National Ass
in the interests Of a
stronger man.
Barclay, the erst-
while anti-Jingo M.P.
for Dunedin (M.L.),
has hauled down his
colors. He now de-
clares that, “if the
Empire desires it,” he
t! links a tenth contin-
gent should be sent.
His backing-down at this
latejhour probably means
the ‘end of his political career.
Had he stuck to his guns, he would
in all likelihood have been returned
again at the coming election. The dour
Scots of Dunedin may be Jingoes, but they have
sense enough to appreciate toughness and out-
spoken consistency. Hucheson has always been a
more fearless anti-Jingo than even Barclay ; yet,
at the very height of the M.L. blood-frenzy, he
was-returned for Wellington at the head of the
poll, and will be so returned again this year.
The local clergy barracked pro-Philp for
all they were worth in Charters Towers. Alleged
reason—the moral shortcomings of one of the
Towers members. There is no instance on record
of any Q. sky-pilot ever having denounced a mem-
ber of the Govt. Party for his moral lapses, though
several of the Philpite representatives have truly
fearsome reputations.
The S.A. Jenkins had to throw over two
Ministers, the other day ; and his retention of
Foster, the Works Minister, who howled for the
Snow train grab, instead of O’Loughlin, was a
plain intimation that the more the Laborites
attack him in this election fight the better he will
like it. If Jenkins can’t get an absolute majority
of members, he is done for—the Laborites will go
over to Darling, for sure.
Senator Drake’s new clause in the Public
Service Bill, giving preference in appointments to
the Service of men who had served five years in
the naval or military forces, deserved the fate it
got. It was another indirect attempt to fillip up
militarism. If the Federal Govt, feels justified in
developing militarism let it do it straightly by
direct pay for military services. Then everyone
will know exactly where he is.
According to the Jingo papers, N.S. Wales
M.L. A. Holman’s political light had been quite
extinguished by his opposition to the Gore policy :
it was only a matter of an election and he would
be sent by his constituents to a disgraced private
life. Now Holman has just toured his electorate,
having enthusiastic greetings everywhere, except
at one palpably packed gathering, and the pro-
Gores haven’t the decency to print the facts.
The S.A. Labor party includes old-age
pensions in its programme for the coming State
election, but says nothing about raising the money
to pay them—-and the S.A. Treasury is already
stony-broke without undertaking new liabilities.
It also demands a minimum wage of 7s. a day for
all adults employed by the State or on State con-
tracts, and says nothing about raising the money
to pay for that either. The odium of raising the
money is always left to the other fellow. It is a
dreadful thing to be the other fellow.
Writes “ Up North ” :
The indignation of the Philp Govt, over the conduct of
certain Q. Federal Parliamentarians who travelled on
their passes over the Q. lines to help Q. Democrats during
the late campaign, seems very absurd in face of the fact
that Premier Philp had the front to run a special train
for himself from Brisbane to Rockhampton (cost about
£100), in order that he might deliver a speech of half-an-
hour in favor of G. S. Curtis, the Govt, candidate for
Rockhampton. The Federal reps, cost the country no
additional expense, for special trains were luxuries clean
beyond them. . . . And just fancy the condition of the
country that pays £lOO for the privilege of hearing poor,
hopeless, hapless Bob Philp orate for 30min.! During next
session man after man of Philp’s supporters will probably
rise and denounce the State railway system as a failure,
and wonder why the deuce the Govt, lines don’t pay.
Why, indeed?
THE SPREAD OF IMPERIALISM.
HORI (just returned from Maori Council meet-
ing, to Marae): “Kapai Tick Seddon!
Kapai te Tuke! Kill ’em te plurry Poa !”
THE MILITARY VOLCANO.—GREAT WOOLLOOMOOLOO ERUPTION.
Vic. Chief Secretary Trenwith shows a
desire to return to democratic ideals in his pro-
posal to enact the registration of homesteads, so
as to prevent small farmers and tenants of ‘ ‘ work-
men’s homes ” from getting into the clutches of
mortgagees without the knowledge and consent of
their wives. Safe to predict that the aggregation
of mortgagees in the Council of Rich Landlords will
have nothing of Trenwith’s idea, but the Peacock
Govt, cares not a rap what happens to its p 6-
posals so long as it can brag about their mention
in the Governor’s beginning-of-Parliament speech.
The most disappointing feature in the pro-
gramme of S.A. Labor party at the coming elec-
tion is that it demands the reform, instead of the
abolition, of the Upper House—which latter
would be the wisest economy S. A. could adopt.
Besides, the existence of Upper Houses in the
subordinate States of a Federation is a shocking
outrage on Imperial precedent, at which the Tory
Council itself should shudder—either personally
or by deputy. The only other British Federation
is Canada, and of its eight divisions six, including
the principal province (Ontario), have one House
of Legislature only.
The action of the Labor party in wiping off
the tea and kerosene duties has landed S.A. in a
fearful financial mess. There is sure to be a deficit
of from £70,000 to £150,000 at the end of the
official year, fsr the railway revenue has fallen
dreadfully owing to the slump in metals. It is
probably the complicated condition of the Ex-
chequer which has induced Premier Jenkins to
retain the services of Treasurer Butler, who would
otherwise have dropped out of the Ministry when
the reductions came into force on Easter Monday.
Butler is a very whinesome person but he is
supposed to know something about finance.
Tasmania, in its bitterness about the loss
of “Tattersall” and the revenue he brought it,
seems utterly unable to realise that even if the
Federal Govt, hadn’t shut down on him he was
bound to be lost in any case. He only left his old
stand at the Marble Bar, Sydney, and went to
out-of-the-way Brisbane because N.S.W. stopped
his letters. He moved on again to still more out-
of-the-way and inconvenient Hobart because
Queensland stopped his letters, and Victoria and
S.A. refused to deliver them. If the Federal
Govt, decided to deliver Tatt.’s letters that deci-
sion would apply all over the Commonwealth, and
Tatt. would get back to his old Sydney premises,
or some other central spot. Supposing Tasmania
can save Tatt. from extinction by appeal to the
High Court it will lose him and his revenue just
the same. So what is its row about ?
The House of Reps, having, with immense
labor, got through the tariff, it has been re-com-
mitted, and the awful process of going through it
has started over again. Some 77 items are to be
re-considered. The first day three hours were
spent over the duty on socks, and then the item
was postponed to be talked of all over again.
Then cotton piece goods were discussed, the little
gang of Freetrade obstructionists being on their
feet almost all the time. Finally they demanded
an adjournment with the alternative of talking all
night, and nothing was done. Apparently very
little will be done till Pumpkin Smith gets tem-
porary paralysis of the jaw.
The latest move of the nigger-labor party
comes from the Very Far North. The pearl-
shellers of Thursday, that most drunken of islands,
threaten that if they are not allowed to import
nigger labor they will have to remove their head-
quarters to Dutch Papua, and become naturalised
Dutch subjects, drinking on Dutch territory.
And the pearlers talk as if this would be a very
Shocking Calamity indeed. But probably, like a
good many other people, they will think better of
it. In. North Queensland, according to the
colored labor advocate, the white man is a helpless
idiot who can’t do anything without a nigger, and
he carries this theory of the white man’s useless-
ness to such absurd lengths that He makes the
country incredulous as well as tired.
There was much underground engineering
over the recommittal of the salt duties in the
House of Reps. Bonython and Batchelor did the
whipping-up on the side of the Protectionists, and
Pumpkin Smith worked t’other way. The Oppo-
sition talked all day. Early in the evening it
was reported to Reid that a majority had been
assured against the Govt., two members having
been got out of the way. With his eyeglass on
the gallery, Mr. Reid then, instead of going to
a vote, started a yelling denunciation of the
Govt. And while lie was talking the S.A. salt
party was actively at work and nabbed four votes,
and when the division was taken, Yes-No was in
a minority of three ! It is not often that a speech
has sucli a marked effect on a division. George.
Raid converted four members—to the other side.
The Queensland Government proposes to
close up some of its non-paying railway-lines—the
Bowen line (48 miles), Cooktown fine (68 miles),
and the Emerald-Springsure branch (41 miles) are
spoken of as three that are likely to be shut, and
others may follow. These three lines cost
£644,180. No information about the Springsure
branch is available, but the Bowen and Cooktown
lines, in 1900, produced £4llB revenue and cost
£15,871 in working expenses alone, to say nothing
about £24,000 a year or so for interest. They are
both “ permanent and reproductive works ” of a
good many years standing, and though the in-
terest bill will go on there will be a saving of
over £ll,OOO a year to the harassed Philp’s re-
venue by their abandonment. Besides, they
have been neglected and allowed to fall into
decay till there isn’t much left now to abandon.
A return of all the closed-up railways of Australia
will soon be interesting reading.

Mr. PARKS of California Gully, Bendigo,
Victoria, swears that
VITADATIO
has Cured him of
CANCER in the TONGUE.
31/5/’Ol
To Mr. PALMER.—
I, Frederick Parks, of California Gully, near Bendigo,
in the State of Victoria, do solemnly and sincerely declare
that this is my testimonial to you on behalf of my cure.
I have been a sufferer with cancer in the mouth for the
past six years. I went to the hospital, and the first thing
they did was to take the tongue right out. In two
months’ time, after the operation, the cancer grew again;
it got larger than before. The doctors said that nothing
could be done, so I went home and was put to bed. I
asked my wife to get me a bottle of VITADATIO. I took
half-a-bottle that night, and in three days I was able to
take oatmeal and sago, and have had no other medicine
but VITADATIO within my mouth. I can solemnly
declare that the world does not know the power of VITA-
DATIO. lam the only one living out of forty-five cases
of cancer treated last year, and can solemnly swear that
only for VITADATIO I would not be here now, so I think
you can guarantee a cure for cancer, as mine was a very
bad case ; there being no hope, the doctors said, and now
I can take any kind of food the same as before the opera-
tion. And I make this solemn declaration, conscien-
tiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of an
Act of Parliament of Victoria rendering persons making a
false declaration punishable for wilful and corrupt per-
jury.
F. PARKS.
Declared before me, at Bendigo, in the State aforesaid,
this 14th day of May, in the year of our Lord one
thousand nine hundred and one.
J. M. DAVIES, J.P.
Far further particulars,
8. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

A Bathurst resident sends this :
Peter J. Handcock, court-martialled and shot with
“ The Breaker,” Morant, was the latter’s antithesis. He
was a fine type of the silent Australian ; essentially a doer,
not a talker. Reared in the bush, after serving his time
(blacksmith) he returned to bush pursuits. Resourceful
and a grafter he was never out of work. Morally and
physically clean-living, careful financially, he was no rude
over-stepper of social conventions. He was a devoted
friend, a fair but bitter enemy. If half the charges urged
against him are true, then war must have created and
fostered a blood-lust and boodle-lust that were ever
absent from his actions in this country. That he ever
initiated an outrage I must doubt, but that, carried away
by the arguments of another (he was too silent to argue)
he may have countenanced, or even assisted, is possible.
If he did so, Peter Handcock would face death with
pleasure rather than go back on that mate, even though
he left a wife and three loved children to mourn him.
Facially, by the way, Handcock bore a startling
likeness to Ketchener.

HERR RASSMUSSEN (HANS PETER RASMUSSEN),
The Celebrated Danish Botanist,
Proprietor and Discoverer of the World-Famed Alfaline Herbal Remedies,
which have been the means of effecting the most marvellous Cures for Blood,
Skin and Nervous Diseases ever heard of in Australia.
Herr Rassmussen’s
It is about Twenty Years since Herb
Rassmussen introduced his Herbal Remedies
to the Australian Public. At that time
he was only known to the public as a Botanist
pure and simple, having devoted almost his
whole life to the study of that most useful
and interesting branch of science. It how-
ever, soon dawned on Herb Rassmussen that
immense virtues existed in many plants which
were entirely unknown to medical men, and
that it was his duty towards his fellow man
to bring these new discovered Herbal Remedies
within the meaus of suffering humanity.
Herr Rassmussen at once set to work,
and his entire energy was directed towards
extracting the medical properties from all
kiuds of plants, and putting them up in the
most suitable and practical form, according
Australian Career:
to the nature of the complaints which they
had been found a cure for.
Pills and Powders were found to be the
most practical form to administer these new
Herbal Remedies.
It is unnecessary to particularise here the
immense success Herr Rassmussen met with
in his venture twenty years ago ; suffice it to
say that in less than twelve months the
great and wonderful cures brought about by
his Alfaline Herbal Remedies were known
throughout Australia, New Zealand, and many
other parts of the world. Testimonials and
Honors arrived from all parts. Gold Medals
from Paris and other places were awarded, and
Herr Rassmussen’s name has been a house-
hold word ever since.— (Sydney Press.)

a man) from the methods of the married slattern
(feminine counterpart of the city-loafer), the baby-farmer
and abortionist —all extreme types. Setting- aside these,
the average mother’s life is a daily round of self-sacrifice,
when she is—as is usual —mother, nurse, housekeeper,
cook, washerwoman and woodchopper combined. When
“ Gumleaf ” says he knows of half-a-dozen who didn t
suckle their babes “ to save their figures,” he is talking
pure bosh. Any woman knows that a nursing mother is
pleasantly redundant in figure, w'hilst the average Austra-
lienne has to have recourse to upholstery. Most mothers
nurse their babies as long as possible—for reasons.
“ Gumleaf ” also speaks of the growing dislike women
have to child-bearing. Well, the ancients, when they
spoke of the pains of hell in that connection, knew' of
what they spoke. And there are the added terrors that
the extra few thousand years of deterioration have effected
in the feminine frame. And it profits them in no way to
add to the population, for, to the moderately-circum-
stanced, every birth means a deprivation of comfort.

“Guinleaf” returns to the charge of cal-
lousness against women :
The-assertion that there are thousands of well-cared-
for-lovingly-watched-over babies proves nothing. Women
cannot take credit to themselves for doing their obvious
duty to their offspring, for the brute beasts of creation
also take particularly good care of theirs. Bofling a child
is not the only way in which the callousness of women is
demonstrated. Let “ A Melbourne Mother ” take a stroll
in our parks, and see the flies sucking the eyes out of
scores of babies while the mothers or the nursemaids read
sickly love-stories about ‘ ‘ the nice tall duke with the
flowing black moustache.” Let her go to the railway-
stations on any holiday and see little children being im-
patiently lugged and banged about by women hurrying
off to enjoy themselves at a picnic. I know I shall be
told that “ it is all for the sake of the children,” but it is
a queer way of giving the children a holiday. Then, any
night in the week, women are to be found travelling in
the trams with squalling, coughing infants that ought to
have been in their cots hours before. The mother has
been out gossiping, or to a theatre, and the child can
take its chance of getting its lungs ruined on the home-
ward journey. Again, let “ A Melbourne Mother ” reflect
on the callous treatment of women by women. She might
start with the doings of the Catmittee of Melbourne
Women’s Hospital.
