SPORTING NOTIONS.
Heavy showers and boisterous weather at Rand-
wick on last day of A.J.O. Autumn meet. A.J.G. Plate
(3m.) was the main thing. Owing to underfoot conditions
the committee extended time limit from 5.45 to 5.55. La
Carabine failed to line up, hut Blue Metal, Haymaker and
Sir Leonard opposed Wakeful, who, at barrier rise, was 6
to 4 on, with Sir Leonard 7to 2 against. Blue Metal made
the pace from flag-fall and kept matters boiling till passing
the sfur. post the last time ; then jock. Barden brought
the favorite to the front, and, giving her her head, let
the little wonder canter home a half-furlong to the good
in the remarkably speedy time of 5 36£. Sir Leonard
tugged fiercely in the rear for perhaps 2m. and sacrificed
much thereby, as evidenced by the failure of his finishing
effort. No 3m. event was ever before won so decisively at
Randwiek. Original time limit was beaten by and
altered time by IS.jSee., consequently the full stake is
available. Wakeful literally lost the field. She was never
credited with owning such staying power, excepting,
perhaps, by those immediately concerned, and Australian
turf records make no mention of a mare who could sprint
and endure so well. The continent’s “best” for 3m. is
Quiver and Wallace’s 5.23 J at Flemington—but comparing
Wakeful's feat with this the state of the going must carry
much weight. The wonder about Mr. McDonald’s mar-
vellous mare is that she is only a handful—little more than
a pony.
* * * *
Seventeen shaped for Second Nursery on last
day of Randwiek meet. Though having to hump 9.7 over
a heavy track, Suivre was rushed and sent out first
favorite at fours, but a 10 to 1 chance (Milner) scored (in
a walk, almost) by 10 lengths. Milner was bred by Mr.
A. E. Thompson, and is by the imported sire, Grafton,
from Loch Lava, by Lochiel from Lava, by Chester from
Etna, by Maribyrnong from The Alpaca—running back to
the mare bred by Mr. Lawson (Mudgee), and said
to have been given by him to his gardener, after-
wards employed by Rev. W. Walker, of Bathurst. Suivre
failed to make any sort of a show at any stage of the
race.
• * • •
Winning sires represented at recent A.J.C.
gathering were topped by Trenton through Wakeful’s
.four triumphs. Then came Grafton with three (Brakpan
two and Milner one), Impetus (Sir Leonard's two wins),
and Russley (Lucknow and Dahomey). Most successful
riders were J. Barden, A. Richardson, W. Bradshaw, and
F. Dunn (three winners each), and F. Kuhn (two). First
batch accounted for half the programme. Wakeful’s
owner (Mr. O. M‘Donald) heads the prize-list, getting
£3373 ; Sir Foote brought “ J. Baron ” £1370 ; then follow
A. Lawrence (£972), A. Wynne (£993), and Kelso (£872).
Unluckiest of ail was Randwiek trainer-owner Wootton,
who, though close up often, failed to score, excepting
with Alannah, and that almost un backed. He was particu-
larly unfortunate in having Acetine so good and so cherry-
ripe to bump up against such a marvel as Wakeful in
Sydney Cup. Had she won, Acetine would have landed
a small fortune for her owner.
* * * •
Presence of Sydney Cup winner Wakeful in
A. J.C. All-aged Stakes (lm.) last week scared everything
but Ferryman, Sir Leonard, and Great Scot. At flag-fall
Wakeful was 4 to 1 on with Sir Leonard next—s to l
against. Race proved a match between the favorites and
they were locked to the distance, but in the finishing
furlong Wakeful shook the other off and won by
lengths, with Ferryman a bad third. Time (1.39 J) was
very speedy—only |sec. slower than the record.
Wakeful didn't shape for subsequent Cumberland Stakes
(2m.) same day, but Sir Leonard did. Five only saddled.
Party behind Sir L. gave out that he was simply going
for the prize, they hadn’t tried him over the distance and
he would carry little, if any, of their coin. Well pre-
served daughter of Carbine (La Carabine) was favorite, 6
to 4 against. Blue Metal made the going merry. Passing
6fur. post La Carabine showed in front and stayed there
round the bend—her ultimate success being uproariously
proclaimed—but there she swung wide, losing lengths, and
Sir Leonard shot up on the rails. Jock Lewis pulled La
Carabine together well and at the distance was with
Blue Metal (who also swerved) just behind Sir Leonard,
whom rider Bradshaw kept going without the
smallest slip and landed winner all out—a neck better
than Blue Metal, with La Carabine a head away. First
half-mile pace was slow but subsequently it became solid
enough for anything. Watch revealed 3.344.
* * * *
Second Steeple at Randwiek, Saturday, was
unsatisfactory. Top-weight (Brokerage) didn’t take any,
and Bay Eagle started hot at 5 to 4, while 2 to 1 was
greedily snapped about Grafter, who turned turtle on
Easter Monday. Race was robbed of all interest at the
outset through A.J.C. officials carelessly leaving a hurdle
in front of the first lep (“ the logs”), which jocks, inter-
preted as meaning that that obstacle was to be ignored.
Underwood, on Grafter, was the first to size things aright,
and, whipping round, cleared the jump 20 lengths ahead
of Sportsman, who negotiated the task a full furlong in
front of Bay Eagle and Oriel. Thence on, only Grafter
and Sportsman were in it. Bay Eagle slipped on the flat
in the second round, and, after being remounted, was
just negotiating the last task when Grafter passed the
post an easy winner from Sportsman.
* • * *
Onkaparinga (S. A.) Races —biggest one-day turf
fixture on the continent—drew 20,000 onlookers Easter
Monday. For the affluent Hurdles Holkar was a strong
first choice, but lightly treated. Lochiel gelding Sydney
sprung a huge surprise and won his first lepping event
after streaking in front all the way. Tote paid £l9 12s.
Melbourne Cup winner Auraria’s first foal—King Edward,
by Bill of Portland—was served up warm for Trial Stakes,
but Broken Hill-Compact colt Mt. Lofty got the money.
Onkaparinga Cup fell to Combat, one of the few of
Carnegie’s get in Australia. Maid of Honor is Combat’s
dam. Great Eastern ’Chase (1100 sovs. added money)
was the best thing of the bill. Ten carried silk. Chelsea
and Sindhis were favorites. Last round Chelsea fell at
the stone wall, and a broken leg necessitated destruction.
Regent and Ronald had the finish between them. A few
strides from the post they were together in a desperate
struggle, which went to Ronald by a neck. Div. was
£l4 17s.
» ( * * *
Stiff running was as glaring as ever at Rand-
wick last meeting, and more so than on suburban or
much-condemned pony courses. Most palpable cases
were some of the later winners, who did wonders com-
pared with efforts during previous days, when the
market indicated that they were not wanted, but too late
to save the bulk of public money. The daring of the
methods adopted evidences that- those closely concerned
are, at any rate, satisfied they run no risk of being car-
peted an inference supported by whispers all too
common—regarding the blinding of official vision.

The big Mo.’s intend having Sydney Tatts. to
themselves henceforth, i.e., unless lesser Mo., of City
Tatts., feels like planking down a cool century for the
privilege of daubing “Member of Tattersalls” on his
satchel, which isn’t likely, as he can do, and is doing, as
well without it. T’other day, big Tatts. held its annual
meeting, and decreed that henceforth entrance-fee for
“ books ” should be £lOO. Previously it was £5O, reduced
from £250, in order to lure members of the smaller insti-
tution over, and at the same time replenish practically
empty coffers. Some came, but, with the cessation of the
first rush, there were no more, and have been none for a
long time, nor was there any apparent possibility of an-
other enrolment—the rest deciding to stick to their own
flourishing concern. This swelling the fee is merely the
assumption of an exclusiveness which is really a necessity.
Water-pond lep at Agri. Society’s Show (brush
hurdle taken away) went to Skylark (Mrs. Elliott up),
with 21ft. lOin. Mrs. Stace, on Darkie, followed, covering
20ft. 7in. With brush hurdle replaced, Skylark improved
to 30ft. 7in. Same naff and rider cleared 31ft. last year.

Golden Gate A.C. was again piebald on Satur-
night, when Albury Clifford and negro Ike Stewart
scrapped for three rounds. Clifford, though clever,
shirks training drudgery and was far from properly fit.
His several victories in England suggest either that he
rose to the occasion there or that Bulldog bruising
material is degenerate. Clifford monkeyed about the
ring a lot with Stewart and landed often, but his punches
lacked thump and might have been mere whisks for all
the concern they' gave the black. White was down twice
unaccountably—-probably to recuperate—and at “ cor-
ners ” for the third round, after being on the floor Isecs.,
was still there, whereupon his s jnds threw in the rag
and the thing was over. Exit Clifford to swell the ever-
increasing crowd of pugilistic not-wanteds.
* * * •
Victorian spider-limbed pug Tim Hegarty went
under a second time in ’Frisco, Feb. 28, at the hands of
Britt, recently seceded from amateurism. Fight lasted
into the eighth round, when Hegarty owned a hatful of
points to the good ; a left hook on the chin dazed him,
and Britt, following up, soon had the Australian slugged
out. Hegarty never was a fighter, only a boxer pure and
simple, and a clever one at that, which is all right in a
gym. or with another of the same kidney.
Melb. pug., Jack M‘Gowan, had a lively time
hunting half-hearted Rocks bruiser. Jack Blackmore,
around Gaiety A.C. 20ft. ring last week. Time after time
Jack swung right or left at vacancy through t’other’s
disinclination to get within range. Wiles and coaxings
availed naught, and when Blackmore did get near enough
accidentally he’d flop on all-fours through sheer nervous-
ness apparently. Once M'Gowan (grown careless and
disgusted) left an wide opening, and Blackmore chipped a
champion hook on the chin with the sinister limb.
M'Gowan sagged and sank amid white-heat excitement
till gong-sound—4see. Subsequently the thing was
devoid of incident. M'Gowan chased and the Sydney
man ran and eluded with dreadful monotony till the 20
rounds’ journey had been negotiated, when referee Daw-
son declared a draw. Decision was unfair. M'Gowan
had won. He wanted to fight all the time and did his
best, but the other fellow simply wouldn’t.
* * * •
The bruising struggle for alleged Australian
“ fedder” supremacy on Monday night, at Sydney Gaiety
A.C. between Melbourne “ Micko ” Walsh and Sydney
“ Bob ” Turner, was crowded at 10s. and ss. Something
in excess of twice the £l5O purse given must have been
subscribed. Conditions bound both to weigh 9st. Walsh
was 6Jlb. under, but Turner brought the balance to 9.3,
which cost him £25 and left the title out of the
question completely. From first to last it was
the hottest and most vigorous bruising thing seen
here for years. Walsh (5 to 2 on) set a breath-
snatching pace, and sustained it marvellously. Turner’s
response was full of ginger. Excitement was strained
throughout. “ Micko’s ” best work was in-fight-
ing. While Turner clinched, t’other would operate with-
right and left on stomach and ribs as if controlled by a
gas-engine. The crowd hooted this, though it was fair as
could be—rules allow punching with both hands free—
but by-and-bye, when Walsh found Sydney’s crack
warmer than anticipated, he hit foul—very low—often,
and was cautioned at least a dozen times, till referee
Baxter, who had a rough time separating the
pair of bruiser bullpups, disqualified the Melbournian in
the ninth round. Neither could have knocked the other
out (provided condition stuck to both) inside the limit
(20 rounds) and it is hard to say who would have won on
points. Each has only one hand to rave about—Turner the
left, and Walsh the right. Left side of Turner’s body and
that portion over the kidneys were all aglow from Walsh’s
attentions ; Turner must be well ribbed up to have stood
that trouncing. Melbourne confined his operations to
the body mainly, and Sydney indulged mostly in left
jabs to face, with an occasional hook on the ribs. Walsh’s
classic mug was a picture afterwards, and a skin-graze
on the neck (inches wide and long) indicated what the
hugging had been to him. Turner’s showing proved an
eye-opener to at least nine-tenths of the house, who
looked upon t’other man as a “ moral” ; but it bore out
prediction printed herein 5,4/’O2.

Annual N.S.W.-Victorian bowling matches be-
gan in Sydney on Tuesday last week when the first leg of
the rub. was decided. Conditions exacted 25 heads.
N.S.W., though leading by four at the close of the 20th’
was three behind at the finish. Only successful rink on
the vanquished side was Waverley “skips.” Kondio’s
which accumulated 29 to Neill’s 14. Second meeting
(Wednesday) produced close play only through the initial
half hour. After 15 heads N.S.W. was 23 to the good
five later its advantage was 39, and this it hung to
till the last, winning in every rink. Coker’s four had the
biggest surplus (34 to 15) with Kondio’s handy (34 to 18).
The final tussle on St. George green, at Rockdale (Thurs-
day) was sensational. Twentieth head totals gave N.S.W.
a majority of 21, and its prospects looked more than
rosy. Two heads from the finish the home players were
15 to the good but disaster set in subsequently, for in the
next couple of counts over 16 were notched against them
thus leaving the visiting side winners by 1. Towards this
fours captained by Waxman, Hansen, Wood and Neill
(Victoria) scored 5,5, 3, 3 respectively, opposed to Coker
Bradbury, Kondio and Wilkie as named. Until the last
shot (Neills), in the last iink left on the green had
stopped rolling, 90 all seemed sure, for N.S.W. lay two
but Neill’s effort cleverly trailed the jack 2ft. and cut
N.S.W. out of one. In the finishing quintette of heads
Victoria was credited with 32 to 10. Kondio again did
best for N.S.W., gaining 27 to Woods’ 17. Something in
N.S.W. s favor is the fact that the grand total of the
rub: gives it 36 points in excess of the visitors’ com-
pilation. Up to now, of 25 matches contested, Victoria
has succeeded in 16.

Big Tas. cricketer Eady opened out his shoul-
ders and smote the leather with a big smite last and pre-
vious Saturdays. Playing with Break o’ Day against
Clubs for supremacy of South Tas., he piled
up 419 first day and, resuming next week-end, brought
the figures to 566 before getting stumped. What an end-
ing to such an innings 1 This is easily ahead of the best
previous Australian individual score (417 not out) owned
by Worrall. Prior to Eady’s feat the biggest compilation
ever made in Tasmania was li. Burn’s 365. The world’s
record (628) belongs to youthful Collins. Eady’s side
notched 911 against its oppone-ts’ 277, of which one
--(K. Buni)supplied 161. Thj big scorer also distin-
guished himself bowling, gathering in 7 for 87.

Double Bay (Sydney) sculler Pearce, in his first
match with English crack Barry, last month, started
exceptionally warm in the betting, as much as sto 1
being laid on him. He got to the front early and stayed
there for nearly a mile, when Barry came full of go and
Pearce cracked up badly, leaving the Britisher to paddle
to the finish as he liked.
* * * *
At last Riverview (Sydney) regatta, the spec-
tacle of big, hairy oarsmen, dressed in a very small allow-
ance of very thin rag, shambling out of their boats on to
a platform, in full view of a crowd of womenfolk, was not
a pleasant one. If rowers must strip themselves practi-
cally stark for the boat, they should at least have the
decency to don an.overcoat or a dressing-gown, or even a
pair of pyjamas, if they have to land and mingle with
women.
* * * *
Bill Beach, from his Dapto retreat, last week,
made his periodical swoop on N.S.W. Gun Club’s Easter
Gift trophy, which is supposed to carry 100 sovs. added
money. Bill, though the absolute winner, only raked in
£l7 10s. through the “ divy.” system, now so common
among alleged sportsmen who affect pigeon-slaughtering.
Modus is, no matter what the amount offered, to “ cut ”
something each after a few drop out, and then, when
another few go, have a further slice, and so on, till the
last few are shooting for a cheap pot (stated worth of
trophy in Beach’s case was only a tenner) or a paltry few
pounds. There’s no greater sure-thing battler than the
present-day pigeon-shooter as a whole, though, in most
instances, he is particularly well-fixed financially. One
can number genuine sports among these bird-murderers
on the fingers of one hand. Beach ought to have gone
home with the hundred as well as the pot, but they
wouldn’t let him. One or two propose—often beg for —a
division, and the real brand sport doesn’t like to refuse
for fear of being dubbed selfish. Same day, “Kosciusko”
(a leading Sydney photographer) came out well—grassing
40 of 42 fired at from 28yds. and actually closed on double
the money available for the winner by carrying his two
noms. to the last division.

Annual Australian ped. institution—Stawell
(Vic.) A.C. Easter meet —was more successful this year
than ever previously, and brought competitors from all
parts. Entries exceeded prior records and “ gate ” topped
last year’s substantially. Day’s programme, all foot-
running, included 38 events. Winner (Tredennick) of
the big thing (Stawell Easter Gift) is among the greatest
outsiders that have succeeded. At card-call only very
little business was done against, and then more through
the temptation of long odds than aught else. Surviving
the third round, however, made him warm, and then
Tredennick money was fairly plentiful; but the bulk of
backing came from the public and not from the winner’s
party, who are reported to have only scooped £IOO over
and above the prize. Tredennick is a clerk, living at
Malvern (Vic.); he is 27 years old and sft. Bin.
high. His mark was lljyds. and the time put up
for the distance (130yds.) 12 1-5 secs. Stawell Easter
Gift has, since ’BS, only been won three times by men
behind 10yds. Clark (7yds.) was first home in ’B6,
jMurkan Skinner (a big “ Botany” winner) succeeded
in ’B9 off 9yds., while Joy (syds.) secured the money in ’95.

Henry, aboriginal bowler, of Brisbane, is a
slightly larger edition of Marsh, the N.S.W. trundler.
Both take a long, flat-footed run prior to a lightning
delivery. Each bowled the other at recent inter-State
match and gloried in the feat. Henry is far ahead of
Marsh as a batsman.
The latest pair of Queensland bush-
rangers seem to have murdered a policeman and a
squatter, burnt the bodies, and then gathered up
the. ashes into a bag and strapped it across the
policeman’s horse, which they then set adrift.
Either that was done as a hideous act of bravado,
or else the horse bolted with its ghastly burden
while the remains were being carried off for con-
cealment. Among the ashes, bones, teeth and
buttons were found.
The Mayor of Sydney promises to “hit
hard ” in the enforcement of sanitary precautions.
He has already 5000 names down for prosecution
for offences against the health-laws, and expects
to keep a special court very busy for many months.
Fines are to be energetically pressed for, and,
judging from the exemplary penalties (£lO and
£5 mostly, with respective alternatives of eight
and four weeks’ gaol) inflicted in a few prelimi-
nary-canter cases already heard, there will be a
big clean-up when the dirty brigade has been
cyanided.
Of the horrible outraging and murder
of a young girl near Brisbane a Hindu,
who haunted the vicinity for some time pre-
viously and who has now vanished, is suspected.
In an amazingly large proportion of Queensland
crimes a nigger of some kind is suspected, though,
very often, by reason of his close resemblance to
other niggers, he is never identified. On Ist Jan.,
1900 (the latest date for which the Statistics op
Queensland supply information) there were 551
convicted prisoners in Queensland gaols, and of
these 104 were Asiatics or Kanakas. That is to say,
these races form less than 5 per cent, of the popu-
lation and they supply nearly 20 per cent, of the
convicted criminals, which means that they are
about four times as criminal as the white race.
Also only one of these niggers was confined for the
minor offence of drunkenness ; the great bulk were
in for more serious offences.
Mr. Frank Cotton writes :
Editor Bulletin, — I desire to draw the attention of the
heads of the Christian Churches to the recent utterances
of the Premier of Maoriland, who is about Cos attend the
Coronation ceremonies in a representative capacity. Mr.
Seddon declares that there has been too much “ kid-glove
sentimentality ” in the conduct of the S’African War, and
suggests that Maori troops, led by native leaders, should
be given a free-hand in S’Africa, unhampered by the
usages of civilised warfare, for the purpose of forcing the
war to a conclusion. As the representatives of the various
churches throughout. Australasia have at different times
passed resolutions dealing with the S’African War, I
respectfully invite an expression of opinion from them,
in their capacity as moral guardians of the community,
upon the above brutal and infamous proposal.
The Bulletin devoutly hopes to hear of the
parsons holding forth on this next Sunday.

Premier “ Toby ” Barton — easy-going,
fat, gentle, unpractical, drifting “ Toby ’’—has
been invited to England.to drop in at the Corona-
tion and discuss the advisability of the Common-
wealth paying more naval subsidy and contri-
buting in other ways to the defence of the Empire
—also, probably, to consider the advisability of
sacrificing a lot of customs revenue by admitting
British goods at reduced rates. He has been
invited to drop in on Canada as he comes back,
and discuss the advisability of losing a lot more
revenue by admitting Canadian goods at reduced
rates. He has been invited to drop in on Japan
(though there are hopes that he may not go) and
discuss the advisability of losing still more re-
venue by letting Jap. goods in at reduced rates,
and also the advisability of destroying the White
Australia policy by admitting the Jap. himself.
And constant dropping wears away many things;
It would be wise to ask Toby Barton how much
be will take to drop the whole business and stay
at home.
At Hobart town-liall, t’other night, ex-
Premier Braddon alluded to Labor-leader
Watson’s power in the Reps., and, in reply to
interjections, admitted that Watson was “a good
man.” “ But,” he added, “Mr. Watson repre-
sents a party that is inimical to the best interests
of the Commonwealth.” Same old Tory tara-
diddle ; but Braddon repeated it in the manner of
an ancient man trying to voice an almost forgotten
slab of childhood’s catechism. There was no vigor
in the utterance—-it was clear that the veteran felt
half ashamed of returning to the claptrap “ flati-
tudes ” of his political youth. One short year in
Federal politics has taught the wily old warrior
that even a Laborite may be “a good man,” and
that the Tory tradi tions of the house of Braddon
must be kept strictly for private use, in order to
avoid ridicule. Brave old man Braddon is several
milestones past seventy, but if he stays in the
Reps, for another year or so he may yet become
honorary vice-president of a Wharf Laborers
Union. It will be a bit late for him to start on a
career of democratic usefulness, but better late
than never.

George Leake, Westralian Premier, has
been having a high time in Melbourne. He has
been in daily confab with Barton, and some people
connect his visit with approaching changes in the
Federal Ministry. They reason it out thus :—
If Forrest becomes High Commissioner which
the Lord forbid ! —the Ministry will want another
Westralian to fill his place. Leake is Premier at
E resent, and what more likely than that be should
e asked to join ? Leake himself has openly
stated that he did not mean to remain long in
Westralian politics, and hinted that he desired to
go back to his practice as a barrister. But
there is a blemish in Leake’s little story, for he
never had much bar practice. He was Crown
Solicitor—a Public Service job—when Forrest was
supreme. He left the service to go into politics
and become Opposition leader —and he has
been in politics ever since. Leake was a member
of the Federal Convention, and if Barton further
betrays the true interests of Australia by sending
Forrest to London as High Commissioner, more
unlikely things might happen than Leake be-
coming a member of the Federal Government.

Very slowly the British are beginning to
realise the magnitude of the S’African contract.
West of the Capetown-Mafeking railway lies a
vast stretch of territory which ends at the Atlantic
Ocean or the German frontier. It is sparsely
populated, much of it unexplored, and somewhat
similar to our own Northern Territory. Much of
it is actually within Cape Colony proper, and the
rest is part of the British Bechuanaland Pro-
tectorate. What inhabitants there are are Boers,
and while Ketchener grapples with the elusive
De Wet and De la Rey a handful of farmers have
declared a new republic in this territory, and are
importing arms and ammunition, and Ketchener
hasn’t a man to send against them. _ This has
been going on for some time. The idea of the
Boers actually founding a new republic in British
territory is a strange upending of things.

Concerning the election of a successor to
the late Mr. Piesse, M.H.R.: —
The Tas. mining centres went almost solid for Laborist
Whitelaw, at the recent Federal bye-election ; though
the Tatters-all Govt, crowd sent the weightiest emis-
saries it could find to try and seduce the miners. But
the one-horse villages of the interior were just as unani-
mous for the Tory Hartnoll, and crushed the progressive
miners by sheer weight of accumulated fossil. Generally
stated throughout Tas. that the Tattersi.ll contingent
spent £3000 over the election. Three Senators (M'Gregor,
O’Keefe, and Stewart) voyaged to Tasmania at the last
moment to give the Laborite a lift. They helped him
undoubtedly, but this jumping into a fray at the last
moment is not good business in a place like Tasmania.
O’Malley knew' his people, and put in solid months at
steady organisation work ere asking them to vote. He
won. The Tas. electors are no less amenable to demo-
cratic reason than those of other States, but they mustn’t
be rushed.

A prosecutrix (the last syllable being par-
ticularly expressive in her case), whose name is
not given in the newspaper reports, out of con-
sideration for her feelings, at the Adelaide
Criminal Court on April 3, charged a youth of 24
with criminal assault. Evidence showed that she
had previously had an unauthorised baby, and
that she “accosted” accused on the night, in
question. Jury returned a verdict of not guilty,
and the lady went scot free—as usual.

Even unto old age you may feel
the vigor of youth, with its light
heart, elastic step, courage and
tireless energy. You may be free from
pains and aches and defy your years.
There is a fountain of perpetual youth, and you have only to reach out your hand

your might you will proclaim, as other men have,
“I AM A MAN ! ”
Like the vigorous giants of old, you can be in your prime at sixty—strong, vigorous
and full of youthful enthusiasm.

lam very well satisfied. Your splendid Belt has removed the pain from my back and legs.”

can defy your years.

Languid
Condition
“ Doctor said there was one thing
which might do me good —Scott’s
Emulsion ”
Life has a poor lookout when you are too
languid to do anything —too languid even to
wish to do anything ! Such a condition is
usually a legacy from anaemia, and if not
corrected leads to disaster. Here is the
story of disaster averted :
“ 29, George How, Dockhead,
“ London, England, June 10th, 1901.
“ Dear Sirs,—Last autumn I suffered very
badly from the effects of anaemia, which was
followed by lung trouble, and I got into a
very weak and languid condition. It was
positively too much trouble for me to do any-
thing, as I could not exert myself in the
least. I had to take to my bed; yet I did
not make the slightest progress. In October
my doctor said there was one thing which
K
YM
il i\V «\
\\V\ \'
MISS N. BOOTH.
(From a photograph.)
might do me good, and that was Scott’s
Emulsion. I acted upon his advice, gave
the preparation a fair trial, and am heartily
glad to be able to say the results have been
most satisfactory, for Scott’s Emulsion was
successful where everything else had failed.
I can truly say that I never felt so well as
I do now, and I cannot help thinking Scott’s
Emulsion has been the means of saving my
life. I consider your preparation a wonder-
fully strengthening and invigorating remedy,
and would certainly advise all suffering from
chest and lung troubles to give it a trial.
“ Yours truly,
“(Signed) (Miss) N. Booth.”
Miss Booth’s story is a story often told by
those who write to us—that a doctor pre-
scribed Scott’s Emulsion. He could, of
course, had he thought wise, have prescribed
plain cod-liver oil, or any of the many sub-
stitutes and imitations of Scott’s Emulsion.
But he did not; he told Miss 80. th that
there was one thing which might do her
good—Scott’s Emulsion.
The doctor, like doctors everywhere who
prescribe Scott’s Emulsion, had the best of
reasons for prescribing this remedy, because
Scott’s Emulsion is the highest and most
valuable form in which cod-liver oil is to bo
obtained nowadays. Thousands of doctors
admit this. Then, additionally, all the nasty
taste and smell of cod-liver oil is eliminated
by the Scott & Bowne process, and the
virtues of cod-liver oil are reinforced with
hypophosphites of lime and soda, the first of
the bone and tissue builders.
If you are languid, anaemic, listless, and
without strength to meet the round of daily
cares, take Scott's Emulsion, and you will
be quickly cured. But you must remember
what the doctor advised Miss Booth : that
there is “ one thing—Scott’s Emulsion
that will help in such cases.
To secure the relief Miss Booth had, see
that on the wrapper of the bottle there is a,
picture of a man carrying a big codfish ; if
they are there you have Scott’s Emulsion.
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpence to Scott and Bowne,
483 Kent street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

PURVEYORS BY SPECIAL APPOINTMENT TO HIS MAJESTY THE KING.
At Poverty Point.
A business-man in a Vic. town rented a room adjoining
his premises to a showman, who exhibited a monster
shark. Summer weather does not improve dead shark,
and on the fourth day the exhibitor levanted with his
takings, leaving the exhibit as security for the rent. Ke-
moval and burial of the shark cost the business-man A.l.
A friend to whom he lamented his loss told him that, it
he had removed the shark’s teeth before burying it, the
proceeds would have paid all expenses and left a margin
of profit. Taking the hint, the business-man paid 109.
more to have the shark exhumed, and found that tne
showman had removed the teeth before leaving. Next
“ shark ” exhibitor who comes to that town will have to
pay rent “ strictly in advance,” and give security for re-
moval of exhibit when over-ripe.
The Italian opera-singers had some peculiar little
ways about them. One of them nearly died of shock
through the recklessness of the little Australienne who
was house-keeping for him. The singer would arrive home
about midnight, and after vin ordinaire, maccarom, and
ze pea-nut, would put the light put while he removed his
valuable gems and placed them in safety. Even his tempo-
rary spouse never knew where he concealed the jewellery,
till she found out accidentally one morning. They
slept in a top back room, and when an inebriated and
playful couple and several musically-inclined cats roused
the echoes of the night, the little Australienne arose in
indignation and reached excitedly for something to
throw out, by way of a hint. The singer sprang
from his couch and reminded her the wine-bottle was
not empty, and begged her to throw something belonging
to the landlady. Disregarding his entreaties, she heaved
the first thing her hand touched, and it was the Italian
gentleman’s boot. It crashed down into the lane, the
cats fled, the two drunks, firmly believing the sky had
fallen, crept silently away, and the neighbors lay and
shivered with apprehension. In the morning the singer
arose and noticed, with a scream of horror, that one of
his boots was missing. His wife saw him clutch the tmng
and dive one fat, flabby paw into the interior, and with-
draw it with a cry of despair. Then he shot through the
door, she heard him falling downstairs, heard the back
gate wrenched off its hinges, and presently he came
back, laughing, crying, and doing several other things at
the same time, and nursing his beloved boot, from which
he extracted the sock and then shook out his jewellery.
It was his safe-deposit.
“ V.” : It was in the gallery, at “ Boheme.’ Just
in front of us were several Dagos, who rose excitedly
during the second act to cry “ Brava !” to Dam. They
remained standing and obstructing our stage view. An
emphatic request made them all sit down again, except
one man, who took no notice. We levelled sarcasm at
the offender. We called him “Signor Peanuto, and
requested him to be seated. He disregarded our petition.
We called him “ largera plata,” ‘ ‘ stele o’ the ovst, * iji-
a-banan,” and several other Dago expressions, without
avail. We called him “Vesuvius” and ‘ Julius Oaisar.
A riot was narrowly averted (they always are) by the
opaque one turning slowly round and remarking, quietly,
“ Oh, go to ’ell !” He was an Australian. We
might have guessed it.
From a South African correspondent: “ Miss Annie
Mavor, well known to Australians, is doing well in Natal
and Cape Colony with a repertoire similar to Nance
O’Neil’s. Nance O’Neil was to open at Mantzburg on
Jan. 13th. She leaves Natal for Egypt and London—
appearing at the Lyceum. Thomas Kingston is down
with enteric fever, which is having a good innings here
just now.”
The trial in which Wallace Brownlow got a verdict for
£1282 against the management of a Vic. hotel for having
left unlocked a dangerous door through which he fell,
added a phrase to the literature of drink. Mr. Brownlow,
according to one witness, has a pet name for a long
whiskj’ ” —“ a good rozzler.” The baritone established it
as a fact, however, that he didn’t rozzle-dozzle to a
muddling extent, either as a habit or on the day of the
accident. He might have called many more witnesses
than Henry Bracy to prove that his voice was seriously
affected by the accident.
With the World’s Entertainers at Sydney Palace is Mr.
Rodney, a singer and patter comedian. His talk is
amusing enough, but he makes the awful mistake of
singing two inane Jingo ditties for the musical part of
his turn ; at which the gallery eats apples and the d.c.
yawns. Theatre Jingoism is a year or more out of date
in Australia.
Imported soloists—tenors, sopranos, baritones—are now
features of both the Sydney music halls. Seeing that
Australia is just now a big exporter of song-birds and
local people could be easily got to surpass most of the
imported vocalists, it seems bad business to bring them
all the way from America.
“A.M.” : In “The Wrong Mr. Wright,” at Melbourne
Biiou, Miss Barton has a good part, and makes fair use of
her opportunities. She looks tail* graceful and extremely
decorative, except when trying too hard to be sinuous.
Holding a blotting-pad up to a mirror to spy on its im-
pressions, she sticks her head forward like a goose.
Sydney girls on the boards seem addicted to these Sara
Bernhardt, Brown Potter, serpentine, eelish, wrigglesome
attitudes. Inez Bensusan at times stretches her neck
forward and squirms as if bent on worming her way head-
foremost out of her clothes. There was a time when
Melbourne stage-neophytes mostly blinked their eyes and
pursed their lips, ala Nellie Stewart. Rose Musgrove
still shows a trace of that habit. Now, local beginners
keep in mind Nance O’Neil—bent forward in her chair,
hands clasped round knees, eyes glued to an imaginary
fat pittite in the far beyond—that was Nance’s patent
“ lion-about-to-spring ” posture. Others reproduce Lillah
M'Oarthy, with her elbow propped on the table. To this
cult belonged the young thing who did Lady Olivia
Vernon in “ Sweet Nell.”

A SLUGGISH LIVER.
Newcastle Proof for
Newcastle Readers.
A Lesson Worth Learning 1 .
The Caee of
Mr. D. LEACH.
(BY A NEWCASTLE REPORTER.)
Tho work of the press is usually of in-
finite public benefit in its immediate re-
buts, as it paves the way for all those bene-
ficent reforms to which all progressive
people are aspiring. Complete evidence was
lately obtained Ly a reporter from Mr.
J amel Leach, of Downie-street, Smedmore,
Newcastle, to justify the above opinion, but
to those to whom the subject is unfamiliar a
perusal of the-Words of Mr. Leach is re-
spectfully commended.
„ “ W he .“ 1 lef t Ratcliffe,” said Mr. Leach,
which is a small town about seven miles
from Manchester, I came straight to New-
castle and have been working'there ever
since, which embraces a period of a dozen
years Three years prior to that I was in a
very bad state of health, my internal organs
being so much upset after an accident in
which my shoulder bone was broken that I
could not get my food to agreed with me
I suffered from a sluggish liver, it was evi-
dent, and the drowsiness which came over
me after meals was distressing ”
‘ ‘ u hafc sor t of an appetite did you have ? ”
en pared the reporter. j
“Avery fickle one. Some days I felt
hungry and so.. e days I had no appetite at
all. When I had eaten freely my stomach
became bloated out, and I felt considerable
pain as if the food was fermenting. If I ate
but very little the same unpleasantness was
experienced, so it was quite plain that in-
digestion was one of my ailments. I becaane
so melancholy that I lost all interest and
pleasure in life, and the awful depression in
my spirits was even worse when I got up to
work in the mornings, as my loss of energy
.vas so great that I felt thoioughly incap-
able of lasting the days out. livery evening
I returned home feeling very weary and
fagged out, there not being a drain of
vitality left in my system; but the curious
part of it was that I could not sleep when I
went to bed. My brain was never at rest,
as I started thinking directly my head
touched the pillow, and as the hours
dragged slowly along my misery increased,
for when the clock pointed to the hour to
arise my limbs were aching intensely, and I
aas almost too exhausted to get up. The
only variety I had was by occasionally fall-
ing asleep for a little time, and then the
most unhappy dreams came to my mind and
woke me abruptly £ My tongue was as dry
as a piece of voo.t, and I also had a foul
taste in the mouth. A fearful pain in my
chest came after I had eaten a little food,
and those pains went right through to the
back and hurt me between the shoulders.
Sometimes there was a sinking sensation in
my stomach as if I were on the point of
starvation, but when food was placed before
mel sickened at the sight of it. I lingered on
like this irom w. ek to week, suffering acutely
from aches across my loins, and anxious
fears obtruded on my mind because of my
he >rt palpitating. This generally happened
when I had been exerting myself by walk-
ing a little distance, but at other times my
heart started beating and thumping with-
out any apparent cause. A strange feeling
came to my eyes, as if they were swollen,
and some days my sight was so bad that
everything 1 looked at appeared to be
clo ked in a mist. t You must know that 1
was quite a young man when alt this trouble
came, and it was a serious thing for me to
be wasting away like I was, and with my
nervous system so terribly affected that.l
was set all of a shake by the slightest noise,
besides continually fearing that something
of a dreadful nature was going to take place.
Fhe irregular condition of my bowels was
very injurious, but what was I to do T
Nothing gave me permanent relief from con-
stipation, and the same remark applies to
all my ailments. As I had to work for my
living my daily loss of strength was making
my future look very black, and anybody
who lias suff ied like I have can understand
what a matter for great rejoicing it was
when I found a medicine to give me re-
lief.”
“ Was it prescribed for you ? * asked the
newspaper man.
“No. I was very fortunate in savin®
doctor’s expenses, as the medicine I took
was simply thiough reading some of the ex-
traordinary cures which had been eflectec
by using Clements lonic. U hat had cui ed
others might possibly help me, I thought,
so I sent for some Clements Tonic an began
to take it. When I had drunk two bottles
my appetite had so improved that I cu d
eat nearly as well as ever, ami tire p .ins
were not half so bad as they had been. I
was quite satisfied with the advance 1 had
already made, and did not lose any time in
providing myself with more Clements
.lonic, which steadily imparted vigor to my
weakened system, and as 1 gut the fub
benefit of the food I could now relish I need
hardly tell you that I was soon strong
enough to work all day without? f eling the
least distress. To promote digestion 1 found
that Clements Tonic could uot be equalled,
as there were none of those lnconven cnees
resulting from flatulence after I had been
taking that medicine a while, and my chest
was quite free from the oppression which
had almost smothered me. Bodily pains
were gradually extinguished by Clements
lonic, and I was exceedingly glad to fiud
that my heart pal itations had ceased to
exist, while as a nerve-toning medicine I am
sure I was taking the best it was possible to
obtain, for my nervousness disappeared
completely, and my system was invigorated
throughout. It was a remarkable i estora-
tion to health which I experienced through
using Clements Tonic, which gave me per-
fect freedom from all my ailments, and as I
continued to use that medicine till my
health was sound in every way it is only
fair to give all the credit to Clements Tonic.”
“ Have you had any illness since then ? ”
“A few years ago I had four of my ribs
broken, and my system was broken down
again for a while. Indeed, I got intensely
weak and miserable, but I knew what to
fall back upon by my first experience, and
with the use of a few bottles of Clements
lonic I was made strong and vigorous as
before. Working in the mines is very ex-
hausting labor at the best of times, but in
summer it is particularly hard on the con-
stitution, and when I feel that the strain is
commencing to tell on my strength I get a
bottle or two of Clements Tonic to keep my-
self in proper working order, and the result
is that I am always healthy and happy,”
“ You have adopted a wise plan Mr
Leach.”
“I know that, and others would be
better off if they followed my example; but
when they have read these remarks, which
you can publish in any way, they can judge
for themselves.” ,
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Daniel Leach, of Dowme-street, Smedmore,
near Newcastle, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and sin-
cerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting: of four folios, arid
consecutively numbered from one to four, and that
it contains and is a true and faithful account of my
illness and cure by Clement- Tonic, and also contains
my full permission to publish in any way my
statements—which I give voluntarily, without re-
ceiving any payment; and I make this solemn de-
claration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oat bs
Act, 1900.
Meowed 2*
Subscribed and declared
twenty-third day of October,
hundred and one, before me,
at Smedmore, this
one thousand nine

Dr. Roth, Northern Protector of Abori-
fNQ’).T!' teS t 0 THE Bdlletin fr «“ Cooktown
“ Trotter’s ” account of the Maytown blacks (8., 15/2/’O2)
is a malicious and deliberate falsehood. The blacks are
not diseased, so far as outward appearances will show,
and sores and swarms of flies are not on the blacks.
HnnrfZJrmt 6 f es st ? rvin £ at Maytown, as 1501 b. of
3 | lb ; of tobacco is distributed monthly amongst
about 50 adults and 17 children, who are unable to obtain
employment and are travelling about the district. [There
is surely some mistake here in the Doctor’s letter
(type-written); 1501 b. of flour a month would give 51b.
a day among 67 people !] A number of blacks are em-
ployed by white people, but none by Chinese, whom
the police have prohibited from having blacks about
them premises No doubt the Chinese harbor and
cohabit with blacks (and white men do likewise) at
the outside camps, but they watch for a time when the
police are not in the vicinity. On account of the Chinese
a ', ''’7, tes . b ? ln " spread over a large area of the Palmer
goldfield, it.is impossible to keep a vigilant watch on
their doings at such outside camps. The natives are not
to be seen near the camps when the police are patrolling.
Chinese and blacks cannot be seen smoking opium toge-
ther in dens, nor can opium be found on the blacks or in
their camps, notwithstanding the police are continually
endeavoring to obtain opium by aboriginal agents from
those Chinese who are suspected of supplying it to the
blacks. Naked blacks can be seen in their camps and out
hunting, but not in the town, as they all have somecloth-
ing. A naked pickaninny may occasionally be seen in
town with its mother, but it invariably wears a loin-cloth.
The only cows milked at Maytown for the past three
years and five months have been two : and this was a
couple of years ago, by Mrs. Brown, wife of the clerk of
the Hann Divisional Board, who milked them herself.
Ihe blacks in the Maytown district are as healthy as
possible, excepting an old one or two, and a few occa-
sional cases of fever, which are relieved by the constable
from the supply of medicine received from the Govern-
ment.

THE CONDUCTOR OF AN ORCHESTRA
AND THE iEOLIANIST.
jEOLIAN
ORCHESTRELLE, STYLE GRECIAN
Does the conductor of an orchestra
Experience the pleasure incident
To the producing of music? .
Did you ever stop to consider this?
Did you ever wonder what would be the
Effect should the master drop the
Baton? Would not the inspiration to the
Men be gone? Would not the result
Be colorless and mechanical? Watch
The effect of his controlling spirit;
See how he “leads in” the different parts
At the right moment. Should the
Strings waver or hesitate, see how, with
Almost hypnotic force, he inspires
His men. Should the brass predominate,
Watch with how gentle a hand he
Subdues the tone to its proper balance.
He makes the music ensemble.
Would you not enjoy the ability.
To produce these musical results *
You can do this with an ALolian
For with it you become virtually
An orchestral leader.
The relation of the performer
To the instrument is exactly the same as
That of the conductor to his orchestra.
Neither the conductor nor the iEolianist
Produces a single tone.
The perforated music-roll performs
The duty of the men. Your pait
Is to bring in at the proper time,
As indicated on the roll, the proper
effects- i.e.y
String, wood, wind or brass.
Should the tempo drag, by means of
One of the stops you quicken the music.
Do you need a greater volume of tone?
Do you wish to produce a delicate
Tianissimo, the touch of the hand and
You have the effect. Thus the
iEolian enables you to produce, with a
Few weeks’ practice, results which usually
Are obtained only by life-long study.
The Orchestrelle is a new type
Of which, every one who ha
Not yet had an opportunity to
Pass judgment upon it should hear..
If you are not able to call and examine
This wonderful instrument
Write for our illustrated pamphlet.

A Literary Dinner in London.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, and winter !
It was a violet-mist of a day. I was to be
dressed at half-past five. Mr. and Mrs. Lusao
were to call for me at that time. I flicked-on my
electric light, and drew down my blinds.
I looked out for a moment. Oh, City of London .
the Poem you are, the infinite strange Poem .
There was a time when I said to myself I wou
never love any other living bit of the world but
Sydney. I pay for that vain promise. Everyday
London creeps more and more lastingly into my
blood. Every day my pulse beats with a stronger
and stronger sense of her rhythm. E J.
take her nearer and nearer to my heart, I sway to
her beauty, I fall beneath her spell.
And now, in these lovely winter days, when the
city lies shrouded in grey mist with a violet note
in ft—o r yellow fog that turns the stern houses
into fairy palaces, and lets their light shine
through Is if suspended in the air like lanterns in
a garden —I say to myself over and over again,
“Oh, London ! how did I ever live without you .
As’l looked from my window for a moment
there flashed across me, unthinking, afrotworkof
bare black trees in the square below, beyond
the fretwork some lingering rose from a sun that
set an hour ago. And I said to myself, How
happy lam ! How lucky ! ,
For London-in-the-Mists is my London, place o
dreams. The thought of leaving her is terrible.
Ah, if you knew the poem-London ! I remem-
ber the glad surprise it was to me. I walk about
asking myself “Why did no one ever tell me?
lam not free yet from that wonder. Why did
no one ever tell me ? ” It flashes across me still,
some moment when London is lovelier than ever
purple, tender-a mist, a city, a soul all in one,
with her sky of grey chiffon, and her strange, dull,
orange suns.
* » * *
In due time I was ready ; Mr. and Mrs.
Lusac came, and off we set. Mr. Lusac is the
well-known publisher of Oriental and continental
books. My invitation came from one of his
writers, Dr. Francis Robert Harper, a /lan-
guished Orientalist, deep in Semitic work, that
brings him over here to the British Museum ,
was called away to Rome a few days bofore the
dinner, but the Lusacs were kind enough to take
m Itwas the Xmas dinner of the White Friars Club.
Who are the White Friars? A purely literary
body, although their chief raison detre is to eat a
dinner together every Friday night. Twice a
year only, ladies are invited ; this was one of the
tW Our cab stopped somewhere in the gay, glitter-
ing cab-tangled Strand. Up red-carpeted steps
we climbed. Mrs. Lusac and I went to a cloak-room
to take off our wraps. In great curiosity I looked
round and saw all sorts and conditions of women
thoro all save one, that is. . , _
But she has ceased to exist. Did she ever exist, I
wonder? Was she not someone else than a
Literary Woman—the short-haired female with her
clothes flung at her and catching her by Divine
Providence as it were, big feet, big hands, etc. . I
believe she never was a Literary Woman at all.
She was just a woman with short hair and big
feet. The confusion arose in the brain of a
Li S these women are dressed as if they had
thought a great deal of the effect they would make.
That woman in pink, for instance a rippling,
flouncing frock of chiffon, and a wavy golden
held and a lot of shoulders-has certainly, given
much care to her frocking, Yet she writes I
couldn’t tell you how many books a year Her
name is Marie Conor Leighton. One ot her
novels is “A Napoleon of the Press,” which is
said to be a fearfully life-like picture of Alfred
Harinsworth. They say that before it came out
the Great Alfred went round to all the reviewers,
and asked them not to notice that book and when
it anneared it went without reviews. She and her
husband, the London manager for Putnam Sons
write for Answers. Said that they make a great
ail the others I see a face, one face,
thlt attracts, and haunts-a lit tie, dark pensive
face full of sweetness, full of dreams. It is
opposite me at dinner, and I often study it instead
of my Xmas turkey, or even my dinner com-
panions, though both these are first-rate.
She is an Irishwoman, was Dora Siggerson, is
now Mrs. Clement Shorter, is dressed in black,
with a lace berthe, and has curly dark hair, and
deep dark grey eyes. On her left sits her husband,
the redoubtable’clement Shorter, ed.tor of the
Sketch the Sphere and the Tatler. His thick
haf is as black as night, and frizzly-curly. They
both looked deeply ingrained foodkumor
They seemed very happy, as if they took lire
easily, though how the editor of three papers can
do that is a mystery. Mrs. Shorter is Radical to a
deeree. With all her dark soft charm she is not
afraid to sit still when the King’s health is toasted,
and everyone else rises. She sits very still, wear-
ing an expression that is half appealing, naif pen-
sive, as if to say : “ I must do this ; don t look at
““She is Irish,” says my companion-“ Pr-
o ” Then I turn to him, and forget the fasci-
nating little poetess at the other end of the table.
He is a big, broad-shouldered man with a g/yish
beard and hair, grey eyes, and the most delightful
voice and manner. He has lived years in 1 aris.
That accounts. He is Richard Whitemg, author
of “No. 5, John-street.” . „ ,
He had a friend in Australia—in Sydney. He
wondered if I knew him. They were together on
a paper in Paris, years ago. The friend went out
to P Sydney for his health. And—yes, I knew him
well And then flashed across a conversation I
had had with that very same friend, years before,
in which Richard Whiteing’s name was often men-
tloltwkspoorMr. Dekker, the editor of Sydney
Evening News. Even when we spoke of him,
there at that gay literary dinner in London, he
ha On a the d o a S'side of Mr. Whiteing sat the
sweetest old lady in the world. I use . old
more in an endearing sense, although her hair was
grey. She was Miss Alice Corkran, editor of the
Girls’Realm, and cousin, by the way, to Mr.
Victor Corkran, Lord Beauchamps secretary.
Anyone more unlike an editor blue, childish
One of the speaker
was a handsome, clean-shaved boy, with a reckless
face and a fine, well-cut head. His dark hair and
carven profile suggested an actor, bo hem
once. His name is Arthur Mason, author of
“The Courtship of Mornce Buckler, etc. Such
an impetuous, dare-devil creature 1 don t know
what lie slid. I was too much taken up with
admiring him, and the delicate yet strong voice of
him.
Mr. Whiteing talked all the time. He occa-
sionally said “Oh, God ! ” softly under his breath
when a man made bad parodies of Swinburne and
Tennyson; or, “Oh, my God! when it was a
comic song. We had great fun m our corner.
Imagine it. Mr. Lusac (Dutch) myself (Aus-
tralian), Mr. Whiteing (English and pro-Enghsh),
Miss Alice Corkran (Irish and anti-war). What a
conglomeration ! Of course, we talked about the
war. Nobody can keep off the subject here.
After dinner there was coffee and talk in other
rooms We all flocked in, and presently I found
myself talking to my adored one with the dark
head—Mrs. Shorter. We had ever so many
things in common. ...
“I thought all Australians were so terribly
Jingo,” said she. Her dark, curly fringe comes
over her eyes.
“Not all,” said I. ' , , . . , „
Next minute Marie Conor Leighton pined us.
She said The Inevitable Thing. Everybody says
it as soon as it’s known you are Australian.
Dozens, scores, hundreds of times I have had it
cast at me —in London, in the country, every-
where—from anybody and everybody: .
“You Australians were so good to us in the
W That is all England thinks of us, I verily believe!
Mrs. Shorter said—“Oh, Mrs. Creed has just
told me all Australians are disloyal.”
Sensation. Mrs. Leighton is as English as Mrs.
Shorter isn’t. She looked at me. I looked at
Mrs. Shorter. Mrs. Shorter looked at the air.
I hastened to explain : “I said all Australians
are not Jingo.” t, ,
“ Why, you were sitting next to a pro-lpei at
dinner,” said Mrs. Shorter to Mrs. Leighton,
“Tins was M. Jules Hedeman, the London cor-
respondent of Le Matin, at present in hot water
with Mr. Chamberlain. ~ ..
“Well, he is the only pro-Boer that I would sit
next to,” said Mrs. Leighton. .
He joined us at that moment, and asked me to
come with him to see Mrs. Kendall in Mrs. W.
K. Clifford’s play, “The Likeness of the Night,
next afternoon. I said “Yes.” •
We went to the play next day. It turn el out
to be an exquisite thing. I can’t understan 1 why
it didn’t have a long run. Mrs. Kendall was
wonderful, almost more life-like than life, as the
woman whose husband didn’t love her. An old
Australian favorite was in the cast, Miss Henrietta
Watson. She wore gorgeous but ugly frocks,
looked thinner, and had no chance to be anything
but pert and smart. I took an Australian with
me as chaperone, and once, when Mrs. K. was
moving everyone with that deep, still realism or
hers, I heard a voice say in a strained whisper,
“ It is a privilege to see such an actress !
I mention that because it’s rare. Every other
Australian I have met in London says How
much better everyone does every thing in
Australia.”
But to return to the dinner. .
No it’s too late; and the rest of it was all
talk.
LOUISE MACK.
The Shifting of Workman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Workman sat like a heavy load on the chest of
Mumble Gumby. He had charge of the school
there, and ruled it with a rod of iron.
The folks of the village had tried many and
often times to get rid of its incubus.
When Dinny Ryan came home with a plentiful
stock of stripes as earnest of his encounter with
Workman, Paddy (Dinny’s father) ventured on
physical remonstrance with the teacher. Paddy
fared worse than Dinny.
When Mrs. Googan, for his “keeping Bridget
in,” tried the efficacy of combined “waddy,”
nails, and language, she was simply bundled out
of the school and incidentally fined “ 10s. and
costs ” for broken windows.
Premeditated and united rebellion by the boys
left Workman once more a victor. He put down
the insurgents with a strong hand and a long
cane.
When they tried keeping the youngsters at
home the utmost rigour of the law (2s. 6d. and
costs for not attending the statutory number of
days) rewarded them. Mumble Gumby was in
despair. If it could only find some flaw in his
character now, surely the department would shift
him ! Or, joy, would “sack” him ! .
But Workman was provokingly regular in his
habits, and strictly minded his own business
unless interfered with. ,
One morning, however, word went round that
Workman was “blind drunk” in Nolans pub.
The town went en masse to gloat over his down-
know it’s tin o’clock?” said Daddy
Ryan, triumph in his eye. _
“Don’ know ’ndon’ care, replied Workman
“Don’t yez me bhoy? Well I’m thinkin its
reportin’ yez we’ll be.”
Paddy and his colleagues then made (iff to pen
the damning epistle that was to shift their incubus.
A week later Paddy was accosted by Workman.
“ Why did you report me ? ” lie said.
“ Shure ter git rid av yez.” „
“Then you’ve gone the wrong way about it, ;
said the “incubus,” throwing a paper in Paddy s
face and storming away.
Paddy laboriously deciphered it.
Memo, to Mr. Workman. ~ _ ,
Public School, Mumble Gumby.
Si r .—Since your explanation of the circumstances re-
ported against you has been deemed unsatisfactory, 1
have to inform you that the Minister for Public Instruc-
tion has decided that you are not sufficiently trustworthy
for the increased responsibilities of the school at Warra
Warra, and has therefore cancelled your recent appoint-
ment to that school. . ,
Having under consideration your previous good
character, the Minister has instructed me to say that no
further action will be taken against you.
You will, therefore, remain in charge of your present
school.
Paddy’s “ Oi’ll be damned ! ” briefly, though
feebly, expresses Mumble Gumby public filing.
Workman, more despotic than ever, is still their
autocrat.

The M ‘II wraith-M ‘Eacharn firm, and
others, have people in the far N.W. of Westralia
preparing for big meat-chilling, canning, and

toria River country, has been short of fresh beef
lately. By the way, Dr. Belgrave, once among
Sydney’s front-rank physicians, but an inveterate
press-scribbler (he helped to found the Telegraph)
and mining speculator, has latterly been Wynd-
ham’fc sawbones.

We Hear of Another Great Cure
in Newcastle.
Mrs. Groves, who lives at Queen’s-road,
Lambton, Newcastle, says :—“ I have much
pleasure in testifying to the virtues of Bile
Beans for Biliousness. My daughter, aged 10
years, has been a sufferer for about seven
years from liver and kidney troubles. When
four years old she was attacked with yellow
jaundice. The doctor gave her one bottle of
medicine, and said it was impossible to treat
so young a child. He advised that she should
be kept on low diet and let the malady take
its course, and it would right itself in time. I
was told to try Bile Beans for her, and since
she has been taking them her skin is much
improved and she is bright and good-tempered
like a new child. She never complains of any-
thing since taking Bile Beans for Biliousness.
She eats and sleeps well, and I am sure I owe
a great deal to your wonderful medicine.”
Bile Beans are the world’s greatest specific for
all liver ailments, comprising Biliousness, In-
digestion, Constipation, Piles, Bad Blood,
Pimples, Headache, Female Weakness, De-
bility, &c. Obtainable at all Chemists; price,
Is. lid per box, or from the Australian depot,
Bile Beans Manufacturing Co., 39 Pitt-street,
Sydney. Remember each box bears the signa-
ture of the proprietors.

Gomes Another Message,
Mr. Graham, of Highgate Hill, Brisbane,
says : —“During the past 10 years I have been
a constant sufferer from biliousness, giddiness,
and headaches, and sometimes through the
biliousness I was unable to move. During
these 10 years of misery I consulted numerous
doctors, who attributed my symptoms to my
habit of smoking strong tobacco, the discon-
tinuation of which gave me no relief. I had
also tried many patent medicines, but ob-
tained no relief whatever, so when a friend
advised me to try Bile Beans I had about
lost faith in all medicines in general; but
when my friend pressed me to give them a
trial, and accompanied his advice by a box of
the Beans, I promised I should do so. After
the first box I felt a decided improvement,
and continued a course of five boxes, by which
time I felt completely cured. My appetite,
which had been very bad before, returned to
an alarming extent. It is now five months
since my cure, and none of the old symptoms
have returned, and I feel as strong and robust
as a horse. I have recommended your Beans
to my friends, and I should like my recom-
mendation to go further, by your making use
of this statement, as I am only too proud to be
known as one of the many who praise Bile Beans
for Biliousness, the working man’s friend.”

Love Loyalty.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Is the loyalty of love but as the loyalty of race ?
Are we each to each just what we are, because of
time and place ?
Are you mine because you are mine? Could
another one be I
If, when you were ripe for loving, that one hap-
pened to be by ?
Would you still be mine in spirit though another
held this hand ?
Would your heart contain a vacant niche you
could not understand ?
Would you sometimes sit and dream of me, whom
you had never met ?
As a memory of happiness which had not hap-
pened yet ?
N.S.W.
MAGIE SULLIVAN.
Our New Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dad yarned to a man passing with horses from
New South Wales, and invited him to dinner. An
interesting man, well-informed, acquainted with
Tyson and Bobby Rand ; knew Queensland and
N.S.W. bush through-and-through, and told Dad
where some good grazing-land was to be selected.
Before leaving, the man sold five horses to Dad
for £5O. Horses were horses then, any old sketch
was worth a £5-note, and Dad went about among
the neighbors boasting of the bargain he made.
Dad always let the neighbors know when he made
a profitable investment; it helped to keep their
hearts up.
A brown mare among the five—by Butler, a
blood horse, the man said —turned out to be worth
more than £5O herself—not to Dad, though.
Dave fed her, and raced her at Pittsworth, and
was promptly “taken-up” on the course as soon
as she won.
“ Stolen from old , on the Barwon ” —the
policeman said—“been watchin’ six munch
forrer.”
The other four were stolen also.
Dad cursed. Said he would never buy another
horse as long as he lived.
Then the neighbors chuckled. They always
liked to remind Dad of any bad bargains he made.
It helped to keep his pride down.
* m * *
Newspapers gave full accounts of the arrest of
Palmer alias Whistler Smith on the Border.
“That’s th’ man,” Dad said ; “that’s th’ damn
scoundrel—red whiskers, strapped trousers, bow-
legged, finger missing—th’ daylight robber.” And
he clenched his fists on the newspaper as though
he held the delinquent in his grasp, and walked
up and down like a caged lion.
* * * *
Constable from Toowoomba waited on Dad
with a handful of legal documents and a cheque
for £l5, to solicit his attendance at Barree Circuit
Court as witness against Whistler Smith. He
explained that the law couldn’t compel Dad to
cross the Border, but if he could see his way to
make the sacrifice he (the law) was certain of a
conviction.
“ Be heaven then,” said Dad, “ I’ve a mind t’.”
He paced about, thinking the matter out. “If
y’ do,” the law observed, “call on the sergeant at
Goondi and he’ll fix you with a fresh horse and
give you directions. An’ I’d advise y’, meself, to
put a revolver in your pocket—it won’t be any
load, an’ y’ might want it. ”
“Pshaw!” Dad blurted out. “Pshaw man !
wot wud Ibe doin’ with firearms. Haven’t I
travelled the country long before you were born ?
an’ see (Dad paused before the constable, and
raising his hands, punched his own left palm hard
with his right fist), see here, an’ though I’m saying
it meself, never yet did I see th’ man (Dad tapped
his palm gently), never yet did I see th’ living
man (Dad raised his right hand above his head) I
was afeared (elevating his voice) to take me shirt
off t’ ” (he pounded his palm hard).
The constable smiled, and said he quite believed
it.
“Well, y’ better let it slide, an’ stay at home,”
Dave said, advisedly—“y’r too old fer that sort o’
thing now.”
Mother and Sarah, who were listening, agreed
with Dave.
“Tut, tut,” Dad said ; “not a bit of it— not a
bit of it.”
Then his thoughts reverted to the £5O he lost,
and an angry, vindictive spirit rose within him.
“ I’ll go ; policeman ! ” he said, in a loud, deci-
sive voice—“ Til go ! ” And when Dad spoke in
that tone persuasion was futile.
* • » *
Mounted on his old brown mare, Dad started
one Friday for Barree, and how anxious mother
became the moment he disappeared from view !
Barree was three hundred miles off on the
New South Wales side and .most of the track and
the country were new and unknown to Dad. But
we were sanguine enough about him. Dad had
always been a wonder and an object lesson to us
in the way of courage and endurance. Floods,
fires, droughts—nothing ever stopped him, and
for anything the bush contained in the shape of
beast or being, he never held a dread.
* * * »
A drought was upon the land. Grass round
Saddletop withered, stock poor, water scarce; but
as Dad travelled on—covering mile upon mile,
plain after plain, ridge after ridge—things got
worse and worse. All was parched, perished ;
nothing but dust and desolation. The mighty
bush was a vast sorrowful was'e —cracked, burnt,
baked. A horror ! it was hell!
Shapeless, blear-eyed, looney bullocks—gro-
tesque caricatures—staggered pathetically by the
way. All day a foul, foetid air filled his nostrils;
hateful crows flocked from carcase to carcase
clamouring in fiendish exultation. And skeletons
—skeletons and bones lay everywhere !
At intervals Dad met pairs of grim, sullen souls
along this endless avenue of odium—mates on the
terrible track—strong, able-bodied men—men with
bright, clear intellects, not loafers, not liars—
British men, Australian men—shouldering their
swags, almost bootless in the blistering sand-
through the sickening, thirst-provoking heat in
search of a job.
* * * *
Dad left Goondi with a fresh horse, a water-
bag, tucker, a head full of directions, and a re-
volver.
The latter the sergeant pressed on him, and
Dad finally took it, saying, “ Perhaps it ’ll be
company,” and he carried it, projecting from his
coat pocket like a cob of corn.
The fourth day, Dad penetrated a dense scrub,
and emerged on the bank of a creek, watered
ms norse, and, throwing the reins on the ground
as with his own old mare, left him standing on the
bank while he filled the water-bag. The brute
made off. Dad tried to catch him, but the old dog
was knowing as a detective. He trotted when
Dad ran and walked when Dad pulled up.
• 1 j n a mess. Determined not to lose
sight of the horse, he followed at its heels, sweat-
mg, swearing, tripping over ruts and sticks—fol-
lowed till it got dark and he could see the fugitive
no longer. °
Weary and hungry Dad rested at the foot of a
gum-tree and thought of home and mother and
us, and called himself a darned fool, and wondered
it, after all, convicting horse-thieves was worth
the candle.
In the morning Dad’s horse was only a few
hundred yards away, standing, its hind leg fast in
the bridle. Joy ! Dad’s heart thumped till he
placed his hands on the brute and was in the
saddle again. He was never so proud of a horse
before; he leaned over and patted it on the neck.
time_ Dad would have tugged its mouth
and belted its ribs with a waddy.
* * * .
A stifling day. The sky a great flaming oven.
i wind blowing. Sandv, wretched waste
land to the right; same to the left. Never a soul
had Dad seen for 40 miles but one solitary horse-
man, and he, at sight of Dad’s revolver, galloped
away.
■"u 6 sun down a ball of fire. A swamp
with water and ducks in it showed itself, then off
the road a public-house, a dusty, tumble-down old
rookery. A couple of saddle-horses outside,
fastened to trees. Four persons lounged on the
verandah. Two more men with beards, strapped
trousers, and spurs. Other two scarcely more than
youths—one a half-caste.
“ G’day,” Dad said.
“’Day.”
And when Dad dismounted every eye there was
on his horse.
Behind the bar,_ hurriedly scrubbing a glass
with a dirty towel in anticipation of trade, stood
a lame, one-eyed old warrior with scars on his head.
Dad called for a beer, then glanced back at the
horse. Dad remembered the sergeant’s warning.
Dad took the beer and drank it at the door.
“Come far?” the publican said, eyeing Dad
closely.
“Two hundred and fifty mile, I dare say,” Dad
answered.
“Queensland ?”
Dad was wondering whether he would answer
or not when a horseman of the flash bush type
reined up at the door. He spoke to those outside,
then called out— “ Riley.”
Riley crept under the counter and limped to the
door.
“Th’ traps passed Bingiloo yesterday with the
Queenslander,” the horseman shouted.
Riley didn’t understand.
“With who ? ” he asked.
“The witness to fix ‘Whistler’; they’refetchin’
him in irons.”
The half-caste sniggered ironically.
Riley looked grave.
Dad stepped out, and, clearing his throat, fixed
the man on horseback with both eyes.
“ It’s a damn lie!” he roared.
Every eye was upon Dad in an instant. For a
moment a dead silence.
Dad squared himself and stood up to it—hasty,
haughty-looking.
“It’s a damn lie ! ” he roared again ; “ f’r I’m
th’ man ; an’ where ’re me irons ? ” He stepped
aside, displaying his big feet for inspection.
, The horseman scowled, but something he read
m a glance from Riley changed his expression.
He dismounted and approached Dad smiling.
“You’re Mr. Rudd, then,” he said, “from the
head of the Condamine ? ”
“I am,” Dad answered, never changing a
muscle. The man said he had been to Dad’s
several times. He spoke well of it, and told Dad
he was a nephew of old Gray’s.
Dad forgot the sergeant’s warning. He seized
Gray’s “ nephew ” by the hand and shook it.
“ Wull, wull,” he said, “ an’ I tooky’ fer a 'orse
stealer ,” and Dad chuckled by way of apology.
Gray’s “nephew” chuckled also.
The publican proposed a drink. Dad drank
and returned the shout. That was seven o’clock.
• * • •
Midnight. The moon shone fitfully and lit up
the belt of cabbage gums; from the swamp came
the trumpet note of wild geese ; owls on noiseless
wing were hunting round; a dim, sickly light
flickered at the pub. Two horsemen rode away
from it through the trees leading a horse with a
saddle on.
From a back room a voice kept calling:
“ Dorgsh ! —robersh !—P-übli’an \—P-übli’an !
wher’sh me—hie—sholver ? ”
’Twas Dad’s.
» ♦ ♦ •
The principal witness for the Crown failed to
attend, and the case against Palmer, alias Smith,
broke do ~ n.
STEELE RUDD.
“ F.R.” on a scientific matter :
I was considerably interested in the announcement
that Professor Loeb had discovered that electricity was
the base of our nerve force, and that he anticipated
being- able to prolong life indefinitely. I understand that
this prof, with the strange name hails from Chicago, and
I think I remember reading about him some few years ago
in a weekly called the Progressive Thinker, published in
the pork city. On that occasion the Thinker, which is
devoted to “ the exposition of advanced thought,” pub-
lished a two-col. account, with scare-heads, of the dis-
covery of the “ microbe of death.” The professor had
found the alleged microbe in the stem of a Japanese
lily, which flowers and dies in a day. Upon removing
the devastating microbe, the lily-bloom retained its fresh-
ness for three weeks, and was as good as ever when the
paper went to press. The professor went further, and
extracted the microbe from several frogs, grasshoppers,
&c., which thereupon immediately lived for ever. The
next time I struck the Thinker— two years later—it had
a lengthy detailed account of a discovery made by a
Chicago professor and an Italian scientist, to the
effect that stones were really living* organisms;
that granite and marble were as much alive as a human
being; and that under “certain conditions,” and with
the aid of “ a powerful lens ” the stones could be plainly
seen moving about. It added that the professors men-
tioned were about to publish a big volume with “ 200
wonderful illustrations” proving their contentions. I
made up my mind to get that book, but I don’t see it
announced yet. The last Thinker which fell into my
hands—dated Dec. last year—had the weirdest yarn of
the lot. It was a 3-col. account, with the usual black
double-column cross-heads, of a man “ whose nerve
centres were crossed,” and who saw with his fingers,
smelled with his ears, felt with his eyes, and tasted with
his nose. It gave names, dates, places, and full par-
ticulars. If the man wore gloves he became blind, and
if his hands got dirty his “sight” was dim. By
holding one hand at his back he could see both ways ; and
poking his finger round a corner he could see down a
street without exposing the rest of himself. The legend
wound up with a circumstantial account of the approach-
ing visit of a “ famous German specialist ” who intended
to try and straighten this entangled person. It must be
understood that these munchausens were not printed as
jokes. The Progressive Drinker is about the most
seriously-written journal that I have ever struck ; and
the man who sent me my copies is an exceptionally
solemn individual. He believed the lot, and cited these
awful tales as a sample of American up-to-date-ness.
